The Red Page
LI
Li Hung-chang, we learn from J. O. P.
Bland’s adequate biography of the Chinese
diplomat and profiteer (“Makers of the Nine-
teenth Century” series; Constable, 65.),
would have made an admirable party politi-
cian ; but unfortunately he was born into an
era and a community when and where party
politics did not exist. “But had the materials
for party government existed in China —that
is to say, a politically semi-conscious elec-
torate, a stock of catch-words for its mysti-
fication, and party funds for its demoralisa-
tion—Li would have made an ideal party-
leader and Prime Minister. He might have
had no constructive policy (except for the
distribution of the loaves and fishes), but he
would assuredly have been a pastmaster in
the game of ‘ins’ and ‘outs.’.. . .For Li Hiing-
chang combined the wait-and-see tempera-
ment with remarkable flair as to the direc-
tion in which the political cat would jump at
any critical emergency. . . .which would have
made him an ideal manipulator of the party
machine and a graceful exponent of public"
opinion after the event.” It was evidently'
an irony of fate that Li had not been born a
white Australian. However, even in China,
he did pretty well. But doubtless the “wily-
old gentleman,” now in the flowery fields, is
suavely smiling at the posthumous honor
conferred upon him by his biographer _in
making him one of “The Makers of the Nine-
teenth Century.”
Li was a product of his antecedents, a
typical mandarin. The outstanding feature
of his genius as a statesman is that he, alone
of liis countrymen, recognised the shattering
force of the impact of the Western Barbar-
ians upon the ancient empire, and realised
the paramount necessity of meeting that
shock by radical modifications of the em-
pire’s system of government. The old walls
were toppling: it was necessary not to prop
them up, but to build newer and stouter
ones. Unlike his • brother mandarins, Li.
though sharing their dislike and distrust of
the outer barbarians, did not share their
contempt. And though Li labored to awaken
his country' to its danger of Japanese and
European encroachments, lie never forgot to
fill his own pockets. However, in China his
countrymen never judged his methods
severely : “On the contrary, the admiration
which his career evoked and the power which
he wielded were largely due to the adroitness
of his methods of amassing and increasing
wealth.” (A curious sense of unreality often
comes over the Australian reader of this
volume : frequently it seems as if J. O. P.
Bland were not discussing China, but another
State: and the yellow-jacketed mandarins
that pass through his pages have curious
features of resemblance to politicians of
another color.)
J*
Li began in the usual way. He passed
third out of 4000 competitors in the exam-
ination for graduates in ancient classical
lore and penmanship. After that there was
nothing remarkable (in China) in the fact
that he rose speedily to the command of the
Imperialistic forces in Kiangsu, and then to
the governorship of that province. He took
all the credit of Gordon’s victories over the
rebels, though he too often forgot to pay the
troops. “His fundamental idea in military
finance was payment by results, with the
proviso that what the troops lost by official
short payments they might make good by the
looting of captured cities —Chinese cities.”
And even at the beginning of his career. Li
“displayed all the qualities of subtle intelli-
gence, complacent ignorance, and unbounded
audacity, which distinguished his subsequent
career in diplomacy.” (Again comes that
haunting suggestion of another land.) The
most important period of Li’s career w T as his
25 years’ Viceroyship of Chihli, when he was
the leading man in the empire, and the
richest, the one Chinese in the continual
contest with Europeans capable of saving
China’s “face.”
Of course, his fellow mandarins—and the
Japanese—knew that, behind the imposing-
looking fleet and the frowning forts, the man-
darin “squeeze” system w-as flourishing and
extending; in other words, the mandarins
knew that Li was one of themselves, “an
unmitigated Chinese official.” In the period
immediately preceding, the Japanese war, his
countrymen admired Li chiefly for “the
ability with which he had discovered and
exploited for the benefit of his family and
friends new fields of profitable ‘squeeze.’ ”
He was “China’s chief word-warrior at the
portals of Peking.” (Now, where have we
heard of another “word-warrior”?) Often in
his career Li fell into disfavor with the
Palace —which meant that the Chief Eunuch
wanted a larger percentage of Li’s profits.
Li wisely paid up, and back came his honors
and titles —paid for.
There is one factor of Chinese govern-
ment which Australia —to her detriment-
lacks. This is the official censorship. (We’ve
got censors, too, but their job is to censor
us, not their bosses.) The Chinese censors
play the game in their own way: unless a
man is down and out, and possesses enough
money to make his impeachment appeal to
his rivals’ covetousness, the censors do not
usually wash dirty mandarin linen in pub-
lic. But occasionally a censor takes the risk
—and it is a risk. In one delightfully frank
impeachment of Li, the censor, after de-
manding Li’s head, adds a postscript: “At
the same time, I would ask you I the
Chinese Emperor]to be so good as to behead
me also, as a fitting punishment for. this
plain speaking.” Not for his denunciation
of Li, but for an uncomplimentary reference
to the Dowager-Empress, this censor was
punished, though his sentence was merely
dismissal and banishment. (It is a curious
thing that none of the Australian censors in
their memorials demand their own execution ;
and it has never occurred to the Chief Censor
to plead for the removal of his own head.)
In 1805 Li fell headlong from his proud
position to depths of derision. But in the
six remaining years of his life he was able to
recover much of his former prestige. In this
refusal to accept any defeat as final lies one
of Li’s'chief claims to greatness.
Li was quick to see his opportunity in the
anti-opium crusade of European and Ameri-
can “benevolent idealists” to stop the impor-
tation into China of Indian-grown opium.
What happened was that Id and his wealthy
friends merely acquired a liighly-lucrative
monopoly in the native-grown poppy ! “His
cynical blindness to the persistent hypocrisy
of the Chinese Government’s attitude in the
matter was after all no worse than that of
the British and American missionaries and
politicians who have persistently chosen to
ignore, not only the obvious purposes of
Chinese official policy, but the unpleasant
fact that English and Japanese enterprise
has widely substituted morphia for opium,
with deplorable results.”
In 1881 Li wrote to the Society for the
Suppression of the Opium Trade an unctu-
ous letter asking for the Society’s support of
“China’s efforts to escape from the thraldom
of opium”; but in 1804 Dr. Morrison found
the poppy growing everywhere throughout
Central China —and the largest growers
of the poppy were the family of Li Hung-'
cluing ! Still, T. O. P. Bland reminds us
that Li was merely acting in strict accord-
ance with the traditions of his class.
Early in his career as diplomat, Li realised
that the Japanese were different. “The de-
vices of evasion and circumlocution which
usually served his purposes in dealing with
Europeans were useless when applied to the
Japanese, themselves pastmasters in the arts
and crafts of Oriental diplomacy.” His
real sentiments were expressed in a letter not
intended for publication ; in it, he said it was
wise policy “to conceal from the Japanese
what we knew concerning their lack of sin-
cerity” ; and added: “There is no human
agency capable of putting a stop to the ex-
pansionist movement of Japan.” And in
dealing with the Japanese Li rarely adopted
the semi-genial, semi-hectoring attitude with
which he was wont to impress European
diplomacy.
To the Shimonoseki negotiations, after the
defeat of the Chinese by the Japanese, Li
went with a card concealed in his wide
sleeve. He knew that Russia meant to de-
prive Japan of the chief fruits of her victory:
he handed over territory with a grim smile,
knowing that Russia would insist upon it
being handed back. Here, J. O. 11.I 1 . Bland
tells us that “for several days the destinies
of Japan hung perilously in the balance.”
The Russian Fleet, backed by French and
German squadrons, was ready to attack the
Japanese. Japan would have been com-
pelled to fight, and, had her fleet been de-
stroyed, her armies on the mainland would
have been at Russia’s mercy. But England
remained a dangerously doubtful factor, so
the crisis was averted. Japan prudently
yielded to the joint demands of Russia,
France and Germany, and agreed to the re-
trocession of South Manchuria. Once again
Li had saved China’s face.
But Li’s life was in danger from his horde
of enemies. It was advisable to place him
out of danger; and he was deputed as Special
Envoy to attend the coronation of Tsar
Nicholas 11. So with his yellow jacket and
his peacock’s feather and his purple rein
restored, Li, with a large suite and his lordly
coffin, set out, in 1896, for the first time in
his life, to visit the world of the outer bar-
barians. Id reality he was in disgrace, and
in danger of political extinction with its in-
variable concomitant, assassination or execu-
tion. It was to give Li a breathing space
to recover “face” that he w r as packed off.
Naturally, the outer barbarians received the
impression that this diplomatic mission was
the crowning honor conferred on China’s
most distinguished statesman. (Haven’t wo
heard of another distinguished statesman who
hopes to leave behind him an unsatisfactory
community, to recover “face” at the other
side of the globe V) But by the time of his
world-tour Li’s prestige among his own
countrymen had collapsed. It had been built
up on the gorgeous sham of his naval and
military preparations. Its collapse involved
the author, who had paid into his private
purse what was meant for his country's de-
fence. The foundation of Li’s military
reputation is neatly expressed by his biogra-
pher : “Brilliant theories about strategy, a
strong dislike for dangerous places, a pretty
talent for despatch-writing, and constant at-
tention to his duties and perquisites as Pay-
master-General.” At the battle of the Yalu,
China’s two ironclads went into action with
three heavy shells between them. “It is
strictly correct to say that the battle, of
Yalu was lost by reason of the peculations
of Li’s son-in-law.” And Li knew all about
that. The same cause was responsible for
the fact that at Wei-hai-wei, out of 104
rounds of shell in one fort, only four were
found to be filled, one with powder and three
with sand. Nor did the mandarins alter
their ways after the exposure in 1894. In
1900 at one arsenal the Allies found and
seized an accumulation of unused war
material, bought not because the army
wanted it, but because the -officials needed the
pickings from these transactions. This sur-
plus stock was valued at over £2,000,000.
(We seem to have heard that amount men-
tioned before.) Li is, however, exonerated by
his own countrymen, since every Chinese
tacitly accepts the fact that “the whole art
of government lies in skilfully-organised
deception, the creation of a vast framework of
illusion, and division of the profits.”
-
In summing up Li Hung-chang, his bril-
liant and genial biographer concludes: “The
ship of China’s State had become water-
logged, her compasses unstable and her crew
unnerved, long before Li was called upon to
take the helm.... But the fierce winds that
burst upon her in the middle of the 19th
century had left her hopelessly unseaworthy.
. . . .The best of pilots with the best of luck
could defer, but could never finally avert, the
day of dissolution : what China needed was
a master-builder, and the task was be-
yond the power of man or super-man.
Li left the ship of State in many
respects better than he found her • for
years, by lavish use of paint and bunting,
he gave her a brave outward show of
seaworthiness; nearly all that her officers
learned of navigation in uncharted seas they
learned from him. More than once he was
compelled by stress of weather to jettison
some of her cargo, both territory and sov-
ereign rights, and on such occasions, no doubt,
he was unduly mindful of his private goods
and ventures. But, when all' is said and
done, he was the best and bravest steersman
in the empire, and for 30 years kept the
ship 4n commission under the Dragon flag.
With his passing from the scene, there was
no strong hand to take the helm. To-day,
the ancient craft lies waterlogged and help-
less. ...”
(And once again comes that vague im-
pression that J. O. P. Bland is writing of
some other State, some other Constitution,
some other democracy.)
Li died with the sincere opinion that, for
all its humiliation of physical weakness, the
civilisation of China constitutes a more
rational and humane science of life than
anything that has been evolved by the outer
barbarians ; and if he had been alive to-day
the xaresent condition of Europe would cer-
tainly confirm his faith in that Celestial
system of moral philosophy “upon which has
stood the test of ages, unconquered and un-
conquerable. a non-militant civilisation more
stable than that of Greece or Rome.”
And in the concluding verse of another
little piece
Sprigs, of wattle blown and quiver, lover mine,
Little winds around thee shiver, heart of mine ;
And i thank the wattles blooming-
And the scented breezes roaming
For the kiss that they must give tliee, till I meet
thee, lover mine.
i>ut Eva M. Manning never qu.te says al
she means—-or doesn't mean enough. Her
range of emotion is small, but within thai
iange she is at home. Many of these brie
verses are trivial; but they'will please the
author s triends.

The most comprehensive and most illumin-
ative description of the Trans-Australian rail-
way, its conception, its construction and its
meaning, appears in that most Australian of
all, Australian magazines, The Inlander
(Gordon and Gotch, Is. per number). Like
other bush travellers this magazine of the
Inside of the Continent, appears as best it
can. The present (February) issue is the
second of Vol. 4; and most of it is devoted to
“Spanning the Continent.” The photo-
graphic illustrations of Unknown Australia
are, as usual, its most characteristic ai.d
valuable feature. *

Teaching of Sex Mygiene, the report of a
conference organised by the Workers’ Educa-
tional Association of N.S.W., held at Sydnev
University in November, 1!)1G, together witii
three public lectures delivered by Prof.
Welsh, Mrs. James Booth and Dr. J. Smyth
is now in its second edition (Govt!
trinter). Every aspect of this most
important subject was fully discussed by
experts at the conference; and the book
reproduces the full text of the papers read
and lectures delivered. But the book unfor-
tunately shows little signs of being a book.
H e can always trust the printer of a Govern-
ment publication to give it the least attrac-
tive and least artistic cover and binding;
bu: the x>ublic that this work hopes to
approach, after it has surmounted the real
obstacle of its forbidding cover, is not to be
reached by this mere Hansard of the pro-
ceedings of a conference. The gist of the
book could be loaded into a volume half the
size and such a book would be read by a
public 10 times as large. It is true that
some attempt at editing has been made by
Meredith Atkinson, who provides a useful
summary of the results of the conference.
Ihe main task before the nation is to “pre-
pare the minds of the young and stiffen their
moia. fibre against the pitfalls that await
those ignorant of their physical functions.”
On the following main issues the confer-
ence was in agreement: That it is necessary
and possible to foster self-restraint from the
tenderest years; that the mere imparting
of physiological fact is utterly inadequate;
that of overwhelming importance is the
spirit in which this knowledge should be
conveyed ; that too much should not be at-
tempted : that very little physiology mixed
with a great deal of commonsense is what
is required ; that the earlier the child be-
comes acquainted with his nature the better;
that the instruction should .be graduated to
the child’s age; that the ideal instructor
would be th e ideal parent; that much is to
be hoped from various schemes of teaching
1 a rents how to teach their children; that,
failing the ideal or instructed parent, the
school-teacher is the most desirable in-
structor of sexual hygiene; that it is doubt-
fnl if the clergyman is fit to undertake this
role, but that it is highly appropriate that
he should discharge a task so consistent with
tlm high aims of his calling; that the doc-
tor s part should be mainly the instruction
oi parents and the general enlightenment of
the community. But no decision can yet
be given on the question of individual versus
class teaching, since the stage of experiment
is still in being.
J*
George M. Mollison (late 6th Battalion)
insists tnat his are Poems. We differ. Most
of these sincere but unpretentious rimes were
put together while in hospital in Egypt,
London and Australia, the result of a severe
wound received at Gallipoli, in 1915. The
best verse of a poor collection is:—
Smile) when the struggle is hairiest,
Smile! when care is nigh,
Smile! when luck’s against you,
Keep smiling till you diel
J*
The Squatters and the Bloivflies, by W. E.
Abbott- and Lewis Carroll (Andrews, Syd-
ney), is a pleasant parodv of “The Walrus
and the Carpenter,” in Through the Loot-
ing Glass. Apparently W. E. Abbott con-
siders the blowfly pest to be due to Govern-
ment inspectors and the poisoning of the
bush birds. The humor will doubtless be
appreciated by any squatter that has escaped
being flyblown.
■J*
Vagabond Verse, by Arthur Wade (Foster,
Melb.)', promises well with its opening
“Poems of Papua.” Papua hasn’t produced
o - fathered many poets; and if Arthur Wade
likes he need fear no competition for the
Papuan Poet-laureatesbip. This concluding-
stanza upon a Papuan storm is agreeably
done:—
A sudden peace;
Stirred by a mighty hand the forest lies
When all the ragings cease;
Stars creep back to the skies,
Life quickens in a thousand timorous hearts
As Nature starts
To make repair;
A world refreshed now breathes a wholesome air.
But his miscellaneous vagabond verse shows
merely a tepid humor and a facility for topi-
cal and alliterative verse.
Australian
Eva M. Manning writes simply and sin-
cerely in her booklet, Wings ( published by
the author, Toowoomba, Q.). She touches
the war, with its distant dead, devoutly, as
in “Pro Patria” :—•
Can you hear me calling you,
Half the world around ?
All the seas lie in between,
And five feet of ground.
Can you hear the spears of rain
Falling on your bed,
Silver arrows of the Lord
Blessing his own dead?
Do you know the myrtles grow
In the sun above,
Reaching with their tender roots
To the dead they love?
High upon a cliff you lie
Guarding English sea.
Gulls are wheeling o’er your grave,
Six feet by three.
Summer heat or winter snow
Pass unnoticed by.
As for me you fought and fell,
You can never die.

The Palace Suit has been created to meet the demand of the well-dressed
man who prides himself upon his personal appearance. It is a smart suit,
faultless as to style and finish, and is made in navy serge—indigo dye—
or in good-quality tweed. The materials are thoroughly shrunk, cut by
skilled tailors, in our own workrooms, and every detail to ensure excellent
finish is provided for. The navy serge is selected because of its pure dye,
and there is a pleasing assortment of tweeds in quiet and effective patterns.
The coat is single-breasted and half-lined with best Italian Cloth, and is
finished with an outside breast pocket. The single-breasted vest has four
pockets, while the trousers can be had with either side or cross pockets.

There is only one real scientific method of

away the uric acid (the poison) right out of

The proper way for this to be done is to

tic. The proper one to take is contained
in De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder Pills.
It has the wonderful property of penetra-
ting every crevice of the kidneys and blad-
der, and wherever it touches it washes away
all sediment and bad secretions, and makes
the kidneys and bladder perfectly healthy.
Any sufferer can try this experiment for him-
self by getting a box of De Witt’s Kidney
and Bladder Pills at his chemist’s—all chem-
ists sell them—and you will, within a very

is true. The effect of this experiment is most
remarkable.
The quick relief which these wonderful
little pills bring is, so our readers say, noth-

to rheumatism, gout, gravel, backache, tired
feeling, dropsy, Bright’s disease, constipa-
tion, cloudy urine, inflammation of the blad-
der, or other symptoms of urinary trouble.
To cure rheumatism or any other form of
kidney trouble you must positively get rid of
the cause—the poisonous uric acid. Re-

When you see the urine change to a muddy,

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Snow Peaks.
When frosty stars in the morning pale
And the light is grey in the larches,
The snow-peaks loom in their gleaming mail,
As lords of the lonely marches;
The light grows white on their vizored
crests
When Night still moves in the meadows;
On mantled shoulders the red dawn rests,
When the lakesides dream in the shadows.
The bright noon burns on the grey moraine
And shines on the steep snow-faces;
A million light-points gleam again
Where the loosened ice-fall races.
But the snow-peaks tower, like gods who
stand,
All-wise in this old earth’s story,
With the light of a world in each upraised
hand
And bent brows lit with glory.
The evening comes to the valley soon ;
The lakes, that are jewels splendid,
With singing river and long lagoon,
In grey, warm mists are blended.
But the snow-peaks shimmer, like spires of
gold,
In a city far and holy,
Till every star of the Night’s unrolled,
And the moon wheels westward slowly.
If we might do as the chosen do,
And stand in those lofty places,
And touch the clouds where they dim the
blue,
And feel God’s hand on our faces,
Would there be aught we could yearn for
then,
’Neath the heavens’ wide-reaching
arches?....
Yes, I would long for your lips again
And the evening breeze in the larches!
MOUNT COOK, M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
The Bridge Builder.
My love has built a bridge for me. Oh ! high
and wide and strong,
From Fairyland to Sydneytown he’s thrown
a bridge of song.
And trippingly and daintily as summer rain-
drops beat,
I hear the tinkling patter of the fairy
travellers’ feet.
Pixy, elf and merry sprite, gnome and danc-
ing fay,
Over the bridge of song they come to laugh
my tears away.
My love has built it fair and true of golden
word and phrase,
With airy arch of stately thought to span
the nights and days.
And kings and queens of Fairyland and gal-
lant prince and knight,
When dream-shine gilds the parapet, come
flashing through the light.
Holding high their banners bright, trolling a
merry stave,
Marching over the bridge they come to bid
my soul be brave.
My love has made it beautiful with fretted
fancies fine,
With arabesque of ancient dream and curve
of rhythmic line.
And soothingly and healingly as summer
raindrops’ beat,
Adown the bridge from Fairyland comes
Love on rosy feet.
Softly crooning an elfin song, wuth wonder-
misted eyes,
Gaily over the bridge he comes to make me
la ugh ter-wise.
N.S.W.
MARGARET FANE.
The August Child.
You were a child of August;
With the heralds of Spring you came
When she polished her summer armor
Till it shone in a shield of flame,
Ere she challenged the door of winter
And would not be denied,
With the fans of her flower petals,
With the flow of her blossom tide.
You were shaped for a child of August
In the song of the singing leaves,
To the rise of sap in the forest,
By the nest-cones in the eaves.
You came on a wild-rose budding,
You came on a brown lark’s wing,
For you were the’ child of August
On the trail of the feet of Spring.
And still in the heart of August,
With her dreams and her golden sky,
There is always the touch of winter
That was left from a bleak July!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Grace.
In an old passion-fruited place,
Dark-shaded by dark leaves of grace,
There is a light, a sign—her face;
Grace of her soul and heart and mind,
Grace of her royal hands and kind—
All grace, full late, full sweet to find,
As a late bloomed rose, Mother mine!
Dark grape crying the grace of wine,
There is her face, a- light, a sign.
Lo! It is she, Mother o’ me !
Look down, look down to me! For she,
On an old heaven flowered lea,
Hath bowed in pride her royal head,
Softly, “Lo ! it is he” hath said.
Mother mine, the long nnin is dead !
N.S.W.
HILARY LOFTING.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.

able, acknowledge in the “•Correspondence’ > column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal. Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or

not exceeding tiro columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending' MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
lesponsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 21, 1918.
MISSING AT LLOYD’S.
If the present disturbance ends as the Hun
hopes it will, there are likely to be quite a
lot of nations posted as Missing at Lloyd’s.
The war, in this respect, is different from
any other armed clamor among white races
for many centuries. Germany plans to ex-
pand by adding territory on which
millions and millions—very many millions —
of new Germans may grow up. Now, more
Germans can always be found by reason of
a liberal birthrate, if there are desirable
and convenient and commodious places to
put them. So while the Fritz country wants
more Germans it wants a great deal fewer
other people—the kind who are sufficiently
virile to rally for a new argument in years
to come and interrupt the spread of Kultur.
Even if the firm of “Me and Gott” wins for
the time being, great leaders and organisers
might arise to turn the tables on “Me” and
to annex “Gott” as an allied deity—pro-
vided there were sufficient men to follow*
them and means to support these men. Hence
the Hun view that the fewer white men who
aren’t Huns the better. They eat food and
breathe air that the Hun should have; they
occupy space which is the natural heritage
of the baby Hun ; they possess all kinds of
wealth, which the Hun would find handy.
And if their existence keeps the Hun awake
at night they will further interfere with his
plans for expansion. No Hun who has
had a bad night can be a completely
energetic ruffian next day. “Never maltreat
your enemy by halves” said Frederick the
Great, and the Prussian has pasted that in
his hat.
* * * *
If sufficient people can be killed off in the
countries on which the Hun lays his hands,
then there will be room for Hun colonisation.
If all the people can be killed off, there will
be heaps of room for Hun colonisation. Even
Belgium, which is, bar Saxony, the most
densely-populated land in Europe,- would be
an empty place, just like Australia when the
British came to it, if the Belgium nation was
obliterated. And it would have advantages
over Australia. Saying nothing of the
plunder. Belgium has the attraction of being
absolutely alongside. When the old people
were cleaned out and the new savages had
come in, it would be a solid part of Germany
sending its members to the Federal Legisla-
ture, wearing the spiked helmet as one to the
manner born because it would be its own
helmet, and saying “Hoch” with a good grace
because it had said "Iloch” from infancy. It
would be a source of strength instead of being
a land of irreconcilables which had to be held
down at great expense of men and anxiety.
There would be house-room as well as land-
room ready for the new population when the
Belgian was sufficiently dead. It would be
a case of finding a new country without the
discomforts of pioneering. The same logic
applies to northern France if the Prussian
Hun can keep it, and to Serbia if the other
Hun can keep it, and to any other places
that either of them can get and keep. The
process of clearing the land for settlement
has gone farthest in Serbia, and. unless the
process is stopped one little people will pre-
sently •be Missing at Lloyd’s. During
the actual conquest the slaughter was
just as extensive as there was time to
make it. Even since the occupation was
effected a more leisurely process of destruc-
tion has been going on. A great number of
the able-bodied males have died in slavery
through over-work and ill-usage and short
rations, and the rest of the slaves will die by
and bye, after earning much money for their
masters by digging in the mines or cultivat-
ing the land. More slaves are being requisn
tioned as they can be caught. An honest
copper has been turned by the sale of desir-
able girls to the Turkish harems. A social
difficulty has been got over by sending other
girls and women to the army for the use of
the Austrian soldiers. And the rest is a
record of dull butchery with or without frills.
The chief frill consists in making the victims
dig their own graves and then lie down in
them to be bayoneted. Also there are frills
which are worse.
*** . *
The Austrian soldier may. not have got
down to this level of brutality all at once.
But he is a military machine with orders to
clear the land for settlement. Possibly it
took the machine some time to get used to
being a butcher, a little longer to become
reconciled to it, and a little longer still to
learn to enjoy the work and to decorate it
with trimmings which were not actually on
the bill. But it isn’t worth while to inquire
into mental attitudes. The experience of
Belgium hasn’t been quite so bad up to date
as that of Serbia, because it is less isolated.
Still, a requisition was recently made there
and in northern France for 10,000 more
male and female slaves to do hard labor in
Germany, and 1000 more girls, presumably
for a purpose that need not be specified. And
these, requisitions are periodical. They will
certainly go on till the war is finished. They
will almost as certainly go on after the war
is finished if the Hun and the Gott that
ho has manufactured come out on top.
It can hardly be otherwise. The Hun has
learned that he is no great success as a colo-
niser in the ordinary sense of the term—-the
Canadian and Australian and Maofiland
sense. He has also discovered that he is a
rank failure at the work of assimilating an
alien race and making it a source of strength.
In that respect he seems to grow worse in-
stead of better. If by much annexation he
brings it to pass that there are more enemies
than friends inside his gates, then there will
be a large and permanent worm at the root
of his tree. The only way to make a united
Germany is by getting rid of these enemies,
and. for years past he has made no secret of
his intentions. The order of a German com-
mander at the beginning of the war to kill
all wounded and prisoners and “leave no
living man behind us” was only one of many
orders all smelling of the shambles. But it
isn’t so widely recognised as it might be that'
slavery is intended to be a steady institution
after the, war. Several Hun writers have
outlined the future of Australia, which is the
example that most concerns us. The men are
to work for their German masters under
careful German supervision. They are to be
paid with, food, clothes and lodging—the
lodging being in barracks or compounds
guarded by soldiers or armed police. They
will own no property; men who live in a
sort of perpetual prison with hard labor have
no use for property. They will not be allowed
to marry, for it is no part of the programme
to perpetuate a hostile race. Some of the
women will be allowed to marry Germans;
the rest will remain single. In this way the
carefully devised Hun slavery system will
differ from that in old Virginia and the Cot-
ton States. Sambo’s owner had no ulterior
political motive. He wanted his black
brudder to increase and multiply for the
owner’s profit, and he hated to see Sambo
die except from old age. The slave whose
death is considered desirable, and who is
merely making a little money for his proprie-
tor as a temporary sideline, is in a new cate-
gory Certain Hun writers consider that this
winding up of our race will be an easy
matter when the affairs of Europe are dis-
posed of. Australians, they say, are a silly,
credulous people ivho can always be kept
busy with industrial quarrels through the
hidden devices of Hun agents. Considering
the strange, hypnotised fashion in which the
workers follow the lead of I.W.W. propa-
gandists, many of whom may very well be
the Hun agents alluded to —considering the
practical stoppage of Recruiting, the unac-
countable breakdown of business at the
Federal arms factory, and the way in which
a shapeless Something coming from Nowhere
always gets in the road when an attempt is
made, or alleged-to be made, to build even a
single ship to carry food to England—it
assuredly.looks as if the Huns who talked in
fashion were not discoursing through
their hats. Last year the N. S. Wales coal-
miners, who seem to lend the most copious
ear of all to the I.W.W. person, and who are
more useful than, any other class in the com-
munity for holding up industry, had 182
strikes (in fact, they spent more than a
quarter of their time on strike),-and yet no
big grievance was adduced in explanation.
* * * *
The war isn’t ended yet, and Australia has
still a fine chance to be among the peoples
who are posted as missing.
ARE THE SENTINELS BLIND OR
ARE THEY DRUGGED?
The Federal Parliament is to meet soon
after next week’s bun has been undigested.
There is an official fiction that the meeting
will be for the “despatch of business” ; but
the circumstantial yarn in the newspapers is
to the effect that the session will be a more
or less barren proceeding incidental to
authorising the expenditure of large masses
of money and hurriedly shutting up while
Hughes and Cook despatch themselves
across the Equator. As for the past five
years the Federal Parliament has been sys-
tematically loafing on the job, The Bulletin
puts forward for serious consideration the
theory that the time is ripe for doing some-
thing, and that for this Parliament to pro-
ceed to further achievements in shirk and
holiday-making would, in the circumstances,
be a gross offence against public decency.
The old stunts and stalls are played out.
People who were sickened by the dodges and
evasions of a Labor majority feel no less dis-
gust at contemplating similar performances
by the other crowd. .Most of them are stead-
ily advancing to the conclusion that from
Ministries of any sort under the existing
system there is nothing better to be expected
than exhibitions of twist; but a fairly sub-
stantial number are still waiting to find out
whether the Commonwealth Parliament is
capable of shaking itself free of the demoral-
ising lethargy that has fallen upon it and
insisting upon its obligations to the nation
being discharged. This Parliament owes a
solemn duty to Australia which is not even
touched by blithering about duty to the
Empire and other irrelevant topics. The
Commonwealth is up to the neck in a war of
unexampled magnitude, including in its
hazards the very life of the nation. Arising
out of this war are questions whose import-
ance it is impossible to exaggerate. They
are not questions in any ordinary political
sense—they are vital issues. Parliament
knows this. The people know it. Parlia-
ment knows, too, that these issues are being
clowned with, and that the Commonwealth
is rapidly sliding into the position of having
neither government nor. purpose. What
should it matter if a couple of party touts
want to go to. London? What reason is
there in such a circumstance for letting every
other consideration slide?
Apart from Hughes and Cook' and their
“mission” to the other side of the world,
there are half a dozen subjects to which
Parliament should turn with resolute inten-
tion to clear up the 'prevailing muddle and
get down to the bedrock of policy and ad-
ministratidn. They are these
1. The War.
2. The Defence Department.
3. Recruiting.
4. Finance.
5. Repatriation.
6. The Censorship.
Every informed citizen of Australia knows
that the position in regard to any and all of
these matters is a mixture of farce and
tragedy as dangerous to the Commonwealth
as strychnine to an infant. Never since this
war began has the Australian Parliament
had anything nearing remote resemblahce to
an explanation of the war’s progress, the
military and naval position of the Allies, the
policies relied upon to resist defeat or the
effect of various happenings. If Lloyd
George or someone makes a speech Australia
is provided by Private Enterprise with sun-
dry bits thereof. The comments of various
“experts” are provided also. But from the
Australian Government Australia hears
nothing. For all practical purposes t'he
Commonwealth is carrying on a war with its
head in a bucket, without any official know-
ledge of the proceedings, or of what countries
on the same Side of the fence are doing. The
fortunes of war. the handling of the war,
should be as earnestly explained and dis-
cussed by the Australian Parliament as by .the
Parliament of Britain or France. The case
against that, if there is one, rests upon the
supposition that Australia is a mere joint in
the tail of another country, without individu-
ality, rights or responsibilities. And if
Parliament has a right to claim first-hand
knowledge and information about the war. it
has more right to insist that not another
week shall go by with a Defence Department
in the condition of the calamity controlled by
the poor limpet Pearce. For members of the
Legislature to rant about the “perils of Aus-
tralia,” “the need for reinforcements” and all
the rest of it and yet give sanction to the
ghastly muddling and criminal ineptitude of
this Department would be far more easily
reconcilable with treason than loyalty. In a
very large measure this matter will be the
test of whether the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment possesses even a fragment of moral
right to existence. The evil example and
blighting influence of this Department have
spread far and wide throughout the country.
It has wasted the public estate. It lias
chilled all military effort and enthusiasm. It
is destroying our system of Home Defence.
Is Parliament going to fold its hands, bow
before the Party joss and let the slide to the
Devil continue?
The same considerations apply to Finance,
Repatriation. Recruiting and the Censorship.
The last of these matters The Bulletin
passes on with the comment that what has
ftor.n done and is still being done comes
within the category of the unbelievable. So
far as Finance is concerned it is well within
the region of truth to say that things are
mixed. At a time when war is imposing
upon the nation a bill of almost incredible
magnitude, Waste and Bloat stalk arm and
arm through the land. There are loans for
war and loans for peace. The people are
being taxed to meet losses in battle, losses
on railways and losses on fish-shops. Issues
of p.n.’s darken the sky. and from seven
cashboxes the public credit is pouring like
so much water, and seven taxing authorities
are holding out insistent hands for more.
All this may be part of a policy of winning
wars.. It may also be part of a policy of
hanging on the tail of a Gadarene swine.
The main thing is to discover whether the
Legislature realises where it is going and
whether it considers itself under an obliga-
tion to permit the country to be bled white.
Most of the current talk about “our wealth”
is sheer piffle. The essential point about
the matter is the manner in which such
unpawned “wealth” as w T e have is being used
and how far the combined efforts of the
Commonwealth and the States are assisting
the country to ruin. The Federal Parlia-
ment has the most ample powers to bring
the current saturnalia of public extravagance
to an end. If it does not do so, if it con-
tinues to cower away in a funk hole, drop-
ping from nerveless hands the trust it holds,
what are the people of the Commonwealth
to look forward to? It is no use maundering
about the ignorance and the apathy of the
public and groaning over the average citizen’s
; 'waste” of half a crown in taking his family
to tlie pictures. So long as Parliament is
lazy and wasteful, these other considera-
tions are hardly worth thinking about. And
so long as Parliament sneaks away from its
duty, abandons its right of control and by
raising wild party shouts tries to camouflage
its shirk, it will be entitled to claim about
as much loyalty to Australia as an intern-
ment camp.
MOTHERHOOD.
Dear Bulletin— -Don’t you think it about
time the woman was allowed to present her
side of the case when parsons and professors
and such-like people of importance are bully-
ing her about the birth-rate? Hardly a day
passes but a howl goes up from someone or
other about the wickedness of these women
who won’t have babies as profusely as they
used to. Now it’s Professor Berry telling
the Victorian Methodist Conference a tale so
dire he blanches its collective cheek with
horror. (I’ll have more to say about its
collective cheek later on.)
There were 100,000 fewer babies born in Great
Britain in 1915 than in 1914.... The limitation of
families was deliberate... .The matter was so serious
that on present birth-rate figures they could calculate
the year in which the British Empire would be
extinct.
And much more to the same effect. Then the
Conference 'unanimously passed a motion —
“That the Commonwealth and State (iovern-
ments be urged to take the question into their
immediate and earnest consideration, and to
bring in such legislation as may effectually
restrict the manufacture and sale of preven-
tives and abortifacients.”
Professor Berry is pained and astonished
at the British birth-rate for 1915. “One
fully expected,” he says, “that it would have
been much higher on account of the war.”
An extraordinary attitude for an M.D. or any
other man in full possession of his faculties.
Hundreds of thousands of potential fathers
had gone from the country and hundreds of
thousands more were to follow. The women
were left, tormented by fear and anxiety for
the men in the firing-line or on their way
there. They were, moreover, appalled by the
fate of an invaded country only a few score
miles away, arid went about in constant
dread of a similar inroad on their own
coasts. What more natural, then, in such
circumstances, that many women should wish
to avoid the further anxieties and perils of
pregnancy? Yet Professor Berry considers
it very remiss of them to have had 100,000
fewer babies than usual. The wonder
is that there could be found so many
women willing to take the risk! Does
the Professor think that children born
of such mothers, many of them probably ner-
vous wrecks throughout the greater part of
the period of pregnancy, are such valuable
assets to the race that these women should
have had maternity forced upon them willy-
nilly? There was a still further decline of
20,000 in the births for 1916, and the 1917
and 1918 figures will surely show an even
greater diminution. The possibility of famine
is not an inducement to parenthood.
There seems to be an almost girlish sim-
plicity about a Methodist Conference. Prof.
Berry said: “It is now almost universally
admitted by scientists that the limitation of
families is deliberate.” If this was news to
that guileless body he should have added a
rider that Queen Anne was quite indispu-
tably dead. And, anyhow, what does this
deliberate limitation of families mean except
that people don’t want to have so many
children, and if they don’t want tp have
children why should they? Is there any
other sound reason in the whole wide world
for having children at all except that one
wants them ?
Is it for the good of possible children to
force parenthood on people against their will,
as the Methodist Conference appears to wish
it could? If people are too selfish to become
parents, aren't they going to be the very
worst of parents a child could have? If they
are too poor, why should they bring into
the world a child to whom they cannot give
a fair chance? And if they are degenerate
stock, parenthood for them is nothing less
than a crime. Surely if the business of
child-bearing is left to those who really want
to undertake it, it will be, on the whole, more
satisfactorily performed than if it is forced
on those who emphatically do not want or
are unfit for it. -Are not children whose
parents have wanted them, and have given
them a real affection and proper care when
they got them—-are not such children much
more likely to become good parents_ in due
course than are the unwanted, and is there
not here a key to the solution of the problem ■
“It seems almost incredible,” says Foret.
who is at least as competent an authority as
J. IT. Berry, “that in some countries medical
men who are not ashamed to throw young
men into the arms of prostitutes blush when
mention is made of ante-conceptional
measures.” He gives examples showing the
results of the unrestricted fertility of the
degenerates that should make even a Metho-
dist Conference sit up and think. Thisfaimh
tree, traced back to 1740 from 1815. is par-
ticularly instructive. A woman of that time,
a drunkard and degenerate with an heredi-
tary taint, left 834 descendants. The live®
of some of these have been officially recorded
as follows: 106 were illegitimate, 142 were
mendicants, 64 were incapable of supporting
themselves. 181 of the women were prosti-
tutes, seven were convicted _of murder am
69 of other crimes. All within a
years, at a cost to the State of £250,000; an
this country would have paid £5 a time toi
such people, and does so cheerfully every da.'
in the week ! c
Mvself I am a healthy, normal woman
26. I fully intended at the time of m,'


marriage to have any number of children. I
bore my .first son shortly after the outbreak
of the war, and presently saw that the slogan
was going to be raised “Back to the Breed-
ing Pen” for women, and I therefore post-
poned the balance of my family for the
period of the war. I will not breed children
to order, and no resolutions of a Methodist
Conference—no, nor regulations under the
War Precautions Act, either —will make me
do so. If I cannot do the job my own way
and under conditions the most favorable for
my offspring, I will not do it at all. Even
dumb animals have that much sense.
Even if it were possible to abolish preven-
tion the population would not increase, as
Professor Berry seems to think. The imme-
diate and inevitable effect would be to make
abortion infinitely more prevalent and popu-
lar than it is to-day. Already I find a well-
known preventive device labelled “For the
prevention of disease only.” This means
that a man who vipits a prostitute, though he
may have a wife of his own, may use this as
a safeguard against disease, while a married
woman is forbidden to use it as a safeguard
against pregnancy. It entitles the man to
indulge a completely illegitimate sexual
appetite with impunity, while it forbids to
the woman the satisfaction of an uiulispu-
tably legitimate one. This is almost as
degrading to the mothers of the race as the
baby bonus under the present plan of dis-
tribution. Could anything be more idiotic
than this system which will give £5 for any-
thing as long as it remotely resembles a
baby? It may be rickety, deformed, imbecile,
blinded by gonorrhoea, rotten with congenital-
syphilis ; but a paternal State hands out the
fiver without demur as long as the thing lives
long enough to get it. Do you wonder we
decline to bring any more children into the
world to be swamped by the spawn of degen-
eracy like this? It’s better babies this coun-
try wants, very much better babies, not
merely more babies.—Yours, etc.,
LYSISTRATA.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Australia v. America.
The cry of “-Wilson Hill” :
After four years’ hard sledding in America I find
Australia a happy picnic-ground, little changed
from early 1914, except for the prevalence of soldiers
and an increased political parasitism. This is not
i cry of Stinking Fish. My exile, like that of our
ooys abroad, grew in my heart a passionate devotion
to Australia. Her worth and great possibilities
stand out in bold relief, accentuated by knowledge
gained in America. Yet in one day here I have
seen more drunks, or drink-affected people, than
during all my absence. Horseracing continues to
be a greater topic than the war or the fate of our
country. We are out of focus with the world’s
movements and passions; isolated, narrow and self-
satisfied.
Australia to-day appears to be a nation living
on the fighting reputation of her finest sons, paid
for with their agony and sacrifice. The willingness
to suffer, to sink self-interest, to sacrifice, to strive
to gain through co-operative endeavor and combined
strength, as displayed by our soldiers, is markedly
absent from the actions of those who stay behind.
Australia is not “giving till it hurts,” either to
win the war or to make herself more self-supporting
and a greater part of the Empire.
State v. State; Party v. Party; Capital v. Labor,
or vice versa; Union v. Non-union; Government v.
Industry—it is all one damnable confusion of throttle,
waste and inefficiency, while our men and Allies die
and German influence gains a stronger footing.
In September, 1917, Hoover shipped France 200,000
tons of sugar taken from America’s normal con-
sumption. The writer was one of millions who volun-
tarily gave up using sugar. Candy-makers were
cut down 60 per cent, on supplies; hotels were lim-
ited to 10 days’ supply and householders to a dollar’s
worth once a week. The tariff was replaced on
sugar, and refineries sprung back into vigorous action,
refining the beets from millions of acres which paid
the grower to farm. Tuesday was declared a meat-
less day; Thursday a white-breadless day. The fry-
ing-pan was abolished and people were taught how
to stew or pot-roast steaks and chops to conserve fat.
Men from various great business organisations were
called to Washington to run the departments in
the War machine; they work for nothing or a dollar
a year—their previous employers pay their usual
salary to them. Here we have politicians and
“eushies”; there brains and efficiency. Elwood
Mead, formerly directing Victoria’s irrigation policy,
is out on the many unsettled irrigation areas of U.S.
with capable assistants, placing men on big. areas
to grow wheat, with grain and implements supplied
by the Government, who guarantee 9s. 4d. per bushel.
Others are growing the castor-bean—loo,ooo acres—
to get the oil for America’s big Aviation Army
engines. Here we talk and feed the wheat to mice
and weevils, whereas if fed to pigs we could supply
the English order of 2000 tons of bacon monthly.
America has standardised ship plans, and to any
men who can guarantee a slip and labor, the Govern-
ment gives an order to build, attends to supply of
necessary material, pays cost plus 10 per cent., and
says “go to it.” Cost does not matter; they want
ships. Riveters make up to £2O per week; ship
carpenters 295. 6d. per day of eight hours; mechanics
255. to 295. 6d. per day. The lowest wages are 18s.
6d. per day. On the San Francisco bay shores,
the Columbia River, Seattle and Vancouver, can be
seen boats galore being constructed without any
buildings or “shipyards” worth writing about.
Naturally the high wages offered attract the men, and
ships are the result. The Government now is con-
scripting the further supplies of men necessary.
Prohibition being nearly general, workers are able
to put excess earnings into War Loans, Red Cross and
Y.M.C.A. Here we talk of ships.
To progress properly we need more leaders—men
to whom Australia’s advance means more than power,
party and tainted money; leaders in every line of
industry that we have or should have. We lack
them now. After our children leave school their
education is haphazard and mostly unsatisfactory.
People who can afford to do it send their sons to the
Universities to follow some “profession.” Agriculture
and industry are starved. The best-paid positions go
to imported men, not because our men are less intel-
ligent, but because they have not the broader
experience and close acquaintance with modern
methods that the importee has. If the Australian
went abroad and studied these methods, he would be
figuring them out from the point of suitability to
his home conditions; he would not have to consider
himself infallible like the import has to in order
to hold inh • alon ha would tret alonz better with
his own people than the import can, as he under-
stands them.
Competition is stagnant here under wages boards;
the good artisan and the poor one get the same pay.
In America a dozen others are hunting the dollar
hot-footed, and competence alone holds the job. If
a couple of thousand of our young men were yearly
pushed abroad, to England or America, started in
whatever branch of work they desired, offered big
salaries to return home when they were proficient
and take responsible positions in our industries or
in schools where they could impart their knowledge
to others, there would be a tremendous development
in all business here. Japan is doing this—has been
doing it for years.
Then we, too, can foster growing industries with
sufficient tariff; assist in the general organisation
of production and marketing, that we may replace
imports with the home product, and greatly increase
our foreign trade, making places and employment
for the 15 million people that Australia must have to
make her “safe for Democracy.”
Our Legacy of Cheerful Idiocy.
The party ass broke away from his moorings
in Victoria Iffst week and before an unof-
fending public knew what had happened the
brute was careering all over the State, kick-
ing the local institutions to pieces and pub-
licly wrecking the cherished delusions of the
Argus. To The Bulletin the incident
seemed like the fulfilment of a divine,
prophecy. Nothing more bizarre could very
easily be imagined than this give-away of the
party fraud, nor anything more appropriate to
the moment. For a week or so there had been
wrangle in the Assembly about whether the
Government should keep a promise given by
Peacock to vote £200,000 to the railway em-
ployees or whether £llO.OOO met the case
and was appropriate to the state of the
purse. On Wednesday when the Assembly
met 45 members were present out of a total
of 65. Labor-leader Elmslie’s amendment
moved on the preceding day to “reduce the
estimates by £10,” so as to give the Assem-
bly a chance of expressing its views about
the Ministry, came up for discussion. Nobody
spoke— Elmslie’s side because they noticed
the gaps in the enemy’s trenches, and Min-
isters because they imagined their reserves of
man-power were in the refreshment-room. So
a snatch division was taken, and as Peacock;
and his little coterie threw in with Elmslie
and the refreshment-room was barren of
Bowserites, the Ministry was defeated by one
vote Under any system bearing resem-
blance to sanity this division would have been
taken to mean what it said. But according
to the rules of the game on which Australia
depends for getting on with the job, it was
interpreted to mean “the defeat of the Min-
istry,” the arrival of a crisis, the deliberate
throwing of everything up into the air, the
necessity for resignation, dissolution, howls
and yells in the constituencies and a general
election. Four months ago Victoria struggled
with an election, in the course of which it
fired Peacock out and handed Bowser a
majority. _ Now because in the absence of
nearly a third of the members there happened
at a given moment to be one more member
ready to say “Yes” -than were prepared- to
shout “No,” the effort to elect a Parliament
and all the cost incidental thereto is alleged to
go for nothing. The thing is almost incredible ;
but there it is, and on the whole The Bulle-
tin rejoices. The dullest-witted citizen must
surely see what a mockery of the theory of
government by Parliament is the practice
to which Australia has been fat-headed
enough to become addicted. No question is
determined on its merits under this system
nor can it be. The Legislature is the slave
of a fetich, and by it the people are tied hand
and foot. There may be nothing much to
admire about a Bow-ow Ministry or one
that bolts away from blank cartridges, but
that has nothing to do with the matter. The
tragedy rests in the circumstance of the
people sending 65 representatives into Par-
liament and discovering that, unlike any
other body of men, they cannot do what they
were appointed to do. It is out of this that
the Australian democracy is trying to con-
struct its future. It might as well try to
fish Sennacherib’s army out of the Red Sea
with a frozen sausage.
Why Not Open All the Doors?
It is comforting to know that after Chief
Justice Griffith has toiled at his Commis-
sion there is going to be a distribution of
cards, and multitudinous persons will carry
them to the post and back again and make
schedules of the answers and have every-
thing filed away in pigeon-holes pending
developments in this crisis of our national
history. But this is all going to take time,
and what follows will take more time, and
additional time will be consumed meeting the
new situation. Therefore it is a wise precau-
tion to have some sort of recruiting scheme
while the months are disappearing. So far
as The Bulletin can make out, recruiting
at present is only another word for chaos,
with the doors slammed in the face of pos-
sible candidates. If a citizen yearns to join
the mounted forces he is not now wanted
unless he abandons the yearning and joins
up to go into whatever section of the service
he may be dumped by an official. If he
hankers after an infantryman’s, kit he cannot
be a soldier unless he ceases to hanker and
takes the chance of becoming a stretcher-
bearer. If a thousand men rushed forward
to-morrow morning to announce that they
wanted to join the artillery they would dis-
cover that there was nothing doing—that
they must enlist first and hear about what
they are going to do later on. There may be
good reason for this. Also, there may only
be a fool of a reason. In. the opinion of this
paper, the system is ridiculous to the point
of madness. The very essence of voluntary-
ism is that it allows the widest choice to the
individual. It is only stupidity that would
try to hamper this or discount the force of
that personal appeal which can be made by
individuals who are prepared to say “Come”
instead of “Go.” Not far back a “Sports-
man’s Unit” was raised. Before that “the
Carmichael Thousand” had been made a
reality. It may not be the ideal way of
raising men to promise to keep them together
or issue invitations to join particular
branches of the service. But men can be
obtained that way who would not volunteer
under other conditions —and it is men that
count. Pending the judgment of Griffith,
are any men wanted for the mounted forces,
, for artillery, for infantry, for the medical
corps, and so on ? If they are, what can be
the objection to saying so and taking men
for the special service that appeals to them?
If men are not wanted for one or other of
these particular branches, it is obviously
absurd to keep the matter secret, since the
citizen who yearned to be on a horse might
then, in the light of a more complete under-
standing, contract a yearning to carry
stretchers or a bayonet.
«
The Boiling of Fitzpatrick.
Fitzpatrick, who is Assistant-Treasurer of
N. S. Wales, has made a statement which
should live in history and reverberate like
the clatter of a patient animal’s hoofs down
the corridors of Time. He said it “made his
blood boil” to hear farmers complain about
the low price they receive for wheat. These
wretches growl that they have so far re-
ceived 3s. lid. per bushel for the 1915-1916
crop, and 3s. per bushel for the two suc-
ceeding crops; that this is subject to heavy
deductions for alleged defects, and the boss
tyrants in charge deduct at their own sweet
will, and without appeal to any Arbitration
Court; that the price which they get from
the Government in theory isn’t large and
that it simmers down to a sham in practice;
that they have been warned that the Govern-
ment, which has constituted itself the only
buyer, may presently supply no price at all
because it can’t get wheat away for want of
ships; and that it professes to know how to
build ships but won’t get a move on—in fact,
when the State Government and the B. 11.
Prop. Co. were about to build ships it inter-
fered and the business stopped. Compared
with these things, the complete boiling of
Fitzpatrick would, to be quite frank, be a
mere trifle to the wheat-grower. He also
wishes to tell the at present underdone Fitz-
patrick that in a good year he (the farmer,
not Fitzpatrick) now makes less than the
wages of a coal-miner, though he works long
hours and has hundreds—often thousands —
invested; that this is largely borrowed money
and he has to meet his liabilities or burst;
that he isn’t like a politician who carries
two ounces of plant and implements under
his hat and can be a Treasurer to-day and
a book canvasser to-morrow; that his costly
machinery ties him down to grain-growing;
and that the idea that he can use his ploughs
and reapers and chaffcutters for raising sheep
is quite wrong. Besides, the wheat man
seldom has enough land for sheep-growing,
• nd the price of lambs to start with is pro
hibitive. He would fain add, while Fitz-
patrick is simmering, that the price of
everything he buys, including labor, has gone
up, while the value of his own product has
gone down, or rather has been pushed down,
and if even a moderately bad year happens
he will make less than nothing. Of course
Fitzpatrick can reply that the wheat muddle
is a Federal matter, for which he isn’t re-
sponsible, but* in that case why should he
rise up in the middle of his shockingly mis-
managed financial job, with his liberal salary
in his hand, and be supercilious and boil?
Why doesn’t he stop at the Treasury and
boil at the sight of the terrible deficit and the
loan expenditure and the 5f per cent, interest
rate?

The Ships of Fact and Fiction.
As from the day Hughes started talking
about shipbuilding this paper prophesied
that he would still be wagging a valiant jaw
when an exhausted and bloodstained world
sat back to count up its horrors and draw
new maps, it cannot pretend to be either
disappointed or amused at what has hap-
pened since. But it can claim the right
to entertain profound contempt for the
political parties and the individuals who
one way and another have aided and abetted
the shocking exhibition made of Australia.
It is bad enough that the Commonwealth
should see what it sees to-day in relation to
recruiting. Yet what it has contemplated
in connection with shipbuilding is even
worse, for there is a side to this business
which makes the long record of talk and
piffling uncertainty look like a conspiracy
in the interests of the German. Two years
ago it was known to the Federal Govern-
ment, the Kaiser, the little boy at Manly,
the deadbeats in Sydney Domain and the
Censor that shipping was a vital factor in
the war. Soon after the same individuals
and most of their relations knew that, if
complete victory for the German did not
turn on the Allies’ shipping defeat, for some
of the Allies it most certainly did. And the
net result of this knowledge is that Austra-
lia, with enormous reserves of foodstuffs
awaiting shipment, has not driven a single
rivet into the frame of a ship. Mere state-
ment of the fact ought alone to be justifica-
tion for suspending someone on the end of
a rope. Our responsibilities in the matter
have been enormously increased by the un-
expected difficulties which Britain has met
and is still meeting in her struggle to replace
the sunken tonnage. Of course the facts
relative to the construction of new vessels
and the uses to w T hich available ships are
put have all been heavily obscured. “For the
last four months of 1916,” Bonar Law
was reported to have said lately, “the net
loss was greater than in the same period of
1917.” That sounded very well; but in 1916
the output was only 538,000 tons, which
represented a disastrous falling off in con-
struction, being less than a third of what it
had been in 1913! Plain information about
(1) the tonnage sunk and (2) the new ton-
nage launched in successive monthly or
quarterly periods has been carefully camou-
flaged. And because of that circumstance,
and because Minister for Labor Barnes
announces that the “workshops are in a fer-
ment and that there was more talk about Bol-
shevikism than wmrk,” it is to be feared that
the dinkum details about construction in
Britain and the Allies’ losses are as cheerful
as the scent of camel.
The Waste of Shipping.
“Pitt Street” : It is vain for the Minister
for the Navy to allege that everything pos-
sible has been done to divert shipping from
the coast to overseas. One-lmlf the inter-
state trade could easily be dispensed with.
For the most part it consists of transporting
produce from one part of Australia to
another, when the stuff carried could just as
well be grown in one State as another. One
has only to look at the stacks of potatoes and
chaff landing in Sydney from other States to
realise how disorganised or unorganised we
are. It may not be sound economics for
every man to grow his own potatoes when
he has perhaps more profitable work to do,
but it is certainly not economical in a time
of war to be hauling potatoes and oniony
and chaff and cabbages all over Australia by
land and sea when they can be grown almost
against one’s back door. Mr. Hughes and
Mr. Cook have done a fearful lot of talk
about co-ordination and the need for putting
every ounce of strength and effort into the
war; but they appear to be quite unpracti-
cal men, who spend all their energy in
speech. We are still being regaled every
day with items of news about the shipbuild-
ing programme, which will probably take no
tangible form until Messrs. Hughes and
Cook are transported to England and the
reins are held by men who do things instead
of talk things. We had some great hope in
Senator Millen and his Repatriation
scheme, but he too is talking much and doing
little. Indeed, the whole political machine
looks like a great piece of bluff and make-
believe at present. The only thing certain
is that Ministers are taxing and borrowing
and spending the money like water. The
truth is we have enslaved ourselves to a
political machine, which is manipulated by
wirepullers and produces nothing but ex-
travagant expenditure and bad blood through-
out the community. You must not criticise
or censure your own men, lest you bring
the other devils into power, and the other
devils khow quite well that their criticisms
and censures carry no weight, simply because
they are the other devils. How is a com-
munity to save itself from such humbug and
imposture? Is it any wonder that men are
losing faith in political institutions and
looking for more direct methods to achieve
reforms? We cannot go on much longer as
we have been going; there will be a smash
somewhere or other.
Our Revolution: To Be or Otherwise?
Let it be imagined that the voluntary sys-
tem of recruiting develops into an unex-
pected success, wfiat may be expected after-
wards? There will be a large exodus to the
war Front of the best section of the com-
munity, and the bad or middling or unfit
elements that remain will form a greater pro-
portion of the population than usual. Pro-
bably they may form a greater proportion
than at any time since the old ticket-of-leave
and bushranging days. There will be the
men wnose cnaracters can’t bear inspection,
for the theory that no one save a person of
good character should be shot in Flanders or
Palestine has been carried rather far. The
criminal, in or out of gaol, will be with us;
the hoodlum ; the chronic loafer ; the interned
enemies, including the crowd of Huns at
Berrima (N.S.W.) who play tennis, and run
a general sports club, and hold high meat-
and-drink revelry on the Kaiser’s birthday ;
and the Huns who are not interned. We
will still possess the I. W.W., and the crowd
to whom Simonoff, the new Russian “con-
sul,” lectures on the advantages of Bol-
shevikism, apparently in the hope that they
will go and do.likewise; and the disaffected
in general. The very old and the very young
will remain; the lunatics; the men who can’t
go. however willing, because they have a duty
to perform in organising strikes and hanging
up industry; the invalids, and the “conscien-
tious objectors.” Added to these will be a
number of returned soldiers with good inten-
tions, but more or less incapacitated by
wounds, and a number more who passed
muster here 'but were found to be incapaci-
tated on arrival. The moral of all which is
that a wholly successful voluntary system
will presently make the able-bodied Bol-
sheviker a much stronger force than ever he
has been as compared with the able-bodied
anti-Bolsheviker. A compulsory system
would remove many of the latter under mili-
tary discipline; the voluntary method
won’t. Experience has shown that the Bol-
shevik movement has room for all the lop-
sided people, from the philosophic visionary
with plans and specifications for a new
heaven and a new earth, down to the lout
who throws rocks at a Chinaman. And it
can keep them together long enough to do
almost unlimited wreckage. Without doubt
the solitary Federal policeman at Bowen—
the one whom W. M. Hughes appointed in
his excitement when an egg was thrown at
him—would put up a gallant fight against
the hordes of disorder, and would receive an
impressive funeral with wreaths if the Pro-
visional Government permitted; but it would
be a public sorrow if that officer fell facing
fearful odds single-handed in the grass-grown
street. One way and another, there are
gusty chances aoout voluntaryism.

A Sermon with a Tired Woman for the
Text.
The case of a soldier’s wife, the mother of
four, who was said to have vainly raked the
Sydney suburbs for many miles in search of
a habitation (cottage variety) at 18s. a
week, was lately ventilated in the press. It
sounds melancholy and it doesn’t sound im-
possible. but what else do the rulers of the
land expect? There are 671,300 people in the
Sydney suburbs and about 106,000 in the
city; total 777,300. Every year, despite the
exodus to the Front, the number increases.
In 1917 it increased by 12,700 and in five
years it has grown by 52,000. Even in 1916,
when the population of N. S. Wales in gen-
eral diminished by 21.500, that of Sydney
grew a little. Nobody except the farmer and
the rabbiter is encouraged to live anywhere
except in Sydney. No other deep-water port
save Newcastle has been opened to commerce
by railway communication, and much of the
trade which would naturally go to Newcastle
is attracted to Sydney by the simple device of

journey practically free. Also the export of
wool from Newcastle has been stopped by a
regulation under which it must come to Syd-
ney to be “appraised.” The pure atmosphere
of Sydney is the only atmosphere in which
wool can be appraised. So almost everybody
comes to Sydney where the industries are,
including the slaughter-yards and the Gov-
ernment Astronomer with his six assistants,
all looking for a new comet. Cottages are,

and the interest on the mortgage has sprung

actually put up only half as many new build-
ings in 1917 as he did in 1913 —not that he
has left off grasping, but ..because there is
mighty little to grasp under present condi-
tions. Wild horses and terraces of elephants
wouldn’t have torn him away from the good
work of supplying a soldier’s grass-widow
with a nice, sanitary, one-storey dwelling, fit
for humans to live in, for 18s. per week, if
it wasn’t that he would make a dead loss by
it. So he prefers to be a natriot and buy 5
per cent, war bonds. There is room for
100,000 or 200,000 hypothetical people to live
at much less cost in the non-existent and
indefinitely-postponed cities which aren’t
allowed, through Sydney influence, to rise at
Jervis Bay and Port Stephens—cities beside
splendidly deep harbors where the Govern-
ment might be sole ground landlord from
the beginning and the sweet flower of State
Socialism might bloom in the spring. But if
a fool people allows a fool Parliament to
make a fool Ministry whose policy is that
everybody should live in one spot, then the
spot must be paid for. Overcrowding is
one of the most costly and breathless luxu-
ries on earth. You can’t have it, with a
garden and a decent bathroom, for 18s. per
week.
