The Warwick Egg Hatches Something.
Whether Ryan won by a 5 to 2 majority
or a bit under matters next to nothing. What
counts is that the other crowd was swept off
the face of the earth again, and Ryan’s
man-power is more than enough for all pur-
poses, reasonable and otherwise. It is not
likely that anyone expected a different result.
The circumstances were all in his favor.
He was able to offer the public something
definite even if most of it was tripe; and
because it is much more profitable to offer
tripe than to mention the bill, and be-
cause the bill will have to be mentioned pre-
sently, Ryan recognised that the time was
just ripe. Therefore very wisely, in the
tactical sense, he rushed the election, and
secured great impetus from the Warwick
egg, the secret police and the riotous conduct
of Hughes. Until an agitated little Welsh-
man again went bersark a few months ago,
the Ryan Ministry was drifting to perdition
and knew it; and if it is anxious now to do
the graceful thing, a unanimous vote of
thanks to Hughes and his Win-the-War
Government, in the form of an illuminated
address, would be appropriate. So far as the
election can be regarded as a triumph for
any particular principles, the brutal truth
is that every other principle except the one
of letting the rest slifle and capturing votes
was treated to an early application of the
axe. In this respect Ryan and his friends
were only following a fashion common to
the whole of Australia, while their opponents
struggled hard to do the same thing. It is
surely something like a tragedy that while
the world is being shot to pieces and finan-
cial problems of ghastly size' are sitting on
the doorstep, the Labor party should be iden-
tified, as it formerly was in N. S. Wales and
now is in Bananaland, with policies and
practices designed to annex votes at the
price of debauching the constituencies. So
long as they can hand round jobs and p.n.’s
and keep wild cats alive, this paper appre-
hends that the Ryanites will find the job
of hanging on as easy as falling off. But
compared with the trouble they are making
for Queensland and themselves the last
cyclone was as tame as a bag of cabbages.
That Breach of the Peace Over Yonder.
On what is happening or going to happen
on the Western Front the official explainers
have lately been as dumb as frozen jumbucks.
Particulars handed out by others have in the
main consisted of trimmings for what is of
no importance. According to these it wbuld
appear that the German is feeling very miser-
able and the Australians very enthusiastic.
If we let it go at that for the moment it will
hurt nobody, anyway. Apparently the dis-
turbance on the French Front has been in-
tensified ; and as General Mud is taking him-
self off, the probabilities of an extension of
the riot increase. Whether the German is
going to fall on the Allies or whether the
Allies are prepared to fall on Yon Hinden-
burg, or whether all hands intend to simul-
taneously rush one anoth.er, are matters to
be heard about when something occurs. In
the interval the suspicion that shorter-range
conversations relating to peace have been in
progress is at least given color by a state-,
ment reported to have been made by Von
Bulow. This is that Germany is prepared
to talk about concessions and adjustments if
given “a free hand to deal with Russia.”
That means carving up the Bear to furnish
a German banquet. The Allies may not be
disposed to agree; but if they cannot dis-
suade the Bear from carving himself up, pro-
longation of the war to save him from
similar attentions from an outsider may not
provoke much enthusiasm. This paper is
persuaded that more is going to be heard
about this subject before long, for Russia is
clearly falling into a more inchoate mess,
and each step in the tragedy adds to the
difficulties of the Allies —or most of them, at
any rate. Incidentally, the commandeer-
ing of Dutch shipping by the Allies opens up
a new prospect for neutrals in future wars.
But what is more important at the moment
is that the use of these vessels will give John
Bull and others time to get their second
wind in the race against the submarine. Old
Von Tirpitz is alleged to be confident that
if the torpedo is kept working overtime all
will be well for his boss. He may be wrong;
but if the hurry-up of new construction in
England is not attended to with less muddle
than there has been in the past, he may be
horribly right. Shipbuilding is absolutely
vital, as W. Hughes has frequently men-
tioned while omitting to do any.
For the time being, at any rate, and so far
as State politics are concerned, Party is
pretty well smashed in. S. Australia, and
there is what those who can’t get out of the
old ways of thinking call “confusion.” This
means that the machines have not succeeded
in limiting the choice of the electors to two
men—neither of them much good, as a rule,
except as party wirepullers. For suburban
East Torrens there are nine nominations, and
for Sturt 10 (three seats available in each
case). In the country, Murray, Burra and
Barossa show the same rush. Among the
surprises is the absence of Clarence Goode,
brother-in-law of Crawford Vaughan and
member of his recent Ministry. Vaughan
himself is in the list, though it was thought
that his absence in America might
keep him out. He is in the Sturt 10,
which includes also Blackburn, V.C., and
Mrs. J. F. Young, who barracks for Propor-
tional Representation. Another less-known
lady is nominated for Adelaide, for which
also. Denny, wo<unded and in England, stands
again. For the Leg. Council, ex-Attorney-
General “Jim” Vaughan, now in the
trenches, is renominated. Kirkpatrick, the
veteran Labor leader and ex-Agent General,
has put aside his expressed wish to retire,
and Styles stands as an Independent. Pro-
bably the election is more important to
Styles than to anyone else in all the lists.
When he was made the scapegoat for the
costly land purchases by Vaughan’s Govern-
ment, he resigned from the present Coalition
Ministry, claiming and expecting a fresh in-
quiry. This having been refused him, he is
presenting himself as an utter Independent
to get into the Council and demand a hearing.
It is a queer platform on which to ask a dis-
trict to elect you as its representative. But
certainly the man ought to have had the
inquiry he asked for.
The plaintive contention of the squatter
that he will be simply ruined by “interfer-
ence” with the price of meat, gets badly
bumped by a report in Sydney Telegraph,
wherein N. C. Buck'nell, a station owner
in the Inverell district (N.S.W.), gave a
frank and edifying account of how the poor
sheepowner is doing in war times. Buck-
nell has some 24,000 acres, on which he
carries an average of 20,000 ewes and 500
to 1000 head of cattle. He has never carried
less than a sheep to the acre, and as to re-
sults, the Telegraph says :
For 10 years before the war his wool sold from
12}d. to 13Jd. His 1914 clip realised 14£d., 1915
clip 1916 clip 224 d., and the 1917 clip has
yet to be sold. In 1916 his 20,000 ewes, with 11,000
lambs at foot, averaged 101 b. of wool, which realised
a total of £16,000.
“The wool ! look upon as all profit,” Buck-
nell candidly informed the newspaper, and
it is fair guessing that, as his story was fur-
nished at the moment when a determined
effort was being made to keep up the price of
meat and the stockowners’ profits, the In-
verell man was heartily cursed for giving the
show away.
Dear Bulletin, —Mr. “Harrison O.” (B.
7/3/T8) has evidently missed the intention
of all my questions re Miss Rout’s proposed
sanitary bagnio. He says “Miss Rout
would. doubtless secure as her employees
recognised practitioners,” and incidentally
suggests an analogy between these and
trained nurses. There is no such analogy,
neither in the occupation nor in the training.
Prostitutes have all been innocent girls at
one time, and they neither require nor re-
ceive any training. To argue that securing
the services of prostitutes is different from
“procuring healthy girls” is a quibble. If no
one made use of prostitutes there would be'
no prostitutes. The responsibility for their
existence rests with those who use and pay
them; so one might as well procure the
innocent girl as wait till someone else has
done it for one. And then “Harrison O.’s”
other analogy—one person paying rates and
letting another work in a sewer —is quite
false. Work in a sewer is honest and neces-
sary. Prostitution is neither. All the rest
of his letter is answered by the trainers of
racehorses, who will tell you that sexual rela-
tions are not necessary for stallions when
racing. I can assure Mr. “O.” that at least
the great majority of medical men agree that
those relations are not absolutely necessary
for health in man. As to the possibility of
keeping an aseptic house for the purpose
referred to, the bacteriological examination
and suitable treatment of the women alone
would be absolutely useless. The proprietor
would be out of business in a week, for she
would have to submit every customer to sim-
ilar examination and treatment. And, lastly,
I remark to Mr. “O.” that the writer is a
middle-aged doctor, and knows something of
what he is taking about, and is not in the
habit of asking “foolish questions.” He leaves
that to his natients. —Yours, “Dr. Nil.”

“That all capital be nationalised forthwith ;
that all incomes over £5OOO a year be taxed
95 per cent.; that all private banks be elimi-
nated ; that rents on property be done away
with ; that peace be sought on a basis of no
annexations and no indemnities; that all
war debts be repudiated, except in cases
where undue hardship would be caused ; that
six hours constitute a day’s work; that all
candidates for public positions place an
undated resignation in the hands of the
executive.” Though they appear so at first
sight, these are not resolutions of the
Russian soviets ; they are merely a few of
the 304 proposals to be submitted to the
Easter Conference of the Vic. branch of the
Australian Labor Party. Probably not one
of the brilliant ideas will receive more con-
sideration than a dog gives to Orion’s Belt;
but if Labor has any intention of getting
into office again, it is time it appointed a
committee to go through conference propo-
sals and rule out those'that have no chance
whatever of acceptance, but are greedily
seized upon by opponents and held up for
public inspection as Labor ideals. There is,
indeed, a strong suspicion that that is what
some of them are there for.
To show how thoroughly he believed in
economy, the now-dispossessed Bowser of
Victoria passed bills to borrow £1,834,200,
which at 5 per cent, means an annual inter-
est charge for ever of £91,700. The “repro-
ductive works” on which the money is to be
spent include new schools, public buildings,
railway trucks and bridges damaged by
floods. No wonder Peacock cackled at the
joke.
Despatch from a returned Billjim now on
recruiting work: —
On Tuesday and Wednesday we were at Leeton
(N.S.W.) show and ran into trouble. Leeton is a
“dry” area, but on special occasions such as this
permission is granted to ran a booth for the sale of
liquor, and as a result there were more hogs in
Leeton than were ever bred there. The Holman
Government transplanted a lot of the turbulent
Broken Hill irreconcilables to this place, so you can
imagine what we were up against. As soon as we
got on the platform they set about trying to upend
things and break up our meeting. They hurled in-
sult after insult at us and at last got out of hand.
The climax was reached when one of them made a
blow at a member of our party and missed. But
he was not missed in return by one of the boys, and
took the count. In the end we were all at it, and
if you people could have seen it you would have
witnessed one of the finest examples of comradeship
ever. There were returned men coming in towards
us, fighting their way, from all directions, and
shouting, “Stick it, cobbers! we’ll soon be with you.”
And that is one of the things we are trying to make
the eligibles of Australia see—the meaning of true
comradeship. I may say that we hardly knew any
of our rescuers; but the mere fact of us being in
khaki and in a tight corner was all the argument
they needed.
“Talaponsi” : “Vandorian’s” reference to C.
F. Andrews’s report on indentured labor in
Fiji (B. 7/3/T8) recalls the ex-parson’s
sensational address to the members of the
Feminist Club in Sydney on the question
of the coolie women’s lot on the Fiji planta-
tions. The newspaper reports, or some of
them, were referred to the C.S.IL, and that
is possibly why the stuff was never printed.
The indignation of the Feminists also came to
naught, although on the night of the address
they were at white-heat to protest against
the degradation of little brown sister. The
C.S.R. is pleased to regard Andrews as a
paid spreader of sedition on behalf of the
Indian nationalists, and considers the
Empire’s present perilous condition should
be sufficient to stifle him. As against that
however, is the awkward fact that the Indian
Government has declared it to be in the best
interests of India that no more indentured
labor should go to Fiji. Andrews told the
Feminists that both sexes, married and
single, in some cases live in sheds fitted
with bunks without any attempt at privacy.
The fact that women are nearly 50 per cent."
less in number than the men makes woman
such a coveted possession that no husband
can keep his wife to himself; consequently
murder and suicide following paroxysms of
jealousy are frequent. But the green-eyed
monster does not only lurk in the environ-
ment which Andrews blames. Only last mail a
horrible story of murder came from beautiful
Tairuni. A planter’s wife heard the terri-
fied screams of her little daughters issuing
from where they were at play with their
Indian nurse. The mother rushed out, to
see the nurse (a prized possession of three
years, married and considered a model of
virtue) lying dead with* her head almost
severed, and her small charge unhurt in her
arms. The murderer (who was not her hus-
band) was standing by; but before the be-
wildered woman could do more than pick up
her baby he snatched a piece of rope and
ran into the bush, where he hanged himself.
That horror cannot be laid at the door of the
barrack system.

The most poignant thing in the procession
on Our Boys’ Day in Melbourne was a lone
female holding a huge sword in one hand and
a pair of scales in the other. She was
supposed to represent “Justice to our
Soldiers,” and the lady was blindfolded!
Perhaps it’s right.
Red tape is often abused in connection
with lav/; but it has its uses. A ship at
the wharf at Port Adelaide was lately
“arrested” for debt. The established pro-
cedure is to nail the warrant to the mast;
but the modern steel things do not lend them-
selves to that. The red tape came in here.
A length of it was produced, and with its
help the document was firmly “served.”
There was one fewer of the old-time
whaling captains left in Hobart when John
Christopher Jones answered the last signal
at Hobart the other day. Born 72 years ago
he had been in the Speck since 1853, and in
his day commanded the Marie Laure (mostly
called the Mary Laurie), the Derwent
Hunter, the Lady Emma and other whale-
chasers. Of late years he had been a signal-
man.
“Croson”: Being an advanced democracy
and in need of population, we look after the
public health. We provide infectious dis-
eases hospitals and insist on patients being
treated there; also we possess Health
officers and inspectors and Boards of Health
—and then this sort of thing happens. A
Collingwood (Vie.) mother was attacked by
diphtheria, was taken to the Infectious Dis-
eases Hospital and died there. Next a
daughter who had helped to place her mother
in the ambulance got the disease and also
died. Then a son followed, and he died, too.
At this point the family concluded that the
locality was unhealthy, and left the house;
also considerable bedding. It being appar-
ently nobody’s business to fumigate the pre-
mises and burn the bedding, the latter was
stacked in the yard and left, so that the sun
and wind might ripen the germs and waft
them abroad. Various prospective tenants in-
spected the “desirable detached cottage,” but
made inquiries as to the unusualness of so
much bedding at large and went away hastily
on learning the facts. /
“Gunner”: “Whist” on the subject of
hearing shells coming (B. 7/3/T8) is all at
sea. Sound does not travel at 40ft. per
second, but at 1120 ft.; and a muzzle velocity
of 80ft. per sec. is equally absurd. Here
are a few British gun muzzle velocities:
ISpt. Q.F., 1610 ft. per sec.; 60pt. 8.L.,
2080 ft. per sec.; 4.5-inch Howitzer, 1010 ft.
per sec. “Whist’s” statement about hearing a
shell pass overhead and at the same time
seeing it burst 100 ft. further on is a “flight
of fancy.” I don’t know, either, why he
should consider the “muzzle velocity” in
making a calculation at the end of the pro-
jectile’s _ flight. The “remaining velocity”
is what is then required.
The. Senate’s committee which is inquiring
into liquor and the army has been taking
evidence in Adelaide. Blackburn, V.C., the
returned men’s president, favors a wet can-
teen and believes that the frequency of
French estaminets has an enormous influence
in keeping the Australians contented. Drink
has affected discipliile very little, he thinks.
But Forsyth, Commandant in S. Australia,
has an idea that drink is “the worst enemy
a soldier has, so far as efficiency is con-
cerned.” If that is so, this paper has to con-
clude with deep regret that the medical and
other Defence Department services in S. Aus-
tralia can be rarely sober.

Though the scenery is overstocked with
strident lavenders and greens, Fuller’s panto,
of ‘‘Kobinson Crusoe” at Melbourne Bijou
seems likely to haunt its supporters’ memo-
ries for some time to come. The show is
not rigidly decorous in jokes and jingles, nor
distressingly refined in other particulars, but
it provides the right amount of innuendo and
.assault to catch the good silver of the Bloke
About Town. The songs are mostly gay
exposures of defective vocabulary, whilst the
principals are nearly all rough and hearty,
and there is no visible difference in effect
between Clias. Zoli’s Pirate Chief, Walter
Cornock’s Dame, and several others who are
too humorless to mention. The leading boy,
Nellie Kolle, gets the large help of a hand-
some exterior, but her Warble might have
been trained by Mons. Rabbiteau. All the
same, she is the Bijou’s Melba, and fills
many a heart with rapture during her deaf-
ening chorus upheavals. Nellie Fallon, the
principal girl, looks dainty, Dorothy Hastings
catches the ear with a clear and pleasing-
soprano, and Maud Fanning, as a Cannibal
Queen, dashes into Coonland with all her old
ardor. Victor Prince fits out Robinson
Crusoe with his physical plenty in chest and
chin, but there is never a moment when
Victor is anything more than an incident in
the scheme of things. The best turns in the
performance are a whirlwind bike act inside
a steel-ribbed globe, and an intelligent
appearance by two monkeys and a dog.

For one more week the professed exponents
of tender passion at Melbourne Majesty’s
will sing “You’re in Love” to the artless
William Greene, after which he will marry
Maude Fane under the stern supervision of
Connie Ediss. Auntie’s efforts to keep
Maude, though married, from the “clutches
of God’s noblest animal, Man,” are not vei’y
humorous material for a musical comedy to
work on. In the reflected light of her extra-
ordinary authority the young people present
all the appearance of chumps; and a chump
hero and heroine have no air of sentimental
importance. Also the audience’s taste for
waltz-time music gets somewhat cloyed by
monotonous references to Love, and Dreams,

This night week will see the present Mel-
bourne Royal bill off the premises. Till then
the New York magnate’s wife will continue
to lift some lace by stealth from Gilder's
counter, and be treated for kleptomania
rather than crime. Mary Turner will per-
severe with her endeavors to make a lady
out of Aggie in order to improve her black-
mailing prospects, and “Within the Law”
will complete its satisfactory revival. People
who don’t remain slaves to their first im-
pressions are speaking nicely of this revival.
It is not so rbugh on the police as the first
production was, and by calling- further atten-
tion to the story it awakens interest in the
fate of Aggie and the other crooks whom
Mary loved. The end of Joe Carson is not
left in doubt. The Law wipes out Joe for
stool-pigeon shooting. But Aggie and the
others? In what way does Mary get rid of
them after her marriage into the Gilder
family? If she pays Aggie to remain good
it must cost her considerable dollars per
annum.

At Melbourne Tivoli the city’s insatiable
appetite for panto, is getting two meals a
day as before. “Jack and the Beanstalk”
doesn’t disimprove on acquaintance. Items
like the song to the kiddies and “My Gramo-
phone Girl’* have some charm as well as
noise in them; in fact, if the latter had
only half as much of Mabelle Morgan’s per-
sistent top notes it would be twice as pleas-
ing. The Cow still answers to the name of
Jessie, which is the name most panto, cows
are christened by. The curtailment of the
Giant’s trousers is still a widespread pro-
ceeding, and the King has abated none of
his native unimportance. Considered as a
crowned monarch he is about the nearest
approach to a casual bloke that any panto,
ever struck. On the other hand, Daisy
Sylvester is a graceful Spirit of the Times
(of long ago, when columbines were in
fashion) and the crudity of Dame Trot is

Gregan McMahon
wishes to draw intelli-
gent public attention,
as well as the best
highbrow patronage, to
Granville Bar k e r’s>
comedy. There is a
public that wants good
plays. The trouble is
to make it realise its
soul’s desire.
Ye crooks, who in a devious way
Are seeking dollars all the time,
Go ye to see the Yankee play
That draws from Muggs and Gilly’s crime
A moral to improve the mind.
Good felons, looking for the Light,
Can here a plain direction find—
“ Turn to the Right,”
“Turn to the Right.”
Up Bourke-street, from the G.P.0.,
A bloke wot eighteen months deserves,
May, in a chastened spirit, go
To learn the worth of peach preserves.
With resolute and rapid feet
Walk up that highway any night,
And when ye get to Russell-street
Turn to the Right,
Turn to the Right!
The dramatist, in merry tones,
This maxim teaches at the King’s:
“A burglar may, on stepping-stones
Of peaches, rise to better things”—
May join, through Jam, a wowser sect,
And then, his erring past despite,
Preserve his peach-like self-respect.
“Turn to the Right,”
Oh, turn to the Right!

The corpse of the fatuous millionaire who
loved vermilion moments with red-haired
females is demanding an explanation all this
week at Melbourne Britannia. The feature
of this narrative, christened “Empty
Pockets,” is some of the choicest Tenderloin
scenery that Noo Yark can offer." At the
Melba “The Soul of Satan” fastened hordes
to their chairs for the first three days this
week. In this frenzy Gladys Brockwell as a
soiled dove comes to bring not peace but a
brick to the man who discarded her. To-day
Mrs. Vernon Castle reopens the floodgates
of her mannerisms in “Sylvia of the Secret
Service.”
Current shadows in action at Hoyt’s Mel-
bourne houses are Jack Pickford and Louise
Huff in “What Money Can’t Buy,” adapted
from a George Broadhurst play, and afford-
ing some pretty adventures to the two young
people. Louise is the vest-pocket edition
of an Anthony Hope princess, and Jack
represents American muscle and money that
wrest her from her position of insecurity
and want. There are no unhealthy moments
in this incredible story, and the sentiment is
as artless as kiss-in-the-ring. “Out of the
Wreck,” in which a lady’s past is photo-
graphed in the manner of “On Trial,” yields
some excitement in the earlier scenes and
keeps the lead, Kathleen Williams, busy
throughout.

$* * #
This paper has still to have the pleasure
of meeting the Perfect Pierrots. Strollers
and Dandies come and go, all with clean,
smart, artistic performances; but always
there are dull moments. There is one comic
man who isn't comic, or one singer who sings
one song too many, or one burlesque act
that is a dud. Still, if the English Pierrots
who opened at Sydney Playhouse on Satur-
day in “Pierrotland” don’t score the longed-
for 100 per cent., they climb to a level of
mirth-making and music that is at least as
high as the average. From what this critic
could see in a back seat in the O.P. corner,
the best points are Elsa Langley’s piquant
personality, her pleasant voice and the art-
istry of her acting (her two Dutch songs
are most excellent, and she makes of a duet
with Leslie Austin a poem of refined gesture
and daintiness) ; Billie Wilson’s impersona-
tion of a kiddie reciting (most actresses
impersonate a grown woman impersonating
a child) ; Roy Cooke’s dramatic rendering
of a charming Maori song; the quaint turn
of Mitchell, Lawrence and Turner, who Sim-
ultaneously sing three different songs; the
cleverness of Charles Lawrence at the piano;
and the ensemble work of the melodious
company. The show is quaintly staged and
the dressing is admirable. For those who
like crude burlesque there are two passable
attempts; and those who want the famous
trio from “Faust”—not a burlesque—will
find it forcibly rendered, though the item
seems out of place in a programme of mirth.

The Australian spy film, “The Enemy
Within,” which is chiefly an excuse for
Snowy Baker to display some thrilling acro-
batics, has adjourned to Sydney Lyric for
this week. The Royal is giving the movies
another brief innings, and a Goldwyn version
of Re-x Beach’s “Auction Block” is at the
wickets. Albert Goossens and other concert
artists help to brighten the proceedings of
the popular marriage-market story. Another
fine novel, Robert Hitchen’s “Garden of
Allah,” is being shadowgraphed at the Crys-
tal Palace, a feature of the film being excel-
lent glimpses of the sort of country that Bill-
jim of the Light Horse and Camel Corps
has been frying under. “What Came Out of
the Wreck” is for inspection at Hoyt’s,
blackmailers and bigamists suffering heavy
casualties in this Paramount thriller. For a
dainty round-off to all Sydney screen excite-
ments this week there’s Marguerite Clark,
who, as “The Valentine Girl,” at the Hay-
market, discovers a lot of things a college
miss doesn’t have to know to get five A’s.
* * *
In the long ago the Wirths contracted a
habit of falling upon Sydney at Easter-time,
after the manner of Noah paying a visit to
Ararat. And when they unpack the ele-
phants and the lions and the educated ponies
and the sheep and the dogs and the muscular
ladies and gentlemen, and when the pliocene
witticisms of the humorist with the red nose
and the astounding clothes have been un-
tangled and the complicated apparatus inci-
dental to the aerial gymnast -has been fixed
up, the proceedings commence, Welby Cook
conducting with a half-mile whip. Where-
fore, the appointed time having again
arrived, the Hippodrome in Campbell-street
is now inhabited by some old friends and
numerous accomplished strangers and the
aroma of wild animals. It is a cheerful show
decorated with doubt as to whether the lions
are going to consume the trainer in full view
of the audience, and speculation as to whether
50 tons of elephant will sit on the orchestra.
The first circus seen by the writer was at a
place called Babylon, and though elephants
and horses were at that entertainment there
was certainly nothing on the programme so
good to watch as the work of some of the
performers who have lately come to Sydney.
The Flying Winstills, who do things in the
vicinity of a trapeze; Belle Onra and Les
Monforts, two gymnasts of exceptional nerve
and grace; the Zandil Sisters, billed as the
“human aeroolanes,” and the Cevine troupe
of wire walkers are entitled to the respectful
admiration which this journal hands them.
It would give them, an illuminated certificate
of merit but for the great scarcity of paper.

** * *
Adelaide Majestic has a bright bill;
Sylvia has a fine voice, and Phrozo causes
a lot of guessing, though she is not as un-
guessable as the Fuller management makes
out. The Tivoli has been giving a week to
“A London Arab,” with good parts for the
children Gladys Gaul and Eileen Murphy;
Darcy Kelway and Beaumont look after the
relief. This theatre now promises a play
that “makes you think.” A new team of
“Humoresks” has come in at Austral Gar-
dens—with great pluck, considering the time
of year and coolness of evenings. The chief
feature of the picture-shows is good, hard
fighting. Desmond as an Irishman at West’s,
and Fairbanks at Woudergraph, use their
fists (and other weapons) as though they
really enjoy the exercise. The Pav. has
Ethel Barrymore and the always enjoyable
O. Henry storyettes. The Royal keeps on
being crammed with the Williamson “Dick
Whittington” panto.; it is brilliant as a
spectacle and remarkable for its dancing—
Maggie Dickenson, Maude Amery and Syd-
ney Yates leading. But the biggest audience
of the week attended a free show thrown on
to the balcony of the Criterion Hotel from
the Prince Alfred opposite: it is an attrac-
tive picture of the life of a soldier after
recruiting, and lasts over an hour.
** * *
Of a Melbourne art show :
Forty-four drawing's by Ida Rentoul-Outhwaite at
the Fine Art Society’s rooms show a weakening
imagination and a discursive pen. Really the lady’s
adventures in Fairyland are recorded with the. soulless
precision of an anatomical chart-maker. She
describes everything to the last leaf of a forest and
the ultimate curl on a fairy’s neck. In meticulous-
ness of detail she is the Arnold Bennett of pictorial
fiction. Yet her technical equipment is good, and
she has a dexterous pen-line, demonstrated in such
examples as “The Cradle of Dreams” and “Election
Day,” that is always the main feature of her
achievement. Also her water-color is pleasing in
some tinted efforts, though it is as transparent as her
intention, and rarely arises above the simplicity of
washes. Of all her creations the least satisfactory
are her much-vaunted fairies; they lack the ethereal
and the breath of life, and might be members of the
best families got up for a birthday party.
Mary Marlowe, who figured as heroine in
some early Bailey-Grant productions, has
blossomed as a novelist, with “Kangaroos in
King Land,” a vivacious account of the ad-
ventures here and in England of a bright
quartette of Australian girls. The book,
published in London, has been favorably
noticed.

SOCIETY
MINCHINBURY
“Mirrool”: Norman Lindsay’s picture of
the little kids asking the War Monster
“Why do you want to hurt us?’’ recalls the
most pathetic thing I saw in my IS months
in France. The French civilian population
hung on to Armentieres in a most remarkable
fashion. Generally when a town or village
is within easy distance of the guns some at
least of the people fall back; but very few
left Armentieres, and live days a week ypu
could see the youngsters going to school with
their satchel over one shoulder and their gas
mask over the other.
“Frank S. It.” : How’s this for the Homing
Stakes? In December, 1914, a Testament
left for Egypt with a sergeant of the 6th
L.II. Both eventually reached Gallipoli,
where the sergeant was killed. The Testa-
ment was returned to Australia through
official channels. It arrived in time to be
taken to France by a nephew. At
Bapa-ime the lad was wounded in the arm
and chest. A piece of shell cut open the
tunic pocket and allowed the Testament to
fall unnoticed to the ground. A Tommy
from a Middlesex regiment picked it up and
started it oft' per registered post to the pre-
vious owner’s address in Australia. On the
way the mailboat Mongolia was torpedoed.
The Testament was in one of the 30 mail-
bags washed ashore. Six months later an
early-morning fire totally destroyed a large
weatherboard house. The occupants escaped
in their night attire. The Testament was
one of the few articles they snatched up
as they ran. It had passed unharmed
through shot, shell, water, fire and the bags
of P.M.G. Webster.
Want to know what Black Brother really
thinks of the German? Tiie Bulletin
can’t tell, but this is just as it comes from
a half-caste abo. of the Burnett (Q.) :
The list time up in the line I killed five German.
I did it because they held up their hand till we got
about ten yard off them and then the dirty bruits
threw bombs at us so that’s why I killed them. If
they hadn’t thrown bombs at us we would have taken
them Prisnors and they would have been alright.
This ten for me since I been in France.
By the way, it is frequently mentioned that
this wounded man or the otfyer was carried
back by German prisoners. ' Don’t because
of that run away with the idea that these
prisoners are necessarily kind-hearted chaps
who want to give their wounded enemies a
hand. They simply rush the job because it
offers themselves the greatest possible pro-
tection till they get well back behind the
lines.
* * * »
The Methodist? of S. Aus. (and anybody
is liable to be a Methodist in S. Aus.) are
beginning to wonder whether the proposal
for settling things up after the war by let-
ting communities determine their own fate is
what it is cracked up to be. “Conference,”
which sits annually, decides for every
Methodist pulpit who its boss preacher shall
be Over this there have often been rum-
blings in the pews and grumblings out in the
porch ; and now the congregation at suburban
Hindmarsh, where the bricks come from, has
gone on strike almost unanimously because
it has been shorn of the parson it has had
for a couple of years and still wants. Actu-
ally pickets were posted or posted them-
selves, their tale of woe being that Confer-
ence had hit their man under the belt not for
being a poor parson, but for taking the Anti
side, against the vast majority of his cloth,
in the Conscription fight.
* * * *
“Spare Field-Marshal”: It’s about time
“H.G.T.’s” majah, who invited the orderly
to drop his buckets and salute
(B. 14/3/T8), was taught his
job. Any man employed on
fatigue work is not supposed to
salute when his hands are occu-
pied—he is to turn his eyes
right or left, as the case may •
be. If a non-com. be present,
he should give the order “Eyes
right” (or left), or in certain
cases the privates stand fast
while the senior non-com.
salutes the officer. The igno-
rance amongst some of the so-
called senior officers is appall-
ing. I have seen more than
one of them salute with the head
bare; I have even heard of them standing
at ease while the National Hymn was being
played.

everywhere, no firearm smaller than a cannon
could be discovered. The disgusted astonish-
ment of the cabby became vocal when they
offered him the loan of a fire shovel. What
he said doesn’t matter; but it appears that
if the Wolf’s aeroplane had lobbed in that
barrack-yard the garrison would have had to
repel the raiders by pelting stones at them.

“E,L,S.” r Just out from Victor Harbor
(S.A.) there is an old cemetery. Almost
next to one another are the graves of the
respective spouses of a man and woman who
afterwards married. The woman, over the
grave of her dearly beloved, swore eternal
loyalty to her departed. On the day she mar-
ried No. 2, lightning, thrown by an unknown
hand, split the tombstone of her late hus-
band, and the stone of her husband’s dead
wife tilted over.

WHEN THE ANZAC DEAD CAME HOME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lik(> a gust of leaves they drifted, by vagrant eddies
blown ;
They hung above their continent, lost, loved, known.
They hovered there uncertainly; they could not
understand:
They had fought, failed, fallen for an unremembered
land.
Some drifted down the town streets; they saw a
cheerful throng—
. Fair women swathed in fripperies, tall men that
slouched along;
Sweet girls that clung round windows like bees on
honeycomb,
Behind the counters sleek males. To this they had
come home!
And one soul noticed bright upon a soldier’s empty
sleeve
His old battalion c010r5.... his mate! It must be
Steve!
That soul about his mate hung ere it was wafted
far....
Strange the pain that stabbed his pal, an ache in
that old scar!
And Steve did not look prosperous; his pallid face
was grim.
He wasn’t even selling shirts—no limousine for him!
And the dead soul knew, amid that alien host,
His pal was more than pal now, a living fellow-
ghost.
One fluttered through the Councils, and found them
just the same:
Big word-warriors still played the Party game;
They nobly planned campaigns of words, and deemed
their speeches deeds,
And fought fierce offensives for strange old creeds.
And unions mui’dered industry, and men downed
tools,
And profiteers grew wealthier, making all the rules.
And one soul wandering about the wharves forlorn
Met baby-souls of ghost-ships waiting to be born 1
And all the souls blown inland at last to earth came
down—■
A strange snow-fall on a bare plain brown ;
And they muttered to each other, and their vague
murmur said:
“ ’Tis we who are the living; this continent is dead!
“Australia, that we loved so, has all our hopes
denied;
She hasn’t kept her end up; the darned old cow has
died !
Australia that we lived for, and for her fighting fell,
Is but am empty corpse now—let’s heave it into
Hell 1”
SHE HAD CLAWS, TOO.
“If you catch the same train to-morrow
night you will see my girl.”
“If you go to the war you may see my hoy.”
By its far four corners they flung it into Space;
They cheered the ancient pancake as it settled to its
place....
And though it’s damned for ever in the deepest lake
in Hell,
Australians don’t discuss it now —perhaps it's just as
well!
N.S.W.

A CLASSICAL MASTER’S LETTER to CLEMENTS TONIC
Mr. G. A. Lundie, M.A., formerly Assistant-Headmaster High School, Palmerston North, N.Z., writes of this Medicine
And recommends it to all professional people, especi-
ally to teachers in the Educational service of Aus-
tralia, who suffer from weak nerves, insomnia or
brain fag.
“Palmerston North,
“New Zealand.
“CLEMENTS TONIC LTD.,
“I am an unsolicited witness to your remark-
able remedy.
“I suffered for years from a form of nervous
complaint, which manifested itself in profound
melancholia which I could not throw off.
Clements Tonic Ltd.. P.ozelle, N.S.W.
“My appetite was capricious to a degree, and
I developed ill-health, till life was a real burden.
“I was, furthermore, threatened with a com-
plete nervous breakdoAvn. I could hardly fol-
low my duties as a high-school master, much
less give attention to serious teacher’s duties.
“I can honestly say your potent medicine
came to me as a revelation in healing, for I
regained my health completely through its con-
sistent use.
“For teachers engaged in mental strain and
arduous study, your tonic is indispensable as a
stand-by.
“I have recommended it to dozens of other
teachers, with most beneficial results.
“I am, Sirs, your faithfully,
“G. A. LUNDIE, M.A ”
There are hundreds of teachers of both sexes employed in
the N.S.W. Educational Service to-day who suffer from weak
nerves or acute nervousness, through continuous nerve strain in
teaching > and study, who never really enjoy good health. It is
as much the worry of keeping their schools up to the educa-
tional standard as the work which accounts for their feeling of
general ill-health. They should keep Clements Tonic on hand
as a “stand-by” medicine, feeling certain that in its possession
they will have a sweet soother of the nerves, a promoter of
keen appetite, and a general healer of the rundown and over-
wrought system. It makes weak people well and strong people
stronger. Get it to-day.
ALL chemists and stores sell it everywhere.
“Fax”: With all due admiration for John
Madden as a husband, father, courtier and
social-functioner, this writer had too keen a
recollection of the lawyer’s past to entertain
any illusions about him afterwards. To
begin with, lie was mainly a Crim. Court
barrister, who didn’t stick at much to save
his client. As a judge he was notorious for
giving decisions that were quashed by a
higher Court, and one of his decisions in the
first year of the war had to be quashed by
Federal Government order within an hour or
two of its utterance. Madden ruled that the
Government had no power to intern influ-
ential enemy aliens moving in the best Mel-
bourne sassiety, and the scandal of his ruling
(which the High Court laughed to scorn)
was emphasised by the fact that family
friends of the Judge were waiting to rejoice
with the disinterned person as soon as he left
the court. This is why the truth about
Madden ought to be told and remembered;
it is the best illustration of the objections to
the system of appointing a judge to be the
< lovernor’s lodum-tenens. Madden had a
large family circle that revelled in the oppor-
tunities to vice-regalise; and in the end it
was practically impossible for him to be con-
fronted in court with any expensive peti-
tioner, or defendant, or worthless bounder
of a culprit, who hadn’t been a more-or-less
honored guest of his at Government House.
It wasn’t, in the nature of the man to be
coldly impartial under such circumstances.
No judge should be exposed to the demoral-
ising influence of Acting-Governorship. And
least of all should a judge who hasn’t a
cold, impartial judgment to begin with.
Unless he is a particularly unimaginative
Hun, the first thing the Wolf’s skipper did
when he got home from his raid was to call
for Hochs for Marconi. Without wireless he
would have gone home a very lean Wolf —if
he had got home at all. He had a general idea
of the Matunga’s whereabouts, but it was
only when his installation picked up a
message to Rabaul announcing the time of
her probable arrival that lie knew exactly
where she was. Then he pounced on Irel-
and found badly-needed coal and sundries.
Similarly lie got wind of the Hitachi Maru’s
position, and nailed her. Off Trinidad
Islands an intercepted message gave him the
hint to quit, because a Chilian warship was
nosing around. And several times in the
Pacific he might have bumped trouble but for
the messages exchanged between imminent
cruisers. The Wolf sent out no messages
herself, but was always ready to take one in.
What a grimly-satanic picture of patience is
that German wireless-operator, crouched day
and night over his instrument, receiver on
ears, listening, listening! Old Carlyle might
have been dourly delighted to give him a
niche among his Immortals, alongside the
Bronze Lamplighter of the Bastile!

“The Hangman’s Understudy” discourses:
A tenant of the condemned cell in the Melbourne
Gaol has little or no opportunity of “racing the rope.”
Two cells —sleeping-room and living-room—are pro-
vided for his use. He has no mattress, but plenty
of blankets make a comfortable bed. Belts, shoe-
laces, straps and string are forbidden articles ; quills
instead of steel pens are given to him to write with.
A sleeping suit is provided, thereby enabling the
warder to frequently examine his lodger’s clothing.
Smoking is allowed, the tobacco being cut up ; but
chewing is tabooed. Tea, coffee and cocoa are avail-
able at any hour, but the common belief that a con-
demned man is allowed grog, and the rumors of mis-
guided gentlemen meeting their doom practically
drunk, are false. Intoxicating drink is strictly for-
bidden. A close supervision is kept over visitors, and
when deemed necessary they are searched. As
“the prisoner’s feelings are to be respected,” work
on the gallows is not to be done within his hearing.
Two or three days before the real performance the
boss has a rehearsal, measures the drop, stretches, the
rope, and tests it with a weight corresponding to that
of the leading man. Thus satisfaction is guaranteed,
and there is no grumbling when the Old Man and I
get our little bonus.
The Turkish troops, which are under Ger-
man command, now own the Russian town
of Trebizond. Of the population which
failed to get away some were crucified, some
were burned, some were killed in various
xashions, and a number of children were put
into bags and drowned. So Trebizond is now
available for colonisation. Incidentally,
and apart from any question of drowning
infants in Sydney Harbor, an influential
German writer has just stated that after the
war one of Australia’s duties will be to sup-
ply Germany gratis with zinc, wool, wheat
and frozen meat, so apparently there will be
no lack of work in days to come. Just here
it may be mentioned that the over-fed Hun
prisoners at Berrima (N.S.W.), who enjoy a
brass band and a tennis court and a great:
extent of well-kept garden, held a royal
blow-out to celebrate William’s birthday.
This brutal Government, however, still re-
fuses to supply them with a wireless appara-
tus. <•

“Bedside-Manner, M. 8.”: The other night
a Sydney pal who holds up a special post
got a late message from the police that there
had been a row down in an unfashionable
quarter and a woman was lying injured.
“Better take a constable with you,” ran the
postcript. “It’s a very rough house.” “Um,’
said the old campaigner, “if that's the way
of it she can wait till morning.” Some time
after daylight he collected a young police-
man and started off. Arrived at the house
they found the front of it locked. The doc.
sent the constable round to the back, and he
presently returned with the news that they
could get in that way. The two explorers
entered and found, gracefully arranged on a
bed, a youmr woman. She was unconscious
and undressed. Both visitors stared; but
the policeman stared harder when the med.
started tickling the soles of the poor victim’s
feet —a well-known method of restoring con-
sciousness. Presently Beauty’s eyelids flick-
ered and she woke up with a tired smile.
“Well, my girl,” said Brass Plate, “what’s
the matter with you?” She yawned prettily.
“Nothing that I know of, old sport. But if
you’re the doctor, the girl that was hurt last
night has gone home to her husband.”

I)r. Birks, of the Broken Hill Hospital,
concefning an obstreperous inhabitant:—
The germ of the disease known as X enters the
blood through the air passage, and is carried to
various organs, especially the brain. The germs
burst through the little blood-vessels and rapidb
set up inflammation round the point where they broke
through. In most cases death is so rapid, through
interference with the vital centres of the brain, that
nothing but minute areas are affected. We know
that the germs break through the blood-vessels in
many other parts of the body—for instance, the
kidneys, spinal cord and intestines. The patients
are, however, usually killed by the process in the
brain, before the germs have time to affect the other
organs.
Some other idiosyncrasies of X are that he
seems to toil only during the first two or
three months of the year; he seldom assaults
females, and never adult females; and ho
kills 90 per cent, of the unfortunates upon
whom he calls. The brevity of his “season”
suggests that he probably arrives and goes
with some fruit.

ACID STOMACHS NEED MAGNESIA.
Drugs may give temporary relief from the
pain which usually results from an excess of
acid in the stomach, but drugs do not elimi-
nate the acid, and in consequence the pain
returns—worse than before. To effect a cure
you must first eliminate the acid which is the
cause of all the trouble, and a sure way to
accomplish this is to take a little bisurated
magnesia after your meals. This quickly
neutralises the harmful acid; prevents the
food turning sour and fermenting, soothes
and heals the inflamed stomach and enables
you to again eat and enjoy the foods you
fancy, secure in the knowledge that no pain
or discomfort will result. Of course it will
be understood that ordinary forms of mag-
nesia, such as the citrates or sulphates, will
not accomplish these happy results, neither
will crude mixtures of bismuth and mag-
nesia. You must get the bisurated mag-
nesia, which is a form prepared specially for
use in cases of digestive and stomach trouble.
This can readily be obtained of high-class
chemists everywhere in both powder and
tablet form, the latter being especially suit-
able for travellers and others compelled to
take meals away from home.— Advt.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
As in the case of the Chief Justice of
N. S. Wales, the late John Madden’s pro-
spects at the Bar once looked so dull that he
was a candidate for a small University billet.
The job that C.J. Cullen was lucky enough
not to get was at Adelaide.
John Madden wasn’t the only Chief Jus-
tice and Lieut.-Gov. who used to put on the
“mits.” Edward Stone, of Westralia, in a
little volume of reminiscences which Frank
Henty has just seen through the press for
him, confesses that “when I was a young
man boxing amounted almost to a passion
with me, and to this day I glory in having
had the privilege of seeing the late Tom
Sayers in a match,” and Heenan, too. Stone
was Westralia’s best all-rounder in his day—
footballer, cricketer, oarsman and something
extra as a rough-rider. For lighter recrea-
tion the gay young spark sang in the Cathe-
dral, where he was afterwards choirmaster.
There was a period when the Westralian
Supreme Court might at any rate have chal-
lenged any other Supreme Court in Aus-
tralia to a sort of South-street competition.
Besides Stone there was Onslow, the Chief
Justice, who at one time was Perth’s favorite
baritone; and Hensman, J., was a violinist
who conducted the Perth Musical Union.
Victoria has had only four Chief Justices.
The first two, a’Beckett and Stawell, had
retired on pension and died out of Australia
—-a’Beckett in England, Stawell in Naples.
Iliginbotham and Madden died in office. The
vacancy, according to the gossip of the corri-
dors, will be filled by an Australian native,
either Isaacs of the High Court or Cussen
of the State Supreme Bench. Isaacs is 62 ;
Cussen 58—a chicken among very old birds.
“Lantana”: Thousands of us women in
Sydney don’t care a hairpin what brand of
religion or politics Dr. Mannix recommends;
but what we would love to know is just
what he eats —or doesn’t eat. He has the
slenderest, most elegant figure I have ever
seen in a man’s clojfhes. In fact, it might
be described as a silhouette. Fasting evi-
dently has no terrors for his Grace, nor
would it have for lots of sinners if they
could thereby get their clothes to “set” like
his. And his biretta! The exquisite
shade, like the heart of a pomegranate, with
a fluffy pom-pom that wouldn’t be safe to
hang up in a mixed crowd!
It was Hugh Mahon, lately Minister for
Home Affairs, who wrote the address to
John Redmond which the Orange (N.S.W.)
people presented to him 30-odd years ago and
which led to those signatories who were
J’s.P. being struck off the roll.
Frederick Halcomb, Clerk of the S. Aus.
Leg. Council, is 81, which is not old in
Adelaide; but ill-health prompts him to drop
work. J. C. Morphett, a youth of 74, now
Clerk of the Assembly, takes his place. He
is Australian born. So is Alfred Searcy,
who moves up from being Sergeant-at-Arms.
Searcy is best known by his experiences in
the Northern Territory and the jolly books
he has written about them. His brother
Arthur is chairman of S.A. Harbors
Board ; their father was once Chief Inspec-
tor of Police in Adelaide. Morice and
Freeborn are the other Parliamentary offi-
cials who get a step. Both are experts in
Parliamentary library matters. Freeborn
used to be tutor to the Teck children, one of
whom is now Queen Mary.
“P.X.” sees a good soggarth off: —
Archdeacon Connelly, who died in S. Aus. the
other day, was for 20 years the best beggar West o’
Darling. Coming to Australia from India, where he
was born, and where he worked for years as an
army chaplain, he took a rich brogue and a long
head to Broken Hill when the silver town was very
young; and he knew hundreds where his recluse
bishop, Dr. Dunne, knew only dozens. When a
new father arrived it was Connelly who showed
him the ropes. One, a Russian, imported to reach
the polyglots in the mines, knew English as the
books taught him; and Connelly proceeded to teach
him the other kind. “Now, look you here,”' said
the Man who Knew, “the Bishop will send you out
to get in a thrifle for the poor nuns. Now, do ye
know what this is?” “A sovereign,” said the book-
man. “Tut, tut,” said Connelly, “ ’tis a quid.
And this is half a quid. And this is a dollar; and
this is half a dollar. And this is a sprat.” “And
how did you fare?” said the correct old bishop as
the new priest came back from his first subscription
hunt. “Oh, ryebuck,” said he. “From Mr. O’Mal-
ley I got a quid, and half a quid from his good
lad}', and a dollar from this one,” and so on
through the list. “Ah,” said the old bishop, shak-
ing his head, “Father Connelly has been teaching
you, I see.”
Died at the convent, Nicholson-street,
Melbourne, on the same day as John Mad-
den, a nun who was born in the same year
as he. Madden was a student at St. Pat-
rick’s College when Mary Borgia McGinnis
became the first boarder at the Nicholson-
street Convent, and she was professed as
Sister Mary in the first year of Madden’s
practice at the Bar. They made a remark-
able contrast in contemporaries.
The John Madden who was a whole-
hearted patron of the prize-ring, who was
something of a tough nut himself with 4oz.
gloves, and who had often gone in and
refereed a mill, was almost forgotten in the
titled Chief Justice of Victoria; nevertheless
the early predilection continually cropped
out. Again and again the C.J. showed
fellow feeling for an accused who had been
guilty of a little readiness with his dfikes.
and in all his public moralisings the Chief
never wowserised about pugilism. John
Madden had no delusions about his superla-
tive merits as a lawyer. His private hobby
was carpentering, and when raised to the
Bench he said to a tableful of friends: “I
have always a sneaking suspicion that I
would have done better behind the bench —
with a mallet.”

was Professor of Agricultural Economics at
the Government Rural College, in South
Japan. His fellow-immigrant, Mineichi
Miyata, who is to give the new subject a
start in the N. S. Wales secondary schools
(North Sydney and Fort-street at first), is
also a graduate of the Imperial University,
where he won a foreign-language teacher’s
ticket.
“Snort”: Strange that the death of plain-
clothes man Davis (B. 28/2/TS) should have
happened at almost the same time as Clifford
Mars Miller’s successful appeal against a
conviction in Sydney. C. M. Miller was the
“young squattocrat” whom Davis charged
with insulting behavior 20 years ago, when
snobbish Panton, P.M., sided with the “well-
connected” defendant and attempted to
squash the presumptuous constable. Davis,
as The Bulletin v par. stated, took his case
to the Supreme Court and won an order for
a re-hearing, but the costs of his legal pro-
ceedings were a blight on his life. Clif.
Mars Miller turned up again in Melbourne
lately, and maybe the news of his triumphant
reappearance helped to hasten pobr Davis’s
end.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Re John
Batman and Port Phillip (B. 14/3/T8).
Alexander Wilson romances for the benefit of
the Historical Society when he alleges that
Batman did not come to Port Phillip in
1835. The sure answer to Wilson’s assertion
is that John Pascoe Fawkner never denied
that Batman had got in before him. In the
Cornwall Chronicle of June 13, 1835, is a
paragraph announcing the return of John
Batman from Port Phillip, where he had
secured 500,000 acres of land, and is it not
in cold type that he rushed into the big
parlor of the Cornwall Inn, Launceston,
kept then (1835) by John Pascoe Fawkner,
and, raising his arms aloft, declared himself
the biggest landowner in the world?
Fawkner never claimed to be the founder of
Victoria, which he would have done had he
preceded Batman to 'Port Phillip; but he
did claim —and the claim has never been dis-
puted—to be the founder of Melbourne.
M.L. Minister for Justice Tom Wilford,
who makes good jokes and laughs immod-
erately at them, comes, nevertheless, from
Quaker stock. His
chief claim to local
fame rests in a
frank, unblushing
audacity and air of
good-humored con-
descension towards
black-beetles and
Providence. That
chin of his is the uni-
versal property of
all men who butt
their way in and get
off with a bone at the
finish. His innermost
yearning is to achieve
a reason for being
addressed as Sir
Thomas —to sit in
some high place on
a spacious chair,
where, decorated
with ornate milli-
nery, he might wave
a fat white hand in
acknowledgment of
admiring obeisances.
The wig incidental
to the Speakership
of the House of
Reps, would fit him
to a hair. His fine,
round voice would
treat the supplication
to the Almighty with
which they start the
play over yonder
TOM WILFORD,
recently appointed
Minister of Justice,
Maoriland.
with generous unctuousness. And giving re-
spectful consideration to Thomas’s chin, this
paper feels disposed to expect that it will
happen.
Eric B. Nelson, lately dead in France,
met the Beast on the Doorstep as an elec-
trical engineer in the Postal Department
(Sydney). He got his commission after
training at the Laverton (Vic.) Flying
School, then went to England and grew his
“wings” last July.*. Formerly a lieutenant
in the Engineers (Sydney), during the first
five months of the Big Eclipse he helped to
run the South Head searchlights. Incident-
ally he was the 82nd North Sydney Church
of England Grammar boy to make the Big
Sacrifice. He went out at 26.
Big Bill Cameron, who has received the
N. S- Wales National’ party’s selection for
the Upper Hunter seat about to be vacated
by Abbott, M.L.A., is a doughty lad, both
with his hands and his cricket bat. He was
in the S. African war, and used to be rather
famous for throwing out sparks of wisdom
and otherwise. On a wet and weary outpost
they were without rations, and naturally got
talking about the alleviating circumstances
of life, including food, tobacco, beer and
girls. “Ah,” said Bill with emotion, “there’s
one thing about the good old Backbloeks. If
a man’s got a side-saddle he is never short
of a girl!”

was nine men in one morning. A Hobart
ancient assures me that he saw this artist
account for a batch of 11. One of them was

from the gallows. He was a politician.

neering firm. Born
in 1840, at Ros-

was turning out

for the field. Later

to secure the neces-

while on that trip

direct service of

borough. In the
early ’eighties John
Walker dropped
out and a co. was
“ TEXAS " GREEN,

House's last little com-

presently formed with Harrington as
managing director. Besides building up a
fine reputation for Walkers’ products, this
fine old citizen did splendid work for Mary-
borough, and a whole string of institutions
are due to his energies. Hubert Harrington,
of Sydney Crown Law Office, is the only
surviving son.
“Kola”: Ted Tapp, manager of Yan-
cannia, away back in the far nor’-west of
N. S. Wales, has gone to meet Bill, with
all hands from the station. The run
having been sold by Shaw Bros, to Syd
Kidman, Ted said to two of the men
that it was a good chance to go to the war.
“Righto,” was the reply, “if you go, too.”
“I’m willing,” said Tapp, and so said all the
others. Thus 13 set out from Yancannia —
including three half-castes and one full black.
A dozen rode on through Broken Hill to
Adelaide, were all passed, and reached Egypt;
but there two of them were stopped—for old
age or something. They are at work behind
the lines. The rest are out with the Mounted
Rifles near Jerusalem, Most of them were
born in the saddle, and they are all great
bushmen.
“T. 0.1.” : Apropos the blown-out objection
that the N. S. Wales Judges’ Retirement
Bill should not apply to the present holders
of office. When, nearly 60 years ago, the Tas-
manian Parliament passed the Anti-State
Aid to Religions Bill, the Queen, under
advice, disallowed it on the ground that it
would be unfair to cut out the Ministers
then in receipt of Government salaries.
Parliament amended the measure, allowing
the salaries to remain for the life of the
clergymen then on the list. In N. S. Wales
the promoters of a similar measure profited
by the lesson, and in their Act (1863) they
allowed the salaries to remain. After 55
years one man is still collecting the ancient
screw.

THE NEED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They drove the God from His supernal tower.
No more He shone resplendent in the day;
Was left no hint of Him in dappled flower,
No hopeful star along- the weary way.
Yet man cleaves still to God, in secret cries
A blessing out of Heaven on the eyes
Of one rose-tinted babe that smiling lies.
Fierce hunter Science stalks the moonless past,
An Alp minute as atomy, a grain
Like Andes, cryptic, cavernous, and va^t,
He searches inconceivably in vain.
No rest for God is here, yet man, with wild
Uplift of soul, to Him is reconciled
That He may warm the white cheA of a child.
To fortify our doubt we slowly trace
First causes out, till God and all we lose
In measureless minutiae of space
Where stir the primal pulses in the ooze;
But as when first the cave-born baby cooed,
By wee, soft hands and joyous prattle wooed,
Man, godless, loathes his sheer ineptitude.
Because to-day I am so weak, so blind;
Because to-morrow nerveless as the clod
That hides me I may lie; in wave and wind,
In rose, and ray, and cloud, I set a God
Of love unforced, Who, in the swirling run
Of dread perplexities, where life is spun,
May guard thee ever, sweet, my lovely one!
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

*This includes “premiums purchasing paid-up policies
allocated from cash bonuses not drawn.”
iThis includes “cash bonuses”—£l7,22l for 1911,
£19,612 for 1912, £22,770 for 1913, £28,317 for
1914, £33,687 tor 1915, £38,472 for 1916, and
£44,167 for 1917.
Until 1914 the bonus employed in buying
paid-up insurance with included with the
revenue from single premiums; since then
the amount has been shown separately:—
Total Bonus Bonus applied to
Declared. Purchase Insurance.
1914 .. .. £28,317 £26,979
1915 .. .. 33,687 31,995
1916 .. .. 38,472 37,036
1917 .. .. 44,107 42,521
Thus only 3 p.c. of the bonus for last year
was called for in cash; the policy-holders
preferred to buy additional insurance with
the other 97 p.c. It shows that enthusiastic
managing-director George Crowley has in-
spired his clients with his own confidence in
the office.
It is a very human office. It even talks
plain,, household Anglo-Australian in its
directors’ reports, and The Bulletin won’t
be a bit surprised if some day the City
Mutual says “damn” in the middle of
its most formal announcements. In addition
to the usual life-insurance benefits it offers
(1) “an arrangement whereby the society
pays the fees incurred by any of its mem-
bers seeking hospital attention”; and (2)
“the concession of full payment of the policy
moneys and bonuses in the case of a member
losing the use of his limbs or eyes or becom-
ing otherwise totally incapacitated.” This
liberality may have been the cause of a
little trouble that the office has fallen into.
The directors explain it: —-
Some of the provisions have been suspended.in the
Case of newly-issued war policies. To grant the hos-
pital and accident privileges in the case of soldiers
on active service would be manifestly unjust to the
non-combatant policy-holders without a very heavy
loading. This the directors were disinclined to
impose.
The elimination of the concessions referred to has
been accepted without demur by incoming policy-
holders without exception and has met with no
opposition from any of the State Governments of
Australia in respect of policies Issued to civil
servants.
The sole exception has been the Commonwealth
Government. It is, the directors trust, not too much
to say that the Prime Minister’s knowledge of the
limitations of a life office is not superior to that of
medical and actuarial experts. But Mr. W. M.
Hughes thought otherwise; for on learning of the
society’s intentions in this matter the City Mutual was
immediately consigned to life insurance oblivion
without the formality of being called on for an
explanation, by having its name struck off the list of
Commonwealth approved life offices. Various remon-
strances addressed to the Prime Minister on this
subject since August last have been simply, received.
It is a monstrous thing that it should be in
the power of a Prime Minister to take action
of this sort without informing the office im-
plicated of the charges against it .and giving
it an opportunity to answer them. In this
case there is the very serious suspicion that
no action would have been taken if the City
Mutual had not been thought of by a good
many people as more or less Irish and R.C.
For the City Mutual to cut out some of
its benefits from new policies issued to men
going on active service is as proper a thing
as for other offices to demand from 5 to 10
p.c. extra premium. That they should take
this, action is no discredit to either. On the
contrary, if any offiefe went ahead as if there
was no war, insuring men who were just
stepping aboard a troopship at the old rates
of premium, there would be good reason for
calling it up to show cause why it should
not be struck off the list of approved offices.
Of course the action of the Government
has been used by the agents of other offices
to discredit it. An office that isn’t good
enough for the Commonwealth Government
may reasonably be represented by a rival
agent as not good enough for the citizen ,of
the Commonwealth. There is no reason to
suspect that it is not as sound as the A.M.P.
It is of course much smaller: while the
AM.P. has 37 millions of funds, the City
Mutual has only just passed the million
mark. But slow progress and solidity mean
almost the same thing in life insurance. You
can show rapid progress, as the big American
offices did a few years ago, by spending a lot
of money to get the business. The Equitable,
in the days when the “gilt-spurred rooster”
King O’Malley was one of its agents, spent
in Australia in some years over 300 p.c. of
its receipts. The City Mutual has gone
slowly; and its expense rate has been kept
reasonably low. Assume that old (renewal)
business (including the management of in-
vestments) cost 7 p.c. and single-premium
business 3 p.c., then the new business cost
74 p.c. There are very few offices which
show better results than that —especially
small offices, where over-head charges, are apt
to be heavy. And in this case there would
be an excuse for an expense rate a bit above
the average, because the office looks after the
little fish in the matter of investments. Last
year the interest earnings were £52,333,
which was equal to £5 7s. 3d. p.c. on the
mean of the funds, exclusive of the contin-
gency reserve—a remarkable rate, especially
considering that 21 p.c. of the total funds
are in War Loan and other Government
stock. It is largely this exceptional rate of
earnings that allows the entirely uncon-
ventional City Mutual to pay bonuses which
make the bigger offices quite uneasy at times.

Dear, dear, what a dreadful time we’re hav-
ing ! And, while a few people are making
fortunes, behind them and protecting them
stands a Government which has built no
ships to carry food to the Allies, which actu-
ally stood in the way when the Broken Hill
Prop, proposed to start shipbuilding, which
destroyed all possibility of Conscription and
has now watched recruiting gradually shrivel
to next-to-nothing. Seriously, what can
be made of all this? And isn’t it
time that the business men of the
Commonwealth Parliament who are loyal
to the Allies sat down together and
thought about it, not as members of this
party or that, but as citizens? When a tor-
pedo strikes a ship and there is great danger
of an awful disaster, you can’t conceive of
a sane chief-officer inquiring of the second-
officer whether he voted last general election
for the Ins or the Outs before asking him
to get to work. Yet that insanity is what
the Commonwealth Parliament is guilty of.

In five years net liquid assets have increased
by £211,440. Meantime* however, £26,000
of new capital has been got in. (The .cap-
ital has 1 gone from £204,000 to £281,000;
but £51,000 was transferred from reserves.)
Deduct £26,000 from £211,440, and you get
£185.440 as the more or less liquid assets
accumulated by the co. in five years. Mean-
time. certainly, there have been some changes
in the fleet. The Kapunda, Sydney and
Brisbane have been 501d—6205 tons the lot —
and the Woolgar (3051 tons) has been added.
Not merely the profit is in this £185.440;
that includes tim cash received on the sales
in excess of the amount paid for the new
ship and for other property ashore and
afloat. How much of this £185,440 is really
undivided profit, and how much of it will be
mopped up in building up the fleet to its old
strength this paper can't say. According to
the books the steamships and other non-
liquid assets are worth only £lB,OOO less
now than they were five years ago. Actu-
ally, no doubt, the 16,056 tons of shipping
which the co. has now (exclusive of tugs,
lighters and other bric-h-brac) is worth more
than the old 19,210 tons; and still it might
bite £45,000 or more out of the accumula-
tions to replace the vanished 3200 tons.
Even so, however, the co.’s recent experience
has b'een very fat. You can put it that,
besides recently paying 6 p.c. on prefs. and
10 p.c. on well-\jiatered ordinaries, the co.
has made net very nearly as much more, dur-
ing the past five years. In the past three
years alone the record has even better.

*Subject to remarks hereunder.
Those are as astonishing as any figures issued
by an insurance concern in Aftstralia. The
co. had barely made a modest beginning on a
capital of a few thousands when the war-
thunder sounded. The chairmap and moving
spirit was S. W. Copley,' a Perth man who
had been taking chances most of his life.
With what looks like extraordinary perspi-
cacity he saw that war offered big chances
for a venturesome underwriter in London,
and he soon had a ticket' in his pocket. He has
stayed in England ever since, and the general
manager now has. his office there. Mean-
time business has been extended to eVery
State in Australia, and agencies have been
established in various parts, mainly in con-
nection with shipping risks. The resulting
figures are remarkable, and would, of course,
be practically impossible but for the mari-
time-war conditions :
Net. Xoss Expense
Premiums. Losses. Expenses. Rate. Rate.
1913.. £1,790 £874 1.2 p.c. 48.8 p.c.
1914.. 3.519 770 3,429 21.8 „ 97.4 „
1(915.. 52,061 17,424 16,074 33.4 „ 30.8 „
1916.. 180,716 116,763 40,118 64.6 „ 22.2 „
1917.. 727,868 522,217 27,095 71.7 „ 3.7 „
The nature of the business is pretty well in-
dicated by the expense rate: to get this pre-
mium income of £727.868 and to manage the
business has cost only £25,851.
But since the premium income has in-
creased during the year by over half a million
it follows almost as a matter of course that
the unexpired risks at the end of the year
Were much greater than at the end of the
previous year; which means that a large
but at present unknown proportion of the
£183,8;v2 shown as tentative profit may yet
be. called for to meet claims. Recognising
this, the directors have transferred from this
and the balance brought forward a, total of
£200,000 to a reserve for unexpired risks and
contingencies. If as much as that should be
required to meet claims arising out of last
year’s business, then obviously no real profit
at all was made. The directors did not issue
their accounts till a few weeks ago (the
auditors’ report is dated February 19) : so,
nearly eight months after the accounts closed,
they should have known pretty well how the
business had panned out. Even so, how-
ever, it does not follow that they reckoned
£200,000 would be wanted ; they call their re-
serve a fund for contingencies as well as
unexpired risks. The risks having outgrown
the capital, it w«s decided during the year to
issue another 160,000 shares; this makes
400,000 of 10s. all paid joss. The new
shares do not, however, carry dividend for
the past year. Even now the funds are
much smaller than those of most cos. in pro-
portion to risks; but when the war is over
the income (and of course the risks) will
probably come down with a run. One; can
get some idea of the inflation of insurance
premiums from this: before the war insur-
ance of silver-lead bullion cost approxi-
mately 6s. per cent. The present cost is
approximately £5 12s.
The balance-sheet figures are not much to
go by because of the large amount of uncom-
pleted business. There are, except for a
trifle of furniture, etc., only three lines —
mortgages.and investments. £133.094; sundry
debtors (agents’ balances), £312,317; cash
(less sundry, £77.898. Interest
earnings during the vear were £s27s—more
than enough to pay the dividend. No con-
clusion of much value as to the future can
be formed upon the present accounts: one
can tell neither the amount of ordinary busi-
ness the office has nor what part of the
above-mentioned £200.000 it is likely to hang
on to. But this paper won’t be surprised if
the co. comes out of the war with undivided
profit at least equal to the present capital. It
is a gamble, of course.

This co. deserves a lot of good luck. It was
formed 21 years ago primarily to work an
electrolytic process evolved by E. A.
Ashcroft, the B. H. Prop.’s electrical engi-
neer ; and incidentally the Central B. H. mine
was bought to supply the ore. Ashcroft wife
a few years ahead of his time, and, partly
because the mine didn’t bulk according to
sample, the works, upon which a heap of
money was spent at Cockle Creek, were a
failure, and the co. was on its bfeam ends.
But it had more than good financial back-
ing : it paid well for brains and got them,
and it set to work to slowly evolve a new
process for the recovery of everything the
ore contained. Incidentally, when every
other Broken Hill co. was pursuing the Ger-
man ore-buyers, it struggled against tremen-
dous odds to make the metal business Brit-
ish. When it could no longer decently ask
for more capital itself it side-stepped the
difficulty by promoting and guaranteeing
another co. to smelt zinc concentrates in
England; and' when other managers failed
to make a do of that co.’s works at Seaton
Carew, it sent one of its own young Aus-
tralian metallurgists, J. C. Moulden, to take
charge, and he is there still. A couple of
years ago, being now affluent, it bought out
the Seaton Carew co.; and of the £134,204
of profit shown in the last line of the table
as written off (this, by the way, is addi-
tional to the usual amortization —£32,946
charged to working costs), £55,201 was
spent on duplicating the works, so that they
may treat 30,000 tons of concentrates a year. ’
Also, having the money, it added to its
superphosphate plant at Cockle Creek
(£27,025) and to its acid plant (£36,053)
and put in a refinery to separate its silver
and lead (£23,990). All these things it has
done during the past year out of profits,
thus making the best possible use, from the
national point of view, of the big prices
which the war has given to metals. And it
hasn’t finished yet: arid as long as the policy
of its management is what it has been
thus far, it probably never will be finished.
For instance, it uses a very big producer
gas plant at Cockle Creek, and, since it there
extracts a very large quantity of tar, it is
now at work to obtain from the tar a fuel
oil to be used in the refinery. Now also it is
going back to its electrolytic first-love;
general-manager Courtney reports
The initial difficulties in connection with the pro-
duction of electrolytic zincs from oxide were over-
come, and it is expected that the small experimental
plant will produce sufficient zinc for future refinery
requirements. Over eight tons of high-grade zinc
have recently been produced, and consideration can
now shortly be given to the treatment of the large
tonnage of slag available at the works.
There are approximately 400,000 tons of
slags, and probably the concentrates from
which they have been produced have aver-
aged 9 p.c. zinc and somewhere about 60 p.c.
lead. As the latter has been practically all
extracted, zinc must constitute a big per-
centage of that 400,000 tons. Until now
this zinc has been regarded as lost.
Really what Australia lias done in zinc-
recovery during the past 20 years is magni-
ficent; and the. Sulphide Corp. from first to
last has led the way. Until about 20 years
ago the zinc in the sulphides was simply ft
bugbear. The object of the processes in use
was to get the lead and silver away from
the zinc and the non-metallic gangue, Then
the flotation processes came along to get the
zinc out of these “tailings.” The first (lead-
mill) process last year at this mine got out
60.4 p.c. of the silver and 78.7 p.c. of the
lead ; and 10.1 p.c. of the zinc remained in
this concentrate. Now the zinc section does
more than get out a zinc concentrate. It
saves another 4 p.c. of the silver and another
5 p.c. of the lead. In the zinc concentrate it
saves 81 p.c. of the zinc in the original ore.
In the three lots of concentrates are con-
tained all but 8.4 p.c. of the silver. 5.6 p.c.
of the lead and 7.6 p.c. of the zinc. Of course
they are not all where they ought to be: in
other words, there is still some zinc in the
silver-lead concentrates and some silver and
lead in the zinc concentrates —say an aver-
age of 10 p.c. zinc in the former and 1240 z.
silver (27 p.c. of the total) and 5.7 p.c. lead
(10.1 p.c. of the total) in the zincs. But
these are not gone for ever. So far as the
