The Central mine hasn't been doing much
development work since the war started,
though owing to the unsettled upper work-
ings it has had to drive a good many new
levels; but it still has 1,692,000 tons of ore
in reserve down to the 1300 ft. level; and
the 1400 ft. will be tapped this current year.
llow much this will add it is not much use
guessing. Last year's mine extraction was
167.210 tons; the previous year's 14,782 tons
more. Add to the value of the mine
£705,000 net of assets other than mine and
works (approximately half of this is War
Loan and cash), a valuable custom-smelt-
ing connection (though it is doubtful whether
there is much profit in it at present, in spite
of what look like heavy charges), a growing
superphosphate 'business, the English zinc
works and an organisation of which any co.
in the world might be proud, and you get
approximately the value of the concern. To
express it in figures is a different and difficult
matter. There are 1,200.000 shares, half
pref. and half ordinary.
Talking at the annual meeting of the
Sulphide Corporation in London chair-
man Ivintore said that they still had 10
years’ ore reserves at last year’s rate of ex-
traction. “We are not very sanguine,” he
added, “of any large developments below our
present levels,” though “some further bodies
will doubtless be found to supplement our re-
serves during that period.” x\lso
With a view to setting at rest the conflicting
opinions that arc? held as to the continuance of pav-
atile shoots of ore at greater depth along the Broken
Hill lode, we understand that a new survey of all
workings on the lode is shortly to he made by the
N. S. Wales Government, which will in connection
with this survey carry out also deep-boring opera-
tions that will be of great interest to the whole
field.
Geological-surveyor Andrews, a first-rate
man, has been at Broken Hill on this very big
job, and has thoroughly interested the techni-
cal men of the companies’ staffs in the work.
Incidentally the Sulphide Corporation is
looking well ahead. After mentioning the
additions to the super, plant, etc., at Cockle
Creek, the chairman said
In this and in other directions which are under
consideration we wish to see our Cockle Creek works
become an important centre of chemical industry in
Australia, and to develop there an industrial side to
our business which, when in the fulness of time
our mine ceases to be productive, will enable us to
continue a useful and profitable existence.
* * » *
C.S.A., Cobar (N.S.W.), has entered
upon a fairly big venture at Ivaudos. on
the Mudgee line, where upwards of £lO,OOO
is going into a copper-refining plant. The
crude product will arrive via Dubbo-Merry-
goen-Mudgee, and a hundred men will be
on the premises from the start. Ivandos,
which didn’t exist a few years ago. lias
rapidly become the centre of much of the
West’s mining activity. The N.S.W.
Cement, Lime and Coal Co. has practically
built the place, having got down on a big
slice of freehold early and cut it up into
township allotments. These were readily
taken up at good figures. The co. lias offered
to light the whole show with electricity.
Work has been hindered by the late arrival
of the imported plant, but the second unit
has now been put in position and is under
steam. Argument is meanwhile proceeding
over the Railway Department’s refusal to
provide a staff for the place.
The Ivandos works of the N.S.W. Cement
Co. have been making a dust for about 18
months, though for some time the output was
limited. Part of the plant was from
Krupp’s, and being on the water when the
war started was detained for many months.
Ivandos is on the Mudgee railway line, about
50 miles beyond the Portland works. Four
raw materials are required in cement-making
—limestone, shale, coal and (in much lesser
quantity) gypsum. At these Australian-
owned works the shale and coal are under-
foot, and the limestone is brought about 24
miles over rough country by an aerial tram
of very simple construction, which is work-
ing quite satisfactorily. The coal is appar-
ently the Lithgow seam, and, so far as ex-
plored, is quite as thick and good as it is at
Lithgow. The limestone and shale are
ground and thoroughly mixed, and on this
'operation much depends. The mixture is
tthen fed into long revolving furnaces —there
ere two of them —slightly tilted, and pow-
dered coal is blown in. The interior makes
::i wiery good imitation of the hottest corner of
;IHide's. The mixture, on the point of chemi-
•cal fusion, comes out as clinker. This is
then ground, with about fcwt. of to
the ton, and the result is cement of as good
quality as Australia ever imported.
Cobar Copper, Ltd., originally Young Aus-
tralian and Mount Pleasant, was wiped off
the slate recently, when the ieases (except-
ing Mt. Pleasant, which was forfeited some
time ago), plant, water supply, etc u, . were
nracticallv given away for about ioUU to tne
!SSe Sopper Tribute Co Thus ends
another chapter in the long story ot Am
tralian mines used by London sharks, etc.,
to take down the Englishman. What cash
was put up in this case was wasted and
shareholders never had a run for then
money. It is rumored that the syndicate in-
tends spending between two and three thou-
sand on prospecting, etc., as soon as the
leases are transferred, and no doubt .it will
make a far better job of it than the English
co., which from the outset did the maddest
things that Cobar ever laughed at.
The crude platinum obtained at Fifield
(N.8.W.) is realising Bs. per dwt. The
best clean-up has been 840 z., valued at iOS,
from .10 loads; but the average yield is
about 4dwt. platinum with ,dwt. gold. In
the old lead at Fifield, worked years ago,
some small nuggets were obtained, but
generally both gold and platinum were m fine
grains. * The crude platinum was really an
alloy of iridium, a metal more valuable even
than platinum.

The plant is just as good as the heap of

* Everton (Vic.) molybdenite propositions,
offered to Melbourne, get more attention
because the B. FJL Prop, lias taken an option
over half a dozen blocks. The country is
quartzite and granite, and the locality has
a mining history that goes back to the days
of the Ovens gold rush. Then two of the
old hands tried the formation for gold, but
onlv struck spangles of molybdenite and let
go. because the Lands Department advised
that the minei'al had no value. The present-
day prospectors are Frank Moore and a pub-
keeper, who got on to the track of the old
fossickers and lured insurance-man Williams
into the gamble. Now the field is dotted
with more pegs than gum-trees. The hold-
ings include the Everton, East Everton and
North Everton. These have . 400 shares
apiece and blink across the railway at the
B. II Prop.’s option blocks and at the modest
150 shares of the Central Everton. Vendors
haven’t been more modest than usual.; they
have mopped up half the issues. V> hether
Swinburne will sanction this division of
spoil is not known. Anyway, Collins-street
isn’t on fire because of the
Things in the alluvial line at Ararat
(Vic.) are not cheerful. Justice Powers has
had to settle a row over the cutting up of
the 44 hours that in future is to mean a
week's work in a Victorian mine. When that
trouble was squared, Toole and Bryant,
for the New and the South Langi, had to
think out how panelling was to go on at all.
There is a mild suspicion that the South
wanted to slow down. Jim Mackay’s friends
could see that such a policy would put the
pot on so far as New Langi was concerned.
So things are to go on as usual. That means
that Hebbard’s plan of driving north and
south from the two New. Langi shafts isn t
to be swerved from even if six months’ calls
have to be faced. Meantime Toole will be
out after men, and as pumping will continue
the wash will be the better for waiting. His
latest sampling gives 21dwt. to the Langi
gutter wash and 80dwt. to the Cathcart lead.
Handled on a big scale, and with the dirt
dry, wash of that grade will turn out trumps.
The crux of everything is plenty of panel-
lers, but they are as scarce as sovereigns.
Lower down the gutter South Langi isn’t
better off than New Langi; but the crowd is
backing Bryant’s luck, which in the past has
always been a reliable amount.
Martin Hannah, Victorian M.L.A., mourn-
fully pointing out to an indifferent House
the mounting cost of arsenic, told some
pretty good news to shareholders in at least
one struggling mine. Hannah complained
that arsenic had crept up from £l2 to £47
per ton since the outbreak of war, and
lately had made a dash to £BO. A day or
two 'later South German shares were once
more quoted, after a long absence from the
list, the old Maldon concern being now well
on the way to become a producer of arsenic.

“H. B. Perth” on a Westralian fe-shuffle:
H. S. King, the new Surveyor-General and head' of
the Westralian Lands Dept., was the man who intro-
duced business-completed-while-you-wait methods to
the Mines Department and thereby fairly staggered
people who had grown accustomed to the slow un-
winding of red tape that distinguishes most Govern-
ment officers. M. J. Calanchini, who takes over the
Mines Under-Secretaryship, has been King’s under-
study for many years —ever since he left Kalgoorlie—
and has shown himself a good administrator and
warden. Both of these men have had to refuse, on
the reports of the technical advisers to the Govern-
ment and the mining inspectors, impossible demands
for subsidies and assistance from the hangers-on of
mining, and it is from such and those with an axe
to grind that the agitation for a “practical man’’ has
arisen. The specific instances given of “unsympa-
thetic treatment” by King’s detractors are unfortunate
for them, as they deal with matters administered by
totally different departments—water supply, taxa-
tion, etc. The Department has in Alex. Mont-
gomery a highly-capable State mining-engineer, a
competent geological staff and numerous mining
inspectors to attend to the practical side of its
duties. The secretary should be a practical admin-
istrator. Recently it was proposed to shift Greenard,
the mines inspector whom the Kalgoorlie Trades
Hall wants to make Under-Secretary for Mines, to
Coolgardie. On representations from the people
who are now advocating his removal from
Kalgoorlie he was allowed to remain, because
it was claimed that his knowledge of the
mines and work in connection with their
ventilation made him irreplaceable. Now they
want to put him in an official job in Perth ! It
will be a matter of surprise if the Government
yields to this clamor; but the incident is a fine
example of how Labor bodies can be made use of as
a catspaw to advance personal ambition.
The blister on Nullagine (W.A.) turns
out to be £BOOO due under a bill of sale held
by the fat men wbo financed things for a
while. So the shareholder hasn’t more than
the ghost of a chance of getting a div. out
of the assets. J. .T. Hunter’s final report on
the mine shows that out of No. 1 paddock
13,500 cub. yds. gave 38.1-Joz., and from
No. 2 10,800 cub. yds. yielded 4200 z. gold.
This averages 2s. 3d. per cub. yd., but as
costs were 2s. Bd. the co. could only drift.
Labor was paid 10s. Bd, per day, but even
then the co. could not maintain an efficient
team. What killed the show was not poor
fuel or want of water, but short supply of
labor and heavy stones in the drift. Over
25 p.c. of the material had to be removed by
hand. The nozzle never had a chance. So
Nullagine passes into the hands of the re-
ceiver.
North Mt. Farrell (Tas.) has just in-
stalled its new gas-power plant (OTOli.p.)
and everything is working well. The smaller
silver-lead shows in the Speck are marking
time, the smelting combine only treating
enough ore to enable them to just keep going.
Some Hobart people interested in the shows
are talking of forming their own smelting co.,
and have been looking at possible sites at
Burnie and Devouport.
The well-known Ballarat metallurgical
firm of Edwards intends to give the old
C'urtin-Davis group of mines in the N.
Dundas district (Tas.) a trial. This is con-
sequent on an examination by Major
Lockyer and others some time ago. At the
West Curtin-Davis there is a continuous
lode from 2ft. to 3ft. wide heavily charged
with copper and arsenical pyrites. A bulk
test of GO tons will shortly be made at
Ballarat.
* * * *
Another six mouths’ delving into the
schist at Lyell Blocks (Tas.) has brought
forth 447 tons ore, averaging 6 p.c. copper
and soz. silver. The lot was worth £3 Bs. Id.
per ton. or a total of £1522; but as the
development account shows £6035 spent, it
is a luxury to do mining of this sort. Man-
ager Carroll has got together another 300
tons which promises over 9 p.c. copper, and
he i*eckons to get other shipments at fre-
quent intervals this year. Even so, it’s a
poor game to carry on mining this _ way.
Therefore the plan of drilling to prove if the
three small ore bodies at 13Q0ft. are likely
to make into anything of promise, as well
as to test other untouched portions of the
claim, is the sanest move made for years.

Chris Hanlon is the latest mining engi-
neer, assayer, metallurgist and geologist to
have a go at Mount Jasper (Tas.). He is
about the tenth man called in to get things,
straight at the much-talked-of show, and as
“he thinks highly of the value of our mining
properties and of the way in which both the
copper and the silver-lead lodes are develop-
ing,’’ the Board has a new idea. This is to
part with the Mt. Stewart leases, allowing
some generous people to form a co. with
£lOO,OOO capital “to work the ground on a
large scale.” In view of the past history of
Mt. Jasper, no shareholder could be such an
ass as to frighten off any band of enthusiasts
who might want to take on the job. The capi-
tal to be put to the credit of working account
isn’t disclosed; but the Federal authorities
will see that it "is a fair proportion. Mt.
Jasper’s 2354 £2O shares are now paid to
£l7 6s. 6d., making a paid capital of £40.615.
Liabilities total £506, and £25 is all that is
left in the cashbox.

The value of minerals produced in the
Speck last year, £1,584.390, was a record,
thanks to war prices. Blister copper gave
£842,583 and tin ore £427,917. The feature
of the year was the production of 48 tons
of electrolytic zinc worth £1968. Scheelite
wortli £12,230 was also a new entry on
the State’s mineral list.

Tyrconnell, Kingsborough (Q.), last month
crushed 300 tons for a return of 4060 z.,
which is the best yield for some time.
Despite the fact that the mine has been
worked intermittently for the past 40 years
the deepest level is at 265 ft. Most of the
ore reserves are between the 265 ft. and 200 ft.
levels, but the management is sinking the
shaft another 100 ft. The Ilodgkinson gold-
field between 1877 and 1912 produced
154,7480 z. gold from 130,358 tons stone.

Sydney, March 18, 1918.
My Dear Arini,—-
There was a big audience in Iler Majesty’s
to hear the statement of the lied Cross execu-
tive. Lady Helen, in mourning for a second
brother —the war hits hard —made an elo-
quent appeal which had as text: "Our best is
not good enough.” (I wonder if that’s why
the maimed boys at George’s Heights Hospi-
tal, a mile from any tram, have to hobble
over the distance —some of it a vile track —as
best they can.) The Davidson lady, who
reads hei* notes, lifted her silvery voice to
advertise the great rally on April 26. On
the stage, J. J. Cohen, E. B. Harkness and
A. L. Blythe, joint hon. secretaries, were
shrouded in silence till the hist, of the trio
spoke up for finance. The big man rolled
out with sonorous tones the letter from
veteran Sam McCaughey. It bristled with
golden promise. The Squire of Yanco offers
to repeat his Australia Day donation —
£lO,OOO, payable in 10 monthly instalments.
The French-Australian League, with its
usual unselfishness, is organising a big effort
for Australia Bed Cross Day. With charac-
teristic modesty, Mrs. Playoust (president)
and hon. sec. Mrs. Jewett sat uneasily on the
edge of steps in Her Majesty’s Theatre at. the
big rally on Friday afternoon. They were
steering for a better place, but an usher soon
put an end to that!
Saturday was as good a time as any for
the new Governor to visit the new Zoo, on
which his Ex.’s advisers and their predeces-
sors spent a surreptitious hundred thousand
or so in order to Win the War. You remember
the big shingled refreshment-rooms and other
quarters that ate up a few of the thousands V
Well, having fulfilled their purpose by giving
somebody the work of building them, they
now continue to be a public benefactor by
requiring somebody to pull them to pieces.
After about 32 months’ use it was found that
somebody had forgotten the drainage.
There was little to-do when his Ex.’s party
arrived. usual, the politician has col-
lected whatever limelight the Zoo can supply,
and it was Fred Flowers, President of the
Council and chairman of the Taronga Park
trust, who welcomed his Ex., Lady David-
son and the two girls—the latter physically
not much credit to the Newfoundland climate
—and showed them round. They saw the
youngest lion in Australia (really, he isn’t
much of a credit to our climate). But Pansy
wasn’t there.
I wonder if you’d like a pet boa-con-
strictor? A nice, plump boa-constrictor, a
dozen feet long—they’re rarely more, I’m
sorry to say—and weighing as much as a
small ox? They are very cheap this season.
Because Hamburg, the wild-animal exchange
of the world, is out of bounds, beasts of all
sorts are, so to speak, piling up in the
Asiatic and S. American "and African ports
which the dealers frequent; and you can get
a pet boa-constrictor for £3, Mr. Le Souef
tells me. Hamburg so completely had the
wild-animal business in its hands that if the
Sydney or Melbourne Zoo wanted a North
Australian specimen it was actually cheaper
to buy the thing in Hamburg than in Port
Darwin.
In spite of a soft collar, a boiled shirt and
a light sac suit. Governor Davidson had to
mop his perspiring brow at the Journalists’
lunch the other noon. In the hazy warmth
of a cigarette-smoked atmosphere, his Ex.
gave some precarious arguments to back his
claim to belong to the brotherhood. He had
once, he mentioned, sat at a similar func-
tion between two editors, one of whom,
the editor of the pro-Ally Providence Jour-
nal (U.S.A.), considered one of the most
daring pressmen in America, was an Aus-
tralian. Though playfully complaining that
his wife had told his best stories on the pre-
vious day, the genial speaker contrived to
toss very good copy down the long table.
The dreary and asphyxiating old Muni-
cipal Library goes to the scrapheap in the
scheme for renovating and lighting the Q. V.
Markets in which they were housed. On
Monday afternoon Joynton Smith, standing
on a small rostrum, formally handed over to
the People their new possession. It is on the
same floor as the former sarcophagus, but at
the Market-street end of the building.
The People will hardly credit their luck
when they see the spacious premises where
Chief Librarian Bertie reigns over the ebb
and flow of about 26,000 volumes. For the
festive opening great bowls of flowers
bloomed on every ledge, and tall pot-plants
—permanent boarders—fluttered in the breeze
from widely-opened windows. The news-
paper reading-room is a floor higher. Above
—you mount a handsome staircase of
Queensland maple to reach it—-are the offi-
cial rooms where the catalogues will be kept
up to date and old books re-b_ound. An inno-
vation long needed is the Children’s Library,
where no grown-up, save the lady librarians,
dare intrude.
At the opening of the Society of Women
Painters the Minister for Education sup-
plied the oratory while those present stole
side-long glances at the worst and best of
the exhibits. Amongst the limited black-
and-white section is the not-for-sale work
of Ruby Winckler, dealing largely with the
adventures of Ulysses. This was understood
to be an American commission. Kate
Thynne, a Queenslander, signs two strong
oils—“An English Farmer” and “An Italian
Model.” Alice Norton (Mrs. Allan Stephens),
a Sydney woman, recalls her recent residence
in Westralia in “Wild Flowers” and a coast
scene. In pastel Stella Macnab strikes an
original note in "Peer Gynt in the Hall of
the Troll King,” and show’s, besides, an
"Impression of the Operating-theatre” which
is more suggestive of a cathedral choir and
heavenly wings than nasty anaesthetics. But
the society will have to take its courage in
both hands and throw out the worthless if
it w'ants to be taken seriously.
The Munro Fergusons sampled a Phil. Pop
on Saturday night, being seated in the
Eastern Gallery, with Haskett Smith on
duty in the background. In the distance
conductor Bradley’s white waistcoat was
presently lost to view’ in the frenzy of his
swallow-tails as they urged the fiddlers on.
Wynifred Carter’s harp w r as one of the even-
ing’s attractions. Lili Sharp hit the Town
Hall rafters with impressive topuotes, while
her Rafalewski kept the accompaniment in
its proper place. Bryce and Mowat Carter
(violin and ’cello) and tenors and basses
plunged neck-deep in the chorus, with
feminine voices to add to the glad noises.
Mrs. Tom Willsallen, now in England, has
lost a son ahd a daughter within a few’
W’eeks. The former collapsed after a hope-
less operation in U.S.A.. as you’ll remember.
The latter, Daisy, widow of Lt.-Colonel Ber-
tram W atts, D. 5.0.. died the other day in
London. Heart failure. Poor Watts was
killed in France before his firstborn opened
its eyes on this troubled earth. The Gunne-
dah family hasn’t seen much silver-lining of
late.
Amongst the new members of the Most
Excellent Order of the Empire is Miss Kate
Egan, one of the all-the-year-round heads in
the Red Cross packing department. As regu-
larly as 9 a.m. this indefatigable woman
steps into her niche in tall Warwick Build-
ings. All day and every day except Sunday
she superintends the sorting' and the packing
of the comforts that help our w’ounded.
There’s no doubt about the services of most
of those who are in the lower classes of the
new “distinction.” But so far as the upper
classes are concerned, the civilian’s best hope
will no doubt, as usual, lie in serving the
Empire by making a lot of money out of its
needs.
This little Judith
Fletcher photo, of
Elsie Middleton
shows one of the
younge r pettied
artists who are
pushing into the
open. Though the
Women Painters’
Exhibition has been
too easy-going, it
contains this year
the wmi’k of many
who deserve honor-
able mention. Miss
Middleton is one of
them. A pupil of
Sid Long, Julian
Ashton and Dattilo
Rub b o, she has
worked hard in
black and white and other mediums, and in
a quiet studio at Hornsby has recently
finished some of the oils that attract atten-
tion in the Women’s show.
Ex-Mayor Meagher has got a consolation
cheque for £l5O5 —the goods were handed
over by his successor, Joynton Smith, last
week. The money has been in hand for some
months, but apparently the time w 7 as not
ripe. The strenuous person, who just missed
being great, lost his seat in Parliament and
the Speaker’s w’ig for standing out- as a Con-
scriptionist; hence the token of practical sym-
pathy. To Mrs. Meagher went a gold wrist-
let watch set in a glitter of diamonds.
Maoriland Joe Ward’s friend, Owen Cox,
of Birt and Co., and of goodness knows how
many other shipping concerns, is among the
first half-dozen in Australia to get one of the
new “Empire” knighthoods. Presumably for
helping Hughes solve the shipping problem.
Some years ago a mild and gentlemanly-
looking young man, who enthused on the
subject of the Bush Brotherhood and looked
nice a very nice parson in embryo—his name
was Frank Lamb —used to round _up
stragglers for the Brothers’ Sydney drawing-
room meetings. In his business hours he was
a senior clerk in Dalgety’s. As one of the
lion, majors who do a useful work on the Red
Cross Commissioners’ staff abroad, he now
pins the order C.B.E. (a new war-time
decoration) on his esteemed coat lapel.
Lieut.'-Commander Jackson, R.N., is dead.
Sydney will remember that, according to his
colleagues, he did some smart work in cap-
turing the German Komet in New Guinea
waters. He was one of the younger sailors
on this station, and so small and youthful in
appearance that at 30 he might easily have
passed for a middy.
Lady Davidson
has allowed her nom-
ination as president
of the Society of
Women Painters.
The vice-president is
Mrs. F. W. Parsons,
whose water-colors in
the present show in-
elude some charming
impressions of our
coast scenery. A
sister of Florence
Fuller—more widely
known because she
has roamed the world
TWonY
1 arsons spent ne
TZ
Melbourne Gallery
classes in the days when Folingsby was direc-
tor, she occasionally wandered on the Inky
Way—chiefly disguised in verse for McKin-
ley’s Punch. At one time she was an exhibi-
tor in the R. A. Society’s shows, but other
interests have kept her too busy tb devote
much time to her brush. The founding of
the Women Painters’ Society, however, sent
her to her studio to literally lend a hand.
She’s an active member also of the Arts and
Crafts Society. I send you with this a stern
photo, of a usually-smiling countenance.
The Assembly Hall in the Education
Building was thick with knitting-needles the
other afternoon. Some of the early birds
are supposed to have caught front chairs an
hour before opening time. The bait was
Lady Davidson’s story of Red Cross work in
Newfoundland. The heroine, in dove-grey
touched with flecks of black, read her notes
and gave a vivid story of the fisher-folk on
icebound St. John’s. New A.D.C. Paine,
lately A.1.F., wrestled successfully with a
window that had locked its jaws, while the
Red Cross dames purred round the Lady in
Grey.
A son of Justice Bring leaves shortly for
the Great Uproar with artillery. This is
one of the last lots to get some technical
training in Australia under the new edict.
How popular that is I’m not allowed to tell
you—it might overwhelm the Defence De-
partment with recruits. Mrs. Pring was
president of the committee which hurriedly
got up a good little amateur show in St.
James’s Hall under the eye of Myee Kenyon.
A big house—the chief artilleryman at
Liverpool brought his departing ’forcement to
swell the applause. Cash to a comforts fund
for the smart-looking lads.
The manager of the Asiatic Petroleum Co.
in Tientsin is a Queenslander —Norman
Leslie, a Rhodes scholar, who went from
Oxford to oil. An Australian who recently
came across the Bananalander said, “Why
oil?” Leslie explained that the co. had years
ago decided to test the worth of ’Varsity
training in business. Hence the Oxford or
Cambridge heads of the concern. “And,
strange as it may appear,” the ex-Rhodes
man added, “the co. is making money !”
There’s to be a ball in the Town Hall on
April 3, with a bridge party, to help the
6th and 7th Light Horse Comforts Fund.
The Cricket Ground carnival to help the
Alexandra Hospital for sick kiddies is fixed
for March 22. And there are so many sick
kiddies to help just now.
The War Chest Depot' in Elizabeth-street
had a visit from Lady jDaviason the other
morning. Captain Paine, who treads in the
sub-regal shadow on these occasions, is a
plump, well-set-up young man, ouite new to
the job. There’s joy in the tents of the
Susies, by the way, at Mrs. Mary Antill’s
war decoration. The hon. superintendent,
who has stuck to her task since the War
Chest was the size of a thimble, is smiling
over the top of a ton of congratulations.
Mary Stevens, the eldest of the musical
Dawes Point family, has promised to wear
orange-blossoms for Lieut. B. W. Millier, of
the Ist 'Wellington (M.L.) Regiment. The
soldier-boy, who is home from the Front
after two years’ fighting, is in the Maori-
land Public Service.
After dinner recently on one of H.M.
cruisers on the China station, the gramo-
phone was let loose. "Possibly the best
record,” says an Australian woman who was
a guest, “was that of Rosina Buckman in
‘The Tales of Hoffman.’ ‘lsn’t that a
splendid record?’ said a Gold Lace. ‘You
can’t even drown the Australian voice! That
record, with the instrument, was with me
through the Battle of Jutland in a sub-
marine, and it was submerged—well, a very
long time—and suffered many of the casual-
ties of war. But your soprano still sings like
a lark.’ ”
Sylvester Browne’s boy writes concerning
roads between Longreach and the southern
towns. “Mostly bad and generally worse.”
he says; “but I will have the best mate in
the world to take home to Julia Creek with
me.” She’s Monica Little, only surviving
child of Frank Little and his wife, of
Albion, Brisbane. Her brother, a pal of
young Browne’s in the Light Horse, was
killed in France. Of the four Browne bro-
thers, one fell in Gallipoli, where Miss
Little’s champion also v did his bit. There’s
another, a surgeon-major, on service with
the A.M.C., and the last is a lieut. in the
Irish Guards. They are nephews of the late
Rolf Boldrewood.
Verbrugghen is not back from the U.S.A.
yet, and the name of the chosen has not
leaked out. When it does, local talent will
have a chance to read the references of the
new singing master for the Conservatorium.
Meanwhile Alfred Hill takes the absent
director’s baton.
The Rose Bay golfers are organising a big
Red Cross corroboree on their velvet la\Vns.
E. P. Simpson is chairing it.
An occasional passport can still be
snatched from the dragon who guards the
gangway of outgoing steamers. A busy Red
Cross girl, Molly Merivale, leaves shortly for
Egypt to lend a hand to those who help our
veterans in that flybitten kingdom.
There’s money in the rosebush. H. Y.
Braddon took the best chair at the War
Chest Flower Shop’s financial wash-up. In
15 months a profit of about £l2OO had come
mostly from the sale of posies. There’s a
side-line of toothsome produce, and you can
help the cause by stepping into 126 a Pitt-
street and leaving the change for a pot of
honey.
“Australia Jane’s” note from Brisbane :
The four new baby clinics supplied by the State
were opened last week. Premier Ryan’s wife un-
locked the one in the Valley and was presented by
the Home Sec. with a pretty brooch as a souvenir.
Similar dainty mementoes were pinned to Mesdames
Huxham, Theodore and Fihelly for their services in
blessing the others. Miss Chatfield, who has man-
aged the Diamantina Sanatorium for some time, has
been appointed supervisor of the institutions. She
has gone to Sydney to see how the Ma State handles
its babes.
E. B. Wareliam, the popular manager •of the
Adelaide Steamship Co. here, goes to a better billet
in your city next month. An only son was killed
at Gallipoli.
Dorothy Buchanan, a pretty Southport girl, is to
be married very shortly to Jack Laird, son of the
Mathew Lairds. The bride is to be decorated with
about £5OO worth of diamonds, a gift from father-
in-law elect.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

THE STROUD PIANOLA
Superior to all players of other
make, regardless of price.
The Stroud Pianola provides you with all the power of musical
expression, all the varied musical effects which the skilled pianist’s
talented fingers can attain.
But the Pianola does not stop at endowing you with a tech-
nique that is equal to the difficulties of the most tremendous musical
compositions ever written for the piano; it gives you also simple,
accurate guidance that enables you to employ your new-found power
with a subtle artistry that few amateur musicians ever develop.
Even though you have never played any instrument, the
Metro-style, a device exclusive with the Pianola, is a clear and easily-
followed path to perfect interpretation. It is the master’s prompt-
ings and teachings simplified so you may understand without
tedious study.
The beautiful tone shading—the power of varied emphasis
which gives the Pianola touch such a startling semblance to that
of human fingers—is due to another of the exclusive Pianola devices
—the Themodist.
Many other important features and improvements have given
the Pianola a superiority over every other instrument of the Player
type, that is recognised and endorsed by practically every great
musician of the present generation.
Yet the Stroud is not an expensive instrument.

Melbourne, March 18, 191 S.
My Dear Yarrte, —
Our Boys' Day, on Friday, produced a
record crush. Collins-street was impassable
except by the most valiant fighters, and as
none but foot traffic was allowed down the
Block, it was one huge bazaar. Big posters
by Victorian artists hung high between the
big grey buildings. A fine bit of color by
Mclnnes decorated the Swanston-Collins-
street entrance. Somewhere in the mtdee the
Dominion stall was showing beautiful Cana-
dian goods, and crowds tussled for each article
as at a bargain-sale. Manson engineered
another popular kiosk with Maori ware for
sale. The Americans (with Mrs. Wallace
helping) were near the Equitable, and the
Rennies ran a fisli-o and poultry stall at the
Royal Bank corner. The Stock Exchange
had a spinning-jenny outside, and members
dashed up occasionally to buy the whole
“turn,” just to encourage the multitude.
Indoors, theatrical stars were singing and
demanding money instead of encores. Scott’s
shut out the intrusive feminine rafflers and
only allowed the sale of boomerangs. Menzies’
gave itself over to a big cafe chantant. The
lounge was a # tea-party, and was packed with
women chattering and munching and listen-
ing to other women trying to sing. A few
belated males looked in over the outermost
Best Hats. The day was stormy and the
electrics were lowered. Captain Waters’s
monocle gleamed in a lonely way among
oceans of feminine frills. Colonel Syd Sands
bravely faced the fearful odds. Rupert
Clarke watched from the safety of the smok-
ing-room. Russell Gabb gave an expensive
Royal Worcester tea-set, and his wife raffled
it for over £5O.
Gambling is now an inevitable feature of a
Button Day. Patriots have got beyond hav-
ing roulette wheels indoors. They at last
expect to find old Spinning Jenny at the kerb.
On Friday there were dozens of huge red
wheels set about at the corners of blocks,
with returned soldiers shouting as they man-
ipulated the rakes and drew in the cash.
_ One of the Our Boys’ Day events was a
big theatrical entertainment' at the Royal.
Mrs. Tallis was organiser. The usual pro-
gramme went on in the front of the stage,
but behind that were bridge and poker tables.
Sassiety “came on” to possess these, and
showed incidentally the value of stage make-
up. Sassiety’s very best face-enamel looked
green. Also its dresses were badly hung for
stage effect, and viewed from the house
there was not an effective rig in the lot. The
usual sweets and programme 'vendors were
there. They mostly stood at the back of the
circle and shouted, remarks to each other
while the stars sang or recited.
On Friday night some Naice people hopped
valiantly for Our Boys’ Fund. Mrs. Scott,
Miss Shepherd and Mrs. Green arranged
the dance at the Francatelli, and two
of the ladies guarded the head of the stairs
so that only those, who had been sold tickets
might enter. The O’Hara wedding revellers
seem to have cauaht the secret of perpetual
motion. They were at the dance (as they are
et-enywhere) in one complete mob, circling
dizzily together. Even the bride and her
’groom came out of honeymoon oblivion on
Friday and joined the peripatetic grooms-
men and maids. Nan wore a sheath of gold
tissue and some frills of white tulle. The
Coopers put in a very tired two hours, and
then plodded home to bed, like Gray’s
ploughman : they had been working hard all
day for the cause. Hugh Ward blew in late
with manager Westmacott. Mrs. Buesst, in
blue ninon and some startlingly valuable old
lace, came with sister-Mrs. Kerr.
Returned soldiers provided a fine musical
programme, and so'me crack pupils of Mrs.
Green gave solo dances. Margo Lester and
Jean Clack were particularly noticeable for
their beautiful hair and armor-plated toes.
There was auctioning, of course. Banana-
landers down for the races are noticeably
good marks for bushrangers. A corner lot
of them offered pounds for single bottles of
wine. The air of Xaiceness was so strained
that some mere Bohemians who had blown in
hoping for frivolity were driven away crushed
and saddened. Even a jealous fight was stopped
in the first round.
A big flurry at the Oriental last Wednes-
day to gather funds for Our Boys’ Day in-
cluded a two-step, a concert and the popular
gambles. Dorothy Low arranged the musical
programme and played song accompani-
ments. Claude Hall, the Perpetual Bach-
elor, lifted up his artistic tenor, and Uulcie
Donald and Vida Sutton also made music.
The Oriental opened its heart completely
and even gave up the billiard-room to the
raiders.
It was while in Mount St. Evin’s Hospital
that Mrs. Arthur Woolcott received the tid-
ings that the royal George had been pleased
to make her a commander of “the Most
Excellent Order of the British Empire.”
The handsome Button Day autocrat has been
troubled by her throat for some time and is
now recovering from an operation.
While Guido Baracclii, the plutocratic
socialist, is “doing time” in Melbourne Gaol,
the lady who recently became Mrs. Guido
is amusing herself in Sydney. She retired
from the chorus of the “Aladdin” panto,
before that show left Melbourne.
Dear, dear, one ought to be more careful
these times. Last week I told you that
Howard Hadley, the examiner for the Lon-
don musical bodies, who had just given us
a recital, was a “young Englishman.” A
friend of his, remembering that it’s war-
time, implores me to make it “middle-aged.”
He is 43. The time has come when, if you
mention a man as having done sotnething
except kill somebody, it is advisable to fol-
low the old police-court plan: “John Jones
(27) and Thomas Smith (41) were charged
with,” etcetera.
The late Franklin Peterson’s son Frank
drilled back into the social whirlpools this
week. The young man has been in hospital
for an operation, and had a convalescence
near Mansfield. He wandered through the
big crush of the Menzies’ Friday party look-
ing for tender sympathy.
A conspicuous series of dress fancies were
flung at Melbourne race-frivollers by Mrs.
Harper. Willie married a big, handsome
Californian girl, and brought her home to
Australia some months ago. She has
paraded wonderful New York costumes—
more conspicuous because the wearer is of an
athletic type and amazonian in size.
The Stanleys have sent pasteboards abroad
for a garden party on Wednesday, March 20.
Meantime, fashionables who starve for such
affairs are putting up a daily prayer for fine
weather.
Mrs. Henry Cornish gave a Braille party
last week and invited the blind returned sol-
diers. Tea, music and supper.
Three of the bhoys tripped off London-
wards last week. Jim Alison of Sydney
goes to see his son Seppy, who has been
released after being a prisoner in Hunland
for many months; “Doc” Masters floats
round on some A.M.C. stunt; and evergreen
Billy Bayles, who wears his 50-odd sunlmers
jauntily and is now’ a confirmed Londoner,
hies, home to see if his Jermyn-street flat
still stands and to take up the baton of the
special constable again.
There is no pleasing everybody. The
poor old Austral Salon has been closed by
the Board of Health because of its insuf-
ficient exits; yet for long past there are
plenty .of women who would gladly have
closed it because of its insufficient entrances.
. Dr. Mary Delprat, one of a trio of clever
sisters, is just back from America. The Del-
prats can’t be idle, so Dr. Mary lectured for
a while, at the medical school of Berkeley
University, in San Francisco, and practised
in Rochester, N.Y.
The cold, aloof Fliclie, who stalks rigidly
through the duties of French Consul, has
just presented an armful of French books to
the library of the Alliance Francaise.
Twenty-one of the volumes are by Frederic
Masson, the Napoleonic historian.
The sudden passing of Mrs. Gibney Roche,
at Malvern, was a distressful affair. Her
marriage with land-agent Roche was only a
recent happening and her death followed a
visit to a “health” resort in the high hills,
where shfe acquired scarlet fever. Julia
Doyle she was, the only daughter of wine-
merchant Lawrence Doyle, who retired some
years ago with a weighty bank balance and
went to dwell among the elect of Mercer-
road.
Mrs. Edward Argyle, dead at South Yarra,
full of years and honor, was the relict of a
hard-living old early bird of the Kyneton
district, who settled on the Campaspe in the
days When that region was the wildest of
backblocks. Three sons keep the family
name well before the public—Reginald, ex-
M.L.A. for Dalhousie, who works the soil in
the old spot in the intervals of trying to get
back into Parliament; Charles, who pursues
Costs in the ancient township; and medical
Lieut.-Col. Stanley Argyle, who took the
first N-ray outfit from Australia to the
Egyptian desert. There was a fourth son,
but he was drowned when a little chap.
Algernon Wakefield, once Melbourne’s best-
known shopwalker, left Melbourne with the
10th Battalion and went a-missing last
May. Now it is known that he trod his
final steps upon the path of glory at Bulle-
court, on the third of that magnificent month.
The tawny-headed Wakefield was probably
the subject of more chaff than any young
man of his years in Melbourne during his
this-way-madaming career at Ball and
Welch’s; but the stuff was there all right—
all wool and a yard wide, t
Fashionables have just found out that head-
master Franklin, of Melbourne Grammar,
whose enlistment provoked many lioorays long,
long ago, hasn't left yet. How could 7*e go
without a commission? So he has been held
here month after month, month after month,
and is only now saying his farewells. Per-
haps you can explain the ways of the
Defence Department. I can’t.
Dr. Kent Hughes is coming home. He
had booked for Petrograd, but no one knows
if he ever got into the blood-stained capital
or argued with the Bolsheviks. Son Billy,
an original Anzac, and now a major on brief
leave in Australia, hopes to sight dad before
sailing-time.
Sister Adelaide’s intrusion :
Surg.-Col. W. T. Hayward, who has been two and
a half years with the A.1.F., is homeward-bound on
transport duty. Lieut.-Col. Butler and Lieut. Napier
Birks are also returning—the former invalided and
the latter on duty.
The Trench Comforts store in Bowman’s Arcade
has made in its first year a profit of over £3OOO.
The total expenses were only £3OO, and that includes
rent for one of the shops—the other is given rent-
free by Keith Bowman.
The F. H. Snows have had word that their Wil-
fred, who is with the R.F.C., has gained the
D.S.O. He got the Military Cross some time ago.
George Williams, one of the founders of the Com-
mercial Travellers’ Assoc., died on Wednesday. He
was born in Brighton (Eng.) 76 years ago and came
to this State in ’64.
The State War Council won’t allow any nonsense
with regard to the regulations drafted for the
control of patriotic funds. M. G. Schluter, of
Greenock, has been fined for his share in holding
sports to raise money for the Red Cross without the
authority of the War Council. The maximum
penalty is £5O, but Schluter was let off lightly, as
the law hasn’t yet soaked in.
News has come of the death of Major H. N. Hen-
wood, one of the most promising of the younger
men in the Education Department, who was Secre-
tary to the Director when he enlisted in 1914, He
joined the ranks and won his commission at Gal-
lipoli.
On the vexed question of soldiers and drink, which
a Senate Committee is inquiring into, J. J. Virgo put
the matter concisely. When he sees a few drunken
men in khaki he “thinks of the millions who are
sober.” If the authorities don’t want to see Billjim
slip the best thing to do is to give him occupation
as quickly as possible after his discharge and see
that he can live in decent surroundings. A step in
the right direction is being made by building the
hostel at the rear of the Cheer-Up Hut. For this
the State War Council is footing the bill, and a
Button Day at the end of the month will meet part of
the cost of furnishing.
There’s a whole sermon on disillusionment in the
mere omission of a figure from No. 2 “Honor Board”
at the Adelaide Railway Station. Board No. 1 is
headed “1914-191—”; room was left to add one
figure when the “Cease Fire” sounds. But No. 2
board opens “1914-19—,” with room for two figures.
If a No. 3 has to go.up we may not chance anything
beyond “1914—.”
Pauline Curran, one of Hobart’s beauty
girls, is engaged to Captain Fitzgerald, the
new genthelp on Governor Newdigate’s staff.
He is fresh from the Big Smoke and has
also been well seasoned in war smoke. Paul-
ine is the last of the handsome Curran sis-
ters ; they have all married and flown from
the island.
Nancy Chisholm (Sydney) is to be
married to Ivan Young, son of John, of the
big Victorian clan, who died at Nhill three
months ago. Ivan was wounded in war
aviation and discharged, but has re-enlisted
and is marrying his girl before he returns
to the aerial fray. Nancy is an orphan—
niece of our Bishop Langley. Several of
the Victorian Young clan are journeying to
Sydney for the ceremony.
H. A. Luke, the jolly bubble who directs
the circulation of the Gippsland Mercury,
has just given his son Andrew into the cus-
tody of a popular Melbourne girl, Lois Gil-
lespie. Mrs. Luke is a daughter of J. M.
Gillespie, who is conspicuous in recruiting
circles, whilst her young apostle owns three
stars in the Army Medical Corps.
The Sydney Sketch Club has recently
altered its name and enlarged its scope, be-
coming The Australian Arts Club, with mem-
bers in the other States, and hopes of repre-
senting all the arts and acquiring a club
house of its own before long. Beginning as
a small group of artists who worked during
the day and pooled their half-crowns in order
to pay for a model and a room one evening
a week, it has grown quietly and now in-
cludes some of the best men in three States.
It wall celebrate its transformation by an
exhibition at our Fine Art Society’s rooms on
April 8. The show will be a very interest-
ing one, with the latest work in color and
black-and-white of Norman and Lionel
Lindsay, Carter, Collins, G. V. F. Mann,
George and Alfred Clint, Mclnnes, Heysep,
Shirlow, S. Ure Smith, David Low, Leason.
McNally, Jackson, Bancks, Burton, .Lloyd
Rees and others.
Harold Brodsky, who first dabbled in paint
in these parts, has been exhibiting at a big
water-color show in N’York, and getting very
decent notices.
Artist Ellis Rowan is back from New
Guinea suffering from malaria, but full of
enthusiasm for the country’s flora. “I have
travelled all over the world,” she says, “but
never —never have I seen more beautiful
flowers than in New Guinea.” And she has
pictures of over GO birds painted from life.
Sergeant Penleigh Boyd—no need to intro-
duce "Pen”—has made a number of sketches
of life on the Western Front, which the
British Australasian is going to publish
in London, in book form, with the title of
“Salvage.” A man who has seen some of
the proofs says he got from them an extraor-
dinarily vivid impression of many of the
places made famous by Australian gal-
lantry.
The University Conservatorium of Music
started its 1918 series of concerts this week
with a programme headed by Handel and
ended by Liszt. Bach, Schubert and Chopin
came in between. The programmes of the
20 concerts are almost wholly German; yet
the prospectus invites attention to the
“catholicity of the selection of works.” Prof.
Laver’s idea of the meaning of the word
“catholicity” seems to be “more than some-
what Teutonic.” But in the vocabulary of
his predecessor, Marshall Hall, there was no
such word at all.
The big draw at the Oriental concert was
Guido Cacialli, the basso-baritone of the
Gonsalez Opera Co. Cacialli thundered at
his very best from the romantic music gal-
lery.
Walter Kirby, smooth but chastened-look-
ing, is back in his familiar Melbourne cafes.
At the final recruiting-concert held in the
City Hall, Hobart, the Newdigates vice-
regally demanded that Walter and his
moustache be brought before them to be con-
gratulated on his wonderful singing and
thanked for his recruiting work.

The Old Girls of the Scotchbyterian Col-
lege (Melba is one of ’em) are going to found
a War Scholarship for the daughters of
soldiers and sailors who have fallen or been
incapacitated. The scholarship is to run for
three years, but may be renewed at the end
of that period. Subscriptions to the fund,
from half a crown up, may be left at the
Red Cross Work Room, No. 6 Assembly Hall,
Collins-street. Mrs. Sutherland, of Camber-
well, is hon. sec.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Wart”: Of the Tasmanian blacks only
the East Coast tribe planted the dead; the others all
cremated the lamented and buried the ashes. They
still continued the practice while in captivity on
Flinders Island. When a black felt that he was
S'oing to die he would ask to be carried into the
open, where he could superintend the building of the
funeral pyre. No matter when lie died the others
would not consider he was quite dead until sun-
down. Then they would put him on top of a heap
of logs in an erect position and pack a pile of brush-
wood round until the body was completely hidden.
All the other sick in the crowd would then be
brought out and placed round the pile with the idea
of taking the “devil” out of them. Before sunrise
the pyre was set on Are, and when it was burned
down the ashes were collected, some being sewn up in
kangaroo-skin bags as charms, the rest buried. Very
likely this practice accounts for the scarcity of
aboriginal skeletons compared with skulls —there are
only some ha.lf-dozen complete skeletons known, com-
pared with some 70 skulls. The top-piece was nearly
always saved as a charm. Captain Batman, of the
•'rig Tamar, who took 40 of the blacks to Flinders
Island, states that they brought on board quite a
HIGHLY RECOMMENDED.
CHARIMAN (at the camp ): “I 'ope, Hokes, as yer’ll all vote for our worthy friend 'ere.
' E can tork like a bloomin’ gramerphone, mix it with anyone ’is weight, an’ is
game ter promise yer twice as much as the other bloke. ’E is willin' an’ able to
stoush out anyone as don’t agree with him in his principles as a true-earted. peace-
at-any-price Australian workin’-man."
bushel of old bones, and as the boat began to move
they rattled these together to keep the debbil-debbil
from running off with the ship. Davis states that no
eases of burying weapons with the bodies were
known, but in that he is wrong; two cases are
recorded, one by Braim and the other by Jorgensen.
The latter-—an untrustworthy party, as a rule. I’ll
admit states that Mungo, his black guide, showed
him a cave where there was a pile of large stones with
a spear sticking up from the centre. Mungo explained
that it was the grave of a chief, and objected to its
being tampered with. The spear, he said, was for
the chief to fight with while he was asleep. Braim
states that when it was not convenient to carry off
the dead body to some place of interment, it would
be put into a hollow tree, in as upright a position
as possible, and to preserve it in this position a
spear was stuck through the neck into the wood.
Another spear was left with the dead.

“Bill Bowyang”: Australian buckjump
riders of a few years back are doing their bit. Billy
" a *te, who won the Australian championship from
Lance Slcuthorp in Brisbane about six years back, :’s
horse-breaking for the army in Canada.' Jack Demn-
sey, who went to Egypt with the first Billjims, is
lolling in a remount camp near the Suez Canal, and
Major (“Banjo”) Paterson is his boss. Dave Black,
one of the best horsemen N.Q. ever turned out, was
lighting the Hun in East Africa some months back :
and Jack Goodfellow, of W.Q., is somewhere north
of Jerusalem with the Light Horse.
* * * *
“Bareoo” : Put this down and let no man
cast the eye of suspicion upon it. Forty-three
shearers at Terriek Terriek (Q.), one time owned
by Donald A\ allace, of Carbine fame, shaved 8970
jumbueks during one day in 1916. Jim Ashwood
led the way with 256. About the same time at
Alice Downs 19 men put through 39C0. I believe
the Terriek run was made on the day the shearers
gave their scores to the Patriotic Fund.
“Kalibionba”; “Jimmie Pannikin”. (B.
14/2/’18) has my support when he barracks for the
domestication of native flowers and trees. Not to
mention the kurrajong, which in many ways is the
best of all trees for artificial planting' and responds
quicker to intense cultivation than any other vege-
table I know, 1 can thoroughly recommend the
common grevilleas, known as wax-flower, and the
native fuchsia. When I was a kid I planted one
of these in my father’s garden, where it soon
attained to thrice the size it grows to in the bush,
and through the later winter-time and all the Spring
was covered with adorable coraline blossoms. 1
knew a man, too, who grew a scarlet-flowering correa,
from the Snowies, as an ornamental hedge, and it
was the loveliest thing of the kind that I have seen
in Australia. This correa grows about 4ft. high and
is thick with brightly-shining lanceolate leaves among
which for months the warm-red blossoms “burn in-
sensibly,” as Shelley sings. To come down from
hedges to edges, let me recommend a wee white
heath, whose liliputian bells are almost if not.
quite the first flower of Spring.
FAMILY ECONOMY
LITTLE JOHNNY: “Say, Bill, Father's got a new set of false teeth."
BILL: “do on! Wot will lie do with the old ones?"
LITTLE JOHNNY: “Oh, I s’posc mother'll cut 'em down and make me wear 'em."
“Barcoo”: The Australian station that
nowadays shears the greatest number of sheep in
one shed is Wellshot, on the Barcoo-Thompson
watershed (C.Q.). Three seasons ago the barbers
removed the overcoats from 265,000 jumbueks, filling
5000 bales of greasy wool of the best. Time, 12
weeks. TJhe “run” being- a dry one, the 40 operators
performed on the 60 working days. This station
does its own shearing as against the general custom
of contracting. Twenty years ago it shore 416,000
sheep at the one shed. Sixty men operated. Time,
18 weeks. This was another dry shearing. The
same season Milo (W.Q.) peeled 510,000, but did
it at three sheds.
.* * *
''Quidam” ; Re the policeman-tiy (B.
28/2/’18). I had a par. in The Bulletin some-
where about 1901 concerning the insect, and it started
lemarks about the useful little assassin from pretty
well all over Australia. When I saw him at work
he was on the Crocodile goldfield (Q.), and lie
cleaned out the flies in our camp in about a week,
being the most active and efficient Force I ever
struck. Since then, greatly to my regret, I have
not met him again; but there should be a large
fortune for anyone who can domesticate him and
sell him in large numbers to the residents of the
Blue Mountains (N.S.W.), where there is ample
scope for his energy. He might also be a valuable
addition to the Commonwealth Police Farce.
“Kola”: The man who lately wrote about
dragon-flies overlooked one of their principal failings.
They are hogs on bees. Mention dragon-fly to a
Grampians (Vic.) bee-farmer and watch him jump.
The fly is well known all over the. Grampians. He
finds out the address of an apiary. Then he is
right. He gets fair over it and watches for honey
manufacturers. As soon as one comes out of the
hive the fly gets busy, and soon the bee’s innards are
sucked out. Then he lets the bones fall and watches
for another toiler. A’ fly will seriously reduce a
hive in a very few days. I know one man who had
to shift his hives three times because of dragons.
* * * *
Bad Australian”: The beds of the rivers
flowing east from the Andes almost without exception
are higher than the surrounding country for several
leagues after leaving the mountains. These rivers,
fed by the immense Andean glaciers, have an average
fall of about 3 per cent, where they debouch from
the great cations to the pampa or flat country, and
during the summer months especially, when the
sirocco-like “zonda” blows from the north, the
waters bring down immense quantities of silt.
When the river reaches the flat country, and is no
longer contained by the walls of the eafion, the silt
is precipitated in the form of a long, fan-shaped
| ridge. The main current keeps a channel clear on
| the top of this ridge, and the periodical overflows
• deposit more silt and sludge on either bank. This
gradually raises the ridge, and the river-bed rises
in proportion to the quantity of silt deposited by the
flood. When I saw the longitudinal section of a
proposed railroad which had been located parallel
to the foothills of the Andes, with the bridges
shown on the tops of the ridges instead of at t'hjjj
bottom of the intervening valleys, I felt certain that
the engineer had been mixing his drinks; but that
was my miscalculation,

A PEACEFUL MAN FROM MERRIGOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rowe called himself a neimeful bloke.
He looked it; so we had a drink,
And walked the town. He little spoke.
We had a couple more, l t' ink.
Rowe said they gave him n .dal; for
His peacefulness at Morrigo
Then came a man who glanced a while.
In tin's Rowe some incentive found.
He smote that man with subtle guile,
And left him prone upon the ground.
“No peaceful bloke could stand,” said he,
“The way that josser stared at me."
Then on the station platform sat
A chap regardless of us both,
W ho day-dreamed underneath his hat.
Rowe’s passions stirred. He breathed an oath,
And, setting on the thoughtful gent.,
lie gave his chin an awful dent.
The smitten one in wild surprise
Attempted no expostulation.
Rowe took him then before my eyes,
And with him dusted out the station.
Tn proper wrath “I’d like to know
Who you’re ignorin’ ?” thundered Rowe.

“F.Y.Z.”: Ruminating about flying-foxes
I’m back on the Buna Bay-Yodda roads (Papua) in
a camp where the flat coastal country
begins to join up with the foothills
of the Astrolabe range. These foot-
hills carried a fairly heavy native
population which grew taro, yams and
bananas in profusion, while the flat
forest country was inhabited by an
inconceivable population of flying-
foxes. At evening, as the sun began
to slide behind the range, a vast emi-
gration of the foxes began from the
forest swamps to the banana-laden
highlands. Often we watched them
till long into the night, and as far
as the eye could see, to right and left
and in front, the air was black with
them. By the way, don’t let anyone
ever persuade you to go into a flying-
fox camp to take a pot shot at the
occupants sleeping in the trees. It
was a favorite trick to play on a
newchum years ago in N.Q. As to
the consequences of the first shot—
well, I don’t want to spoil your appe-
tite.
“D. A. Herbert”: “Jim” Carew (B.
28/2/’18) wonders why a crop of wattles springs
up after a fire in a place where there wasn’t a speci-
men before. Well, here’s the reason. The seed of a
wattle is surrounded by a waxy layer impervious to
water. Until that coat is removed or ruptured, and
water is allowed to enter, germination is impossible.
After a fire some of the seeds are slightly charred
without being killed, and these germinate. Others
germinate as a result of the softening of their coats
by alkali in the form of carbonates of soda and potash
from ashes. Filing of seeds and soaking for about
a day in strong sulphuric acid are about the best
methods of getting them to sprout without the
expense and inconvenience of a bush-fire. As to how
the seeds'get on to the virgin ground, ask the birds.
Prof. Ewart of Melbourne University found that seeds
could lie dormant for 60 years and still mature after
filing. The results of some of his experiments are
now health}' young saplings in the Melbourne Botani-
cal Gardens. By the way, the fruit cocky is no
friend of the wattle, because of its habit of harbor-
ing the root-borer, an insect partial to apple-trees.

“Mrs. Lance Rawson”: Talking about
sagacious animals, I knew two in class A. One was
a williamgoat who used to take four small children
to school and back every day. The youngsters rode
him, and he took good care that they kept to their
turns. He was better than a help-maid to the
mother of that family. He carted in the wood and
water, took the clothes to the creek t,o be washed,
and danced to a tin-whistle to please the baby at
night. A circus man who came along looking for lost
horses, saw William and offered £1 for him. The
owner said that he wouldn’t sell the animal for a
gold reef, so the showman, being of an obstinate
nature, stole William. Two years later the circus
MILITARY TACTICS.
“Over the top, cobbers!”
came back to the near-by town, and our boy went
along with his whistle. When the goat turn came
on the lad piped the old tune that William used to
dance to, and in ten seconds there was enough excite-
ment to satisfy the crowd as to whose goat it was, in
spite of his having been clipped and dyed. Next day
the Court gave the boy £25 damages and his goat,
and the circus-man left the town trying to console
himself that he wouldn’t have so much to pay in the
way of income-tax that year. Another pot was a
pig that played hide-and-seek with the youngsters—
and went to school with them. While they were at
lessons he roamed about eating- up all scraps in the play-
ground. If a dog or one of the bigger boys attempted
to interfere with any of his charges he would face
the aggressor, squealing and charging with his mouth
open. 1\ hen he was a small sucker the girls used
to dress him in doll’s clothes and nurse him like a
baby. It was the funniest sight in the world to see
this little black piglet having his bottle in the arms
of a small girl of 10 years of age.
“Pick”: In these days of macadamised
roads shoes are considered as necessary for steers
as for horses. At Bibbenluke, a village 30 miles
from the Vic. border, a regular trade in ox-shoeing
lias brought good money to the local blacksmith.
He uses a crush of his own invention, with a side
winch for hoisting Baldy off his feet during the
operation. His four hocks (Baldy’s, I mean) are
then strapped to rear blocks, and the half-plates
are comfortably clinched on, while the bovine
squirms aloft in vain endeavors to kick the shoer
into the middle of next loading-day.

nest Island and liberated

of ammunition ran out.

“Wart”: “Pick” (B. 21/2/”18) inquires
the name of a pest which is chewing up his cherry-
tree leaves. The pear slug, I expect. The progeni-
tor is a fly which lays its microscopic eggs on the
leaves of cherry, plum, pear and hawthorn trees,
and as soon as the slugs hatch they start to demol-
ish the foliage. The cure is to spray with arsenate
of lead, a quarter of a pound to six gallons of
water, as soon as the first insects appear, repeating
the dose about a month afterwards. If you have
the bad luck to strike a very heavy shower immedi-
ately after a spraying, you may have to repeat the
job. This spray is also the best enemy of codlin-
moth and of caterpillars and other insects tfiat chew
fruit and leaves. The pear slug was introduced to
the Speck about 15 years ago, and for a couple of
years the growers almost gave up in despair; but
with systematic spraying the pest has been com-
pletely got under. Dusting with lime or ashes is
also a remedy. The white-eye is the only bird I
know that will touch the evil-smelling curse, and
for that and other pest-destroying qualifications
should be encouraged by every orchardist. I do
not say that the white-eye does not occasionally make
a mefil of fruit, but anything harder than a ripe
mulberry or grape must be first pierced by some
bird with a stronger bill.

“Azack”: Know this kind of trap for
mice? Cut the top off a kerosene-tin and bore two
holes, one in each of two of the opposite sides,
about two inches from the top. No. 8 fencing-wire
is inserted through one hole, then passed through a
round tin with lid (treacle tin will suit), and
finally through the hole in the other side of the
kerosene-tin, so that the tin on the wire will
revolve easily. Then sink the kerosene receptacle
in the ground, half fill it with water and rub a
little fat on the roller. Lean a board on the side
of the kerosene-tin so that the mice can run up it,
and as soon as they jump on to the roller after
the fat it will revolve and throw them into the
water. As it has been found that many mice
are infected with a disease called favus, which
will also infect man, setting up a species of ring-
worm disease, it would be well, if you handle the
mice caught, to disinfect the hands in a weak solu-
tion of formalin (one part of commercial formalin
in 500 parts of water) and the head and neck with
a weaker solution.
“Billy T.” : I have never met a loafer to
equal Afrat. His mother ran cows, so he could
afford to lie about and take his ease. lie always
declared that he was the original discoverer of the
Native Youth. Iloleman was the first owner of the
reef, it is true, and made all the money
out of it; but Afrat —well, this is his story.
He was lying in the grass under a gum
near, an outcrop. “The quartz was stickin’ up
out o’ the grass ’n’ gum leaves,” said Afrat. “There’d
been rain, ’n’ she was washed clean, ’n’ the gold in
her was fair blazin’. I cud see it, ’n’ I was sayin’
to myself: ‘That’s gold all right’; ’n’ then I’d say:
‘No ’tain’t, it’s just broken glass.’ ‘No,’ I sez, ‘it’s
gold all right.’ ‘Might be a bit iv old copper plate,’
I tells meself; ‘or a brass nob, or somethin'.’
‘Gerrout,’ sez I, ‘it’s gold for certain.’ An’ I’m
wishin’ I wasn’t so tired so’s I could get up ’n’
settle it. Then along comes Iloleman. He was
goin’ t’ have a pitch with me, but, his eye catchin’
the glitter, he slouches over ’n’ knocks a bit off the
reef. ’Struth, it’s gold all right!” Then people
say to Afrat: “Suppose you’ve been mad with your-
self ever since for what you lost?” “Not me!”
yawns Afrat. “No blessed fear ! Look at the trouble
Iloleman’s had worlcin’ her.”

THE GOLDEN GILL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 he brassy skies were turned to blue, the bluest ever
seen;
1 he grey-green nnilga scrub assumed an ultra tint of
green;
lhe breeze that but a day before smelt anything but
sweet
Became as though it passed where blooms were trod
by fairy feet.
’The Plains that stretched north, east and west and
south to God knows where
Had shed their hopeless greys and browns, and rain-
bows shimmered there;
The pad the camel caravans beat hard in single file
Became a pixie avenue—a blossom-bordered aisle.
Brown necks that were of collars free for weeks and
mouths and years
Became abodes for linen white, instead of holes for
beers;
And faces flecked with desert dust were scrubbed
until the skin,
As tough as leather, all but showed the ruddy flesh
within.
'The hopeless stare left sombre eyes, and in its place
there shone
The light which midnight planets spread a silver
beach upon;
The frowns that looked like chronic things were
changed to glowing smiles
That spread across each face and out across the
Plains for miles.
The dusty bottles on the shelves like opals seemed to
gleam;
The hatter’s canine wagged its tail and ceased to
doze and dream ;
The pictures on the bar-room walls that once were
faded prints
Suddenly seemed to resurrect their lights and shades
and tints.
Old Piker even brightened up ; his spit upon the floor
Seemed like unto the jewels that the Queen of Sheba
wore.
And why this metamorphosis, out there amidst the
scrub ?....
The Golden Girl from Sydney-side had come to
Piker’s Pub !

“Jim One” : Any returned Billjim in quest
of a ready-made cattle-run, unafflicted by
rent, rates, taxes, lawyers’ fees or agents’
perks, might be successful if he took a trip
to Amsterdam Island, in the Indian Ocean,
midAvay betAveeu Australia and Good Hope.
This island, on Avhicli the French flag Avas
hoisted by Eure in 1893, when he made a
depot there for shipAvrecked mariners, Avas
discovered by Magellan’s comrades in 1451
(Magellan himself had been killed previously
in tiie Philippines). It derived its name
from the NieuAV Amsterdam, the vessel in
which Van Diemen visited it on a voyage
from Holland to Java some 200 years later.
It contains about 10 square miles, and
abounds in low, thick grass, which makes
excellent food for cattle. Rabbits are re-
ported to exist in one part, Avhile fine fish of
the rock-ccd species and crayfish are plenti-
ful. Fresh water is also, I believe, abun-
dant. In 1880 10 fishermen from Reunion
were residing on the island engaged in curing
fish. Remains of their hut can still be seen.
Cruising round there recently I noticed quite
a respectable herd of cattle of all sizes, from
calves to immense bulls, in good condition,
grazing away contentedly.
