“The Oldest Inhabitant” goes exploring:—
Apropos Sydney Directories (B. 28/2/'’18). The
earliest, published in the ’thirties, were mostly
termed “Almanacs.” In 1839 Macelhouse published
a directory only, which catalogued the best-known set-
tlers at fort Phillip. In 1844-5 Francis Low pub-
lished his “City of Sydney Directory,” a long,
narrow volume of 200 pages, with a number of
illustrations of business places. In 1847 Low
published another of Sydney and district. A feature
of the 1844 book was a “street key,” the .first in
Australia. It showed the public buli,dings—and
the pubs. Thus we find that the Brown Bear was
on the corner of Brown Bear-lane (Lower George-
street), The Crooked Billet on the corner of George
and Queen streets, and the Jew’s Harp on the-corner
of Goulburn and George streets. This was printed
by E. Aleock, Hunter-street. Next came “Ford’s
Sydney Commercial Directory for 1851,” a 6 x 4
book of 156 pages. In 1855 Waugh and Cox pub-
lished a directory of Sydney and suburbs, and 1858
saw the first of “Sands and Kenny’s Commercial and
Sydney Directory,” the pilot-fish of the big book of
to-day. John Sands was a Sydney man; Thomas
Kenny managed in Melbourne. The firm dissolved in
the early ’sixties, Sands keeping Sydney and Kenny
sticking to the South.
«■ * * 4
“Tuan Bongsu”: Just before the war a
companion and I, with a camera, did the
north coast of New Guinea through Dutch
and German territory in a canoe manned by
Pintados. We found that the further avo
travelled from our border-line the more dog-
tooth necklaces the villages possessed. At
last the necklaces becames so numerous that
avo concluded that, as only the four canine
.teeth proper are used, New Guinea must
once have contained millions of dogs—all
the older men of the villages appeared to
possess a necklace apiece. Presently, how-
ever, I had an opportunity of handling one
of the treasures, and then the mystery
vamoosed —the teetli bad no nerve ducts, but
Avere solid ! When Ave reached the first Ger-
man settlement, Ave found that the plantation
store contained boxes full of the porcelain
shams.
“Petrol”: On the Booleroo line, 63 miles
from Port Pine (S.A.), trainloads of Avheat
spoilt by rain and mice were thrown out
along the embankments between Wirrabara
and Booleroo Centre, like so much ballast,
during the time the 1915-16 and 1916-17
Avheat Avas being moved to the seaboard to
make room for this season's grain. More
Avheat was lost during the tAVO seasons on
this line than was gathered in the drought
of 1914-15. Yet the muddling politicians
had the nerve to tell the man on the land
that the loss due to Avet weather and mice
Avas very small! The stacks from Gladstone
to Wilmington showed an average Avaste of
15 per cent., the said stacks being mostly
uncovered and not mouse-proof. Now at
Port Pirie there are 2,500,000-odd bags of
wheat waiting shipment; and the stacks are
falling almost as fast as they are rebuilt.
The scandalous thing is that, though all
this wheat was taken out of the farmer’s
hands, so that he had no control over it
whatever, he has to bear the loss—every
penny of it. Anything more like highway
robbery was never practised by a Govern-
ment smyAvlfpre. >
Lea, insect-expert of Adelaide Museum,
has been exposing the immoral life of the
weevil to the Wheat Commission in S. Aus.
In Lea’s experience the beast lias never
attacked wheat in the field, though he has
seen it do so Avith maize. It seems to prefer
maize to any other grain. The main breed to
go for stored wheat is .the so-called rice Avee-
vil, which can fly freely, and has been found
all about Australia, even expeditions to Cen-
tralia bringing specimens back. The; only
cure for it, in Lea’s opinion, is intense (and
in practice impossible) heating,; or the silo
system, the airless, packed, underneath part
being fatal to ifisect life. Drowning, even
in spirits, seems to be a mere joke to a
Aveevil, which comes up alive and hungry
after hours of submersion.
«■ * * *
“G. Arden Hill”: Blackberry-picking is
noAv an established industry in Illawarra
(N.S.W.) during the couple of months that
the fruit is “in.” Before the season starts
some of the greedy coal cos. invite tenders
for the right to pick on their leases. In other
cases agents for Sydney jam cos. come to
terms with property-owners. Pickers pitch
tents in localities where the vines are most
abundant and accessible, and work from
dawn till dusk. This year the harvest has
been a particularly good one, and the return
to the gatherers is expected to be about
£3OOO.
* * # #
“Tin Pot Bill”: Some time ago various
ancients disputed regarding the oldest tub
afloat in Australasian waters, and the Lady
Barkly, Governor Barkly’s one-time yacht,
was entered for the old buffers’ race. This
fossil has been renovated and done up, and
is iioav the Hina, trading between Nelson
and CollingAVOod (M.L.). She is herring-
gutted , and narrow-chested, and ought, by
the laws of shipbuilding, to turn turtle; but
her record speaks for her seaworthiness, and
in her class she is still hard to beat. A
stranger gets a tlirill when the skipper of
one of these river boats tells all the passen-
gers to go aft Avhile he takes a running butt
at the river bar; if the tub jibs all hands
must run to the bows. In low neap tides the
boat is often toppled over the bar in this
manner.
* * * *
“Eddyson”: ' Long before Wallingford
began his business of getting rich quickly,
Eustace Ball struck Drybone. Ball was a
man of portentous manner and gentlemanly
address. He put up at Kellow’s, and Avhen
a suitable stage in Kellow’s fever of curi-
osity was attained, he admitted, Avith the
utmost candor, that he was a rich investor,
and bad his eye on a great money-making
proposition; but before going further must
have certain rights over some land in Kel-
low’s possession, said rights to be null and
void if Ball’s operations failed to bring
Kellow satisfactory remuneration. “Fact
is,” said Ball, “I am forced to let you in. I
had hoped to work the scheme on my OAvn,
but it strikes me now that the co-operation
of a few substantial men of Drybone Avould
be advantageous. Besides, you hold the abso-
lute essential —-the land.” IvelloAv agreed, and
Quine and Wylow came in on the same
terms. Then Ball let his friends into the
mystery. He had cornered the granite ridge
at Drybone. Drybone granite Avas the most
magnificent granite he had ever seen, and
for 20 years he had specialised on building
material. “The whole world will clamor for
this granite,” said he. “It is superior to the
finest marble for decorative purposes. We’ll
have a railway through from I’eafield. That
Ave will make a mint of money is the only
absolute certainty I Avill admit in this un-
certain world.” Bali had Drybone in a sweat
of expectation. The granite was there all
right, millions of tons of it. . Ball knew all
about granite. Europe and America were
literally clamorous for granite of this sort.
It only remained for Drybone to sell granite
and become preposterously rich. Then Ball
began calling up scraps of capital for inci-
dental expenses, etc. He also began trading
off trifles of interest in the enterprise to
eager inhabitants, always as an act of friend-
ship. In this way he may have got hold of
£5OO. Then he Avent away. A fortnight
later KelloAV received a letter with a Mel-
bourne postmark. It was brief: —
Dear Sir, —Some four years ago, when I was a
poor wanderer, footsore and weary, you threw me
over your slip-panels at Drybone. AVhile I lay
unconscious somebody else stole my last shilling. A
little later a third person stole my dog. I am
pleased to be even with Drybone. Your granite is
not worth a damn. —Eustace Ball.
ANTONIO: A TALE OF R-BEVENGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 scorned John for the dear, dark-eyed,
And pale-faced one so dignified
We met down at Sorrento, where
Mamma had taken us to bide
The passing of the glad New Year.
John had been well enough in town—
A man of dealings, rough and brown —
But seemed too earthly, on the whole,
Beside this one, whose thoughtful frown
Implied immensity of soul.
ilis sweet name was Antonio,
And whispering he told me so,
And that in far, fair Italy
His people ever richer grow,
Of higher social degree.
AVe walked together by the shore,
Undying Jove three times he swore;
We bathed together in the sea ;
Then, when our holiday was o'er,
He bade a fond good-bye to me.
We were to meet again. I knew
Dull days a whole long fortnight through,
And no word came from Love o’ Mine;
But John had written thrice to sue
That 1 just once with him would dine.
1 went, and in a pink-lit room,
With music glad, and bright with bloom,
AVe dined, and at our table gay
Were many happy people whom
We’d met on my dear holiday.
I too might have been happy there,
Forgetting love, and free of care,
But ’mid the napery and flow
Of lights, I saw a face so rare—•
Ah, heart! it was Antonio !
I saw his deep, dark eyes again,
His pale face, and his sudden pain,
And though the rest might happy be
I knew no joy was for us twain,
No jesting ease for him and me.
He haunted me —his raven tress,
His attitudes, his hands, his dress,
Although my eyes were on my plate.
That night John bade, with cutting stress,
Farewell for ever at the gate.
Antonio but once came near
Throughout the meal, but, oh, I fear
To hear those words he spoke again—-
Those awful words breathed in my ear:
“Consomme, broth, or julienne?”
VICTORIA.
S. S.
The Inky Way.
“Third Billjira”: I was witness to this
vindication of art. First Billjim commenced
to read “The Songs They Used to Sing."
After perusing a few lines he paused and
appealed to Second Billjim. “This Henry
Lawson’s a ——. Why don’t ’e say straight
out she’s a ?” Second Billjim, medi-
tatively :* “It’s like this. Bill. [Pause.] It's
same as a dinner. [Pause.] Decent people
like a flash meenu card with a fancy name
in gold paint on it. But you’d like the
waiter t’ push it at yer an’ say, ‘Righto!
Here's yer stew’ !’’
Hodder’s, the London firm that is publish-
ing J. B. Cooper’s “Leg Bail,” a romance of
the early days of adventure in Bass Straits,
wrote in flattering terms to the author about
Australian brain-workers on the Inky Way:
We have been much struck with the general high
standard of MSS. received recently from Australia.
There is undoubtedly a growing school of Australian
literature, and to our mind your people in this
respect are miles ahead of the other colonies.
No doubt due to the splendid encouragement
given to Australian story-writers by the
“great” dailies!
“Norbell” : I wished to register a literary
copyright the other day in Sydney, and went
up to the Registrar-General'S in Queen’s
Square to do it. A young man told me I’d
come to the right shop but the wrong
entrance, and directed me to the big door
facing St. Mary’s. I went there, and another
very affable young man told me that they
kept tl\c copyrights library, but the address
for registration was Warwick House, Hamil-
ton-street. When I got to Warwick House
the liftman, having his doubts about it, oblig-
ingly hoisted me up to the inquiry office on
the first floor. The inquiry clerk explained
courteously that what I really wanted was
the Patents Office, which he said was some-
where in the Customs House. So I went to
Circular Quay. The inquiry clerk at the Quay
was very sarcastic about all the' others, and
told me that the dinkum address was c/o
the Commonwealth Bank. The fever of ex-
ploration was in my blood by now, so I went
uptown once more. One of the Common-
wealth Bank’s shopwalkers directed me to
the fifth floor, and there, camouflaged at the
end of a passage, I found my goal. Next
time I want a Government office I’ll employ
a private D three weeks ahead.
* * * *
Another Inkywayfarer to prove that the
Lewis gun is mightier than either is L.-Cpl.
Jamieson, 14th Battalion. He is a son of the
man who owns the Footscray Advertiser, a
Melbourne suburban rag that had its first
birthday 44 years ago ; and he fingered type
and spiked copy for dad before joining up
nearly three years back. One of his feats was
the leading of relief parties at Messines in
June last, and other daring work in Sep-
tember clinched his hold on a Military
Medal. He has been in the Lewis gun
“suicide club” almost from the day he
reached France after the Evacuation, and
has never suffered a scratch.
Leading publishers have been interviewed
on the state of business in 1917. Without
exception they were agreeably surprised.
Instead of the expected and predicted slump
from shortage of paper and other adverse
war-time conditions there has been a booin.
Rarely has so much poetry been written and
published, and rarely has the average of
merit been so high. Macmillan’s report “a
distinct trend towards works of theology in
the broad sense.” John Lane declares that
the taste of the reading public has wonder-
fully improved since the outbreak of the war.
“First-class fiction, also biographical, histori-
cal and descriptive works, find a better
market,” he says, “than before the Avar, and
novels specialising on the development of
character Avere never in more eager demand.”
* * * *
“Y.Z.” : The Argus on a recent Saturday
published a half-column sketch headed “Phil
May.” It purported to recount the career
of the humorist, and explained lioav he got
work on the Penny Illustrated Papek
and on the Pictorial World. “Then, as
his health Avas giving Avay, he removed to
Australia.” Of his work in Australia not
a word; readers are left to assume that his
career on the English Penny Illustrated
was of sufficient consequence to deserve
mention, whereas his brilliant development
in this country, extending over years, is not
to be spoken of at all. Will Australia ever
grow up? Will she ever be big enough to
be ready in all cases to recognise her own
existence? If she does, it aaIH not be AVith
the consent of the “great” dailies, the Argls
especially.
THE POET’S WIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The poet’s Avife is very good ;
She loves his verse, and tells him so;
She says that when he’s understood
He to the very top will go—
And earn a mint of money, too.
Then, while he seeks with ardor fine
The splendid word, she hustles through:
“I’m looking everywhere for you!
Do come and fasten up the line!’’
She wants her Jack to make his mark,
And let “those other wretches” see
lie has a semblance of the spark
Of inspiration. In conies she.
“Oh, put that horrid pen away,
And come out shopping with me, Jack!
I’ve got to hurry—cannot stay.
A ton of things I need to-day,
Much more than I can carry back.”
He is a literary star
She says. His lightest rhymes enfold
A boon to all mankind that far
Exceeds the worth of pearls and gold.
And when at last he’s in the swing,
And feels, that with a chance he could
Wake all the world, she’ll sharply sing:
“Oh, I say, Jack, you dear old thing.
Do come and split a bit of wood !”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.

COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne. 14th March, 191S.
Tenders for the Supply of Condensed
Milk to the Royal Australian Navy.
1 Uv
TENDERS will be received until l2noon
on Friday, 5th April, 1918, for the
supply of Condensed Milk to the Royal Aus-
tralian Navy until 80th June, 1919.
Tender forms, specifications and any
further narticulars may be obtained on ap-
plication at the Office of the Director of Navy
Contracts, Navy Office, Melbourne; the
Victualling Store Officer, Royal Edward
Victualling Yard, Pyrmont, Sydney; and at
the Naval Staff Offices, Brisbane, Birken-
head (S.A.), Fremantle and Hobart.
Joseph coop:,
Minister of State for the Navy.
WEEDY BOYS AND SICKLY GIRLS.
It is a mistake to think that anaemia is
only a girl’s complaint. Girls show the effect
of it more plainly than boys. Delayed de-
velopment, pale lips,- breathlessness, sick
headaches, and frequent sharp pain call
attention to them. But many boys in their
teens grow thin, weedy, and have pimples on
the face, showing that they have not enough
blood. The anaemic boy is just as likely to
become a victim to consumption as the pale,
breathless girl, with her headaches and worn-
out look. Let him catch cold after cold and
he will lose strength until his health becomes
precarious.
To prevent this serious disaster to those of
the rising generation, let both boys and girls
be given the new blood which Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills are famous for making. Watch
how soOli the appetite returns and how the
languid girl or lazy boy becomes full of
activity and high spirits. Remember that
the boy has to develop, too, if he is to make
a strong and hearty man. Give both of them
a fair chance by starting a course of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills —and, to make sure of
it, take care to ask at shops for Dr. Wil-
liams’. Most chemists and stoi'ekeepers sell
them at 2/9 per box; six boxes 15/3.
A valuable little book on “Building Up the
Blood” will be sent free to any address on
application to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Co., Box 594 G.P.O., Sydney.— Advt.

Thousands of business men who strive for 100 per cent efficiency in the office
are still content with from 40 to 50 per cent efficiency in the bath-room.
The demand for more speed, less muss, less of the tedium of relathering,
rubbing-in, and slow, difficult shaving, and less smart, bite and irritation, has
caused many men to discard successively the mug, the stick and the powder
as they would discard a wasteful, antiquated machine in the factory.
Mennen’s Shaving Cream overcomes every objection to mugs, sticks, and
powders. It is sanitary. It actually softens the beard. It leaves the skin
soft, cool and refreshed. There is not a bite or a smart in a tube of it.
Mennen’s Shaving Cream costs but little more than bar or stick and lasts
longer than powder. A tube, if used according to directions, will deliver a
shave a day for months.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The manager of the Commonwealth Bank
estimates the w 7 ealth of Australia and Tas-
mania at £1,640,000,000, which is grateful
and comforting, like somebody’s cocoa, and
makes the debt look quite reasonable. But
the artificial values created by the scattering
of , loan moneys, paper currency with a
purely local circulation, the artificial values
which it creates while it remains good, the
goodwill of businesses and companies which
is onlv faith and hope and optimism capital-
ised and put on the market, the fancy prices
attached to racehorses and prize dogs and
roosters and public-house licenses—these
and a thousand other things are quite in-
tangible. If any large “conscription of
wealth” blew on the edifice it would double
up < unless the Government was willing to
take out much of its demands in racehorses
and public-house licenses and the goodwill of
land-jobbing concerns at their paper value.
If it wanted real money, and everybody was
selling to raise his share, the Melbourne Cup
winner would be just meat.
NOT THE ALLY HE WAS.
“Time is on our side;"—Early war consolation.
THE FIRST TO VOLUNTHEER: “You see, I’m
getting shorter every day. You’ll have
to hurry if you want to make any use
of me.”
Instead of the returned men being a help
to recruiting, the more of them we get the
w 7 orse the figures are. There is something
radically wrong to produce this result. The
vast majority of these men belong to what
are called the working class —those from
whom the Labor party in politics is drawn.
If that party were represented in the Govern-
ment these men would at least feel that their
rights or w 7 liat they believe to be their rights
were being looked after. The further we go
the more certain it is that we cannot make
a success of the voluntary system under
Party Government. Hughes asks for sugges-
tions to help recruiting. There is only one
suggestion that will carry the movement any
distance: the retirement of the present Min-
istry and the arrival of one that will repre-
sent the whole nation and not something less
than half of it.
Some coincidences coincide too much to be
pleasant. Now 7 that Russia possesses an
alleged Government wffiich has
dispersed the army that was fight-
ing on our side, repudiated its
debts to Britain, admitted the
German tyrants unopposed into
its most valuable provinces, made
a more brutal hash of civilisation
than the Czars did in 100 years,
entered into a treaty with the worst
autocrat on earth, and incurred
a strong suspicion of being a mere
hired Hun agency—now that
these things have happened the
Melbourne Trades Hall and the
“Socialist party” are clamoring
after a passport so that a repre-
sentative may he sent, to a “So-
cialist” conference iu Russia.
What for? Is he to go to an un-
friendly country to study for
future use the disastrous methods
of Bolsheviks? Or to supply
valuable information to Bolshe-
viks and their masters? If, on
the other hand, his mission to
what is practically a ’ Hun de-
pendency is merely the harmless
one of discussing the Brotherhood
of Man, that can surely stand
over for a while. There is no
haste. The Brotherhood of Man
won’t run away.
Again the Vic. Health Board
has shown its occasional likeness
to 71b. of putty. An outbreak of
scarlet fever lately happened at a
greatly-favored health resort in
the hills. At the first signs of
trouble the local hasheries packed
off their sore-throated cases —
back to Melbourne by train. So
far, there have been a couple of
dozen cases of fever among these
returned trippers, with one death.
Yet not a murmur has been
uttered by the Board of Health to
warn prospective visitors against the beauti-
ful plague-spot, nor has there been a line
in the daily press about the scandal. Ap-
parently local vested hash-interests have
managed to keep the matter as quiet as
business demands.
“Where is Mr. Tudor?” the S. M. Herald
asks. Well, the S. M. HeralD ought to
know. He is exactly where it helped to put
him.
The U.S.A. will launch over 220,000 tons
of new' shipping in March—an improvement
on the record for January and February.
That is 31 ships—one per day. On a popu-
lation basis Australia should launch over
11,000 tons of shipping this month, but it is
more likely to launch over 11,000 speeches.
N. S. Wales is the centre of the coal-
mining industry. It supplies not only itself
but to a great extent the rest of Australia.
It is therefore in practical control of the
one industry whose stoppage can para-
lyse the continent. So the I.W.W. person
who poses as a well-meaning anarchist and
a harmless enemy of civilisation in general,
but w T lio is suspected of being very often a
concealed German agent—he and other con-
cealed Hun emissaries naturally concentrate
their attention on the Ma State and in par-
ticular on its coal-miners. The coal person,
who grows more credulous with advancing
years, listens attentively. He doesn’t really
know where the inspiration comes from, and
it isn’t intended that he should. During
1916 and 1917 the N.S.W. coal-miner- has
struck 381 times. In each of these years he
has spent a quarter of his time on strike
while his wife and children went short of
food and clothes. Yet not one really big
grievance can be discerned. No wonder dark
suspicion centres on that influence in the
background.
Hear the vice-president of Melbourne
Trades Hall Council on the subject of the
Federal Government’s commerce-organisation
proposals:—
We listened carefully to the scheme outlined by
Mr. Hughes, and were unable to find any mention of
what advantages (other than what might accrue
generally through increased production) would be
offered to organised labor to secure its co-operation
in the scheme.
Hughes’s scheme may be open to a thousand
objections; but if organised Labor doesn’t
want increased production and general ad-
vantages, it ought to be honest enough to
say so.
The Hun has more luck in the way of
food supplies—cheap food supplies, anyhow 7
—than the Allies. He cleaned out the victuals
of Belgium, Northern France, Serbia and
part of the great wheat-growing land of
Roumania without even leaving his lOL
behind him. He took whatever remained in
Russian Roland and parts beyond, and didn t
give common civility in return. Now he
has occupied Odessa (population 450.000),
where the surplus grain of southern Russia
is stored for export, without any Bolshevik
throwing a peanut at him. The ghost of the
Duke de Richelieu, the creator of Odessa,
THE Pit ELI MIN ARY.
“Wifen I have bled it white, I will carve It
to my liking.”
must have wept in its grave that day, and
Bostopschin, credited with the burning of
Moscow in ISI2, must have looked down —or
up, as the case might be —and asked in
anguish why he wasn’t the man in charge to
set Odessa on fire in 120 places when the
Hun approached. But Britain also gets its
food somahow. For the week ended March
9, N. S. Wales contrived to send away about
553,000 bushels of wheat, leaving only
45,600,000 bushels on hand that it can't get
into motion for want of ships. If the Hughes
Ministry could be entered for a race with the
Great Pyramid, the Pyramid being allowed
10 yards start, the event would be worth
betting on.
At a recent by-election for a Maori seat
in the M.L. Assembly Henare Parata was
challenged as to his views on the liquor ques-
tion. “It’s this way,” he said. “I want a
straight-out issue. If the Government
brings ‘ down a bill for straight-out prohibi-
tion or for .straight-out continuance, 1 will
vote as the case may be. But anyway it will
not be carried.” By the time the congrega-
tion had got that in. the candidate was
over the ranges on his way to address the
next kianga.
Frank Clarke, Bowser's Minister for Rab-
bits, had a few' unhappy moments after
he took on the job. Perhaps the unhappiest
was that which followed the news from
Ouyen, in the Mallee, about the grand open-
ing -of the mighty simultaneous-poisoning
raid fixed for the 4th inst. Four new in-
spectors had been appointed to see to the
operations, farmer-poisoners were employed
at 11s. a day, poison-carts were rolled along
to various chosen spots, and everybody w T as
good and ready to begin work on the Mon-
day morning.' There was only one real
defect. Somebody had overlooked the poison.
Blackburn, captain and V.C., is one of the
candidates for suburban Sturt at the S. Aus.
general election. He is branded Nationalist;
hut it is because he is president of the
Returned Soldiers’ Assoc, that The Bulletin
will be glad to see him top the poll. Nowhere in
the Commonwealth is Defence Department
administration less satisfactory than in S.
Aus., especially in relation to returned
soldiers; and if the men’s president is given
a seat in Parliament they may have a bit
more punch. Nationalist or Laborite, the
Sturt elector who wants to see the returned
men get a fair deal can’t do better than
mark Blackburn down for his (and especi-
ally her) first vote.
A profound lack of enthusiasm is one of
the Vic. Health Board’s chief attributes.
Having decided that sanitary conditions
seem to have nothing to do with the recent
outbreak of infantile paralysis, and after
noting that ‘"if serum were injected in the
early stages the disease might be checked.”
it declared that it “could do nothing further,
as all that was possible had already been
done.” London during the Great Plague
acted in much the same way when disaster
struck it—just said “Gramercy !” or “Odds.
bodikins!” and left the rest to an uncertain
Providence.
“Lou”: If Labor in Vic. doesn't soon
buck up, it will find itself pushed out by the
militant Socialist party. This group, the
result of Tom Mann’s efforts, is at present
a propaganda movement only, but a very
live one. It runs two Sunday-night con-
certs and lectures in the city, a brass band,
an orchestra, Saturday-night dances, a
Sunday-school, tin* weekly Socialist and the
children’s monthly Dawn. With Adela
Pankhurst giving the factory-girls short,
sharp doses of Goslowism at dinner-times,
Scott Bennett lecturing and teaching the
young red flags how to speak, and a member-
ship of close-up a thousand, including poli-
ticians and trade-union officials, the V.S.P.
will find it hard to resist the temptation to
stand its own candidates up at the next
elections. Meanwhile the Labor party pot-
ters along with a honeheaded determination
not to extend its operations beyond a Sun-
day-night meeting in the Gaiety Theatre.
The Bulletin rises to mention once more
that it believes in Sinn Fein. It was
among the first of the believers in
Australia, and it proposes to hang on
so long as there is anything left to
hang for. “Sinn Fein” simply means “Our-
selves Alone.” It is the, whole doctrine
of self-reliance in two words. This paper
has consistently preached that we —our-
selves alone —can build or spin or weave or
otherwise construct everything that we
want, provided this country produces, or can
produce, the raw material. It has even gone
so far as to say that this great gold-produc-
ing land has no need to go crawling to
Britain for loans in gold or otherwise, but
can do its own finance. “Sinn Fein” has
been perverted to signify the driving about
of other people’s cows by night, but it doesn’t
of necessity have any relation to driven
cows. If Australia doesn’t go in for a lot
more Sinn Fein it will be bankrupt one day.
day. j

A PRAYER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When heat lias gone from the blood and friendship
dies,
When hopes and fears are fixed on the hereafter,
And neither rending pain nor rousing laughter
Can stir the soul behind my rheumy eyes ;
When wisdom only speaks through toothless gums,
And in the world no light is worth the winning;
When every cause seems lost from the beginning,
And ghosts of failure grin behind the drums;
When warning signs at every turn are hung,
And all God’s visible world has lost its beauty ;
When naught remains but lifeless creeds of duty,
Ami sage advice rolls easily off the tongue;
When 1 have spent my little store of truth,
And ardent Faith is riddled through by Reason —
Let not my mumbling lips drone forth their treason
To all the sacred vows Of marvellous youth !
But let this treacherous husk be quietly borne
Safe out of mischief, so that none can find me
Save in the rebel songs 1 flung behind me
When scaling hilltops in the mists of morn.
VICTORIA.
VANCE PALMER.

MELBOURNE.
SPORTING
NOTIONS
Considering that the mare had decidedly
tailed in her one previous- attempt to run away with
a two-mile race, plungers took a great risk in laying
7 to 2 on Desert Gold for the King’s Plate at
Klemington. She had practically only one horse to
beat, but that one was Wallace Isinglass. Were he
quite himself, and the mare disposed to make a hot
pace, Wallace looked more than likely to wear her
down at the finish. No animal of her temperament
—a free goer who runs best in front of the field- —
has ever acquired great staying power whilst racing
in runaway style. Wakeful was not a thorough
stayer until she had ceased to be a persistent pace-
maker. She nearly ran away with a Caulfield Cup,
but Hymettus caught her in the last few strides.
Desert Gold is not a first-class stayer. She has more
stamina than, say, Solution, a M.I;. mare that was
expected to win Melbourne Cun from end to end.
She is a lot better than Gladsome, also from M.L.,
who couldn’t last further than 104 furlongs. But
she is of the same type. Her easy impetuosity doesn’t
suggest a two-miler, nor did her record justify anyone
in laying 7 to 2 on her for a long race where she
would have Wallace Isinglass chasing her.
*****
Several likely handicap winners at the
late V.R.C. meeting were beaten by Interference. In a
crowded field he is a good friend to the books, especi-
ally as regards, the favorite. On the last day of the
meeting two favorites —Pah King in the first race
and Red Pennant in the third —owed their defeats to
him. Nowadays racing reporters seem to accept the
triumphs of this ugly competitor as a matter of course.
He starts in every event, apparently. And he usually
makes his strongest efforts at the back of the course
or coming round the turn for home.
* * * *
Two or three of the Victorians that have
some sort of chance for Sydney Cup ran at Caulfield
last Saturday. The talked-of Shadowland scored his
second win. In beating Blackwood and Lavendo
over 11 furlongs he did nothing brilliant, but at
least he showed a capacity for taking his own part
in fairly crowded and respectable company, and he
will have plenty of Melbourne followers if he goes
to Sydney for Easter. On paper, perhaps, the form
spoke better for Blackwood than for the winner. Old
Blackwood met Shadowland on 161 b. worse terms
than their Cup handicap, and was not far behind at
the finish. As to whether either of them is quite
class enough for the bigger job at Randwick, there
must always be a doubt until they are tested under
the different conditions. Two Blues, for instance,
heat the consistent Blackwood over 11 furlongs at
Caulfield recently as though he were a mere com-
moner. And when Wallace Isinglass downed Desert
Gold he was reckoned good enough to have won the
Australian Cup in a canter —on a dry course. All
things considered, it looks foolish to be very sweet
on anything for Sydney Cup until the weather is
guaranteed.
* * * *
Shadowland’s stable-companion and com-
patriot, Elsdon, 'onenecl his winning account in a
welter race at Caulfield. The son of Symington is
only a four-year-old at present, and may acclimatise
into a pretty good one. Ail the same, he has
already put one or two, disappointing performances
to his discredit and is possibly as uncertain as they
make them.
Just as punters were about to lose their
last bit of confidence in Hoprig the fickle quadruped
won a small six-furlongs race at -- Caulfield. He
went out at C to 1, a long price for a sprinter that
had started second favorite for the Newmarket. But
Hoprig, even on his best behavior, is hardly worthy
a place in the best sprinting class. He seemed to
owe this victory mostly to Foulsham, who rushed
him in at the finish.
The Linacre mare Dame Acre, who had
won over a mile with Bst. 121 b. in “40"’ during the
previous week, annexed the Railway Stakes, seven
furlongs, at Rosehill on Saturday with Bst. 101 b.,
incidentally clipping Golden S’liDper’s record for
the course and finishing in “26.” If this lady
Imps off in the Doncaster as quickly as usual, she
won’t be last over the line.
Volpi, a two-year-old brother of Thrice,
who on his first appearance ran third in the Breeders’
Plate at Randwick, has been growing up in peace
and quietness and looks all the better for it. On
the first day of the Rosehill meeting he was pro-
duced among 1 the juveniles, and they all left him
behind at the tape, another youngster in the same
stable winning the stake. On Saturday, carrying
the rider who bestrode the winner of the previous
week, Volpi made no mistake about the time-table.
He arrived on his own. Volpi has developed mto
a smart-looking animal that may be expected to
again leave his card with the judge some day.
Early last week the imported Publican,
trained at Randwick by D. O’Connor, pranced into
first place on the Doncaster betting cards I here
was money for him, and the way he left the scenery
behind over the last part of the Auburn Stakes at
Rosehill on Saturday had warned Isaac to beware.
He didn’t win the Auburn, which was six fuilongs,
but seemed sorry the journey was not longer. Last
Saturday he was given a chance in the KaUway,
seven furlongs, on the same course, and was solidly
backed That was the end of it. Though well
up for the run home he did the early-closing act
and finished away back. Perhaps he didn t like
being tangled up in the ruck most of the journey
but in any case the performance, regarded as a
Doncaster trial, caused no excitement. So they
scratched him. # *
That nobody knows what a good horse will
do was shown again in the Rawson *
mile and a quarter, at Rosehill (N.S.W.), on Satur
day. In point of quality there was a fair field.
But not more than two of the seven —Rebus and
Redfern, seemed ready for the job. This made them
favorites at 3’s with Thana, Chantemerle at 4 s, and
Wolaroi and Westcourt anything from 20 to 30. Of
the last two Westcourt was certainly the most
forward, but. the stable had no faith in either of
them. Wolaroi got chopped out early in the pro-
ceedings, and Westcourt toiled along unimpressively
in the rear to the turn into the straight. At this
stage Redfern was doing well, but was quickly run
down by Rebus, who a furlong from the line looked
a sure winner—until Westcourt was noticed.
McLachlan had moved him along on the outside
with great rapidity, and opposite the Leger he
came swooping up like a sprinter.. The last 100
yards was an all-in struggle to discover which would
crack first. It wasn't Westcourt. With three or
four strides to go the game but shabby-looking little
imported prad punctured, and the Melbourne Cup
winner pushed his head in front. The winner's was
a remarkably fine performance. He ran the dis-
tance in “53,” and if that is not a warning ,to
Wallace Isinglass to keep moving in the Sydney Cup
then time counts for nothing.

At Sydney-suburban Kensington (pony)
on Saturday £11,300 went through the machines;
at ltosehill, where some of the best horses in train-
ing in Sydney were raced, £9BOO. The meaning
of this is that the dollars go on the machine and
the quids go somewhere else —and always will.
* * * *
An old-time jockey, Fred Martineer, died
a few days back at his home, formerly known as the
Melton Inn, near Parramatta. Before Local Option
got to. work he had kept the old place as a pub tor
35 years. Amongst Martineer’s victories in the saddle
were with Cooramin in the old N.S.W. Leger, 1? mile,
in 1853, and the first A.J.C. Derby in 1865, on
Judge Cheeke’s mare Clove. Martineer himself
owned some good racehorses* notably the steeple-
chaser Melton, with which he won some dozen events.
It has happened again ! Sydney police,
hearing a squeak the other day, found it came from
an individual who had parted company with £2OO
and was anxiously inquiring where the coves were
who had it. After restoratives had been applied the
weary old yarn was heard. It was all about; an
honest citizen from Bananaland who came down to
go to" the Show and met a stranger at the coffee-
palace and was introduced to someone else who
owned a vast string of nags that won several races
everv week. The owner of all this horse thought
the Bananalander might as well help himself to a
bit and undertook to get it from Israel. Meanwhile
he took £2OO to go on with and vanished into the
atmosphere, after recommending the trustful suckei
to buy a war bond before sunset. All this conies of
Bananaland not compelling the population to nail its
money down under the carpet and go holiday-making
on a prickly-pear selection with 17 bulldogs.
*** * *
“Jim "'Carew”: The deadliest betting
swindle I ever came across was worked on a book in
a Western N. &. Wales town. Ike is open
to bet on all races, and always pays on the report
in next day’s metropolitan papers. The punter made
a bet on a horse allegedly running at a small Out-
back meeting. Then he sent a report to the city ot
a fictitious meeting showing that the imaginary horse
had won. The report was published and he drew his
money. Ike on taking a tumble tried to bluff the
cheat into returning the money, but the punter
threatened to put the police wise to the gambling
room. The result was a draw, with the punter
attached to the cash.
If anyone wants a really good day’s sport
among the ponies the place to go seems to be Mount
Morgan. They do it in broad daylight up there,
too. In the first race one of the riders had to be
“cautioned.” The rext commenced by two of the
nags bolting, one of them the favorite. Eventually
they were started and after prolonged waiting tor
them to arrive the stewards came to the conclusion
that there were no triers, declared the race off and
cancelled the bets. For the next event Alice was
a red-hot favorite. She won in good style. The
stewards said it was robbery, put other jocks aboard
and had it all over again. This time Alice hadnit
a hope. After two more events had been run with-
out bloodshed the second race was ordered on again
and something else was favorite. Coming up the
straight an outsider seemed ready to beat the
favorite, whereupon the interloper’s bridle was
grabbed and the rider of the uncalled-for animal
started handing out stoush with his whip. These
proceedings led to this race also being declared off,
and everything went up with a bang. At last
advices there was a feeling in the neighborhood that
racing might be curtailed without any great harm
to the horse-breeding industry.
After years of profound consideration
Tatt.’s has at last decided that in future the drawer
of a starter in its sweeps shall be given the sole
privilege, hitherto shared by the lottery office, ot
advising the nag’s owner that the cheerful event has
happened. Now that a certain class of prad-pro-
prietor has reached the dizzy limit in his demands,
which are sometimes as high as “fifty-fifty, ’ backed
by the customary threat of scratching, the belated
decision should be of some value to latt. s in in-
creased subscriptions, not to mention the pleasant
effect it must have on-the concern’s conscience.
* * * *
“Lachlander”; “Jacko” (B. 7/3/T8), in
stating that Jimmy Frost rode his horse The Colonel
from Dubbo to Orange for a wager, and covered 96
miles in 15 hours, is out in his facts. Bob Frost
owned The Colonel—also Lucknow station. He
had so frequently announced that his big, raw-
boned chestnut could beat anything in the West
(NSW) over a distance that Ted Tarrant, well
known along the Macquarie, took him up. Tarrant
had a mare called Barmaid, and he put up £lOO to
say that his nag could heat The Colonel between the
towns, 104 miles from post-office to post-office.
Both were to carry 9st. Tarrant engaged Walter
Higginbotham, later on to be the trainer of Car-
bine, to pilot the mare. Roger Davis, an adopted
son of Paddy Power, of Orange, rode The Colonel.
Paddv rode with his son and used up three hacks
on the trip. The track was a rough one and
boss were encountered between Dubbo and Welling-
ton. At the last-mentioned place the horses were
ferried across the river in a punt. Refreshments
were given the animals at intervals. The Colonel
taking oatmeal and water and Barmaid sticking to
bottled ale. At Montefiores, a holding owned at
the time by Joe Aarons, the track could be short-
ened by a cut across the paddocks. A boundary-
rider, who had backed The Colonel, permitted Davis
to pass, but attempted to block Barmaid and her
party. .Tarrant was a man of few words at any
time and the rouseabout hurried into the scrub.
But the hills beat the Barmaid. She caved in at
Shepherd’s Creek. The Colonel finished up full of
running, taking just IOJ hours on the trip; but
as he loafed along after the other stopped, his per-
formance was regarded by Frost as being much
below the ordinary. Tarrant took his beating badly
and as The Colonel carried 41b. overweight he
entered a protest. Clibborn, of the A.J.C., to whom
the matter was referred, decided for Frost. This
race was run on July 14, 1870. The old chestnut
died years ago aged 30.

In Svdney grade cricket Arthur Mailey
(Middle Harbor) and W. S. Dight (Western Sub-
urbs) are fighting it out. On Saturday Mailey took
three for 137 against ’Varsity; Dight five for 108
against Sydney. This season, Mailey been
smashed to and over the boundary for 1247 runs
for 05 wickets (averaging 19.18). Dight's figures
are 1024 for 47 wickets (average 21.78). But all
this isn’t a novelty to either of them. In ’l5-16
Mailev took 102 wickets for 1433, Dight 54 for 838.
In ’i6-l7 Mailey knocked over 49 for 712, and
Dight’s bag was 45 for 698. Three years’ figures
give Mailey 216 wickets for 3392, while Dight s
146 wickets cost 2886. It is many moons since
bowlers caught the face of the bat so generously.
While there are dozens of junior swim-
ming clubs whose members splash regularly for
trophies and baubles, only a few clubs troubled to
toe the platform at Coogee (Sydney) for the N..S.
Wales junior life-saving championship in connection
with the Hendry Challenge Cup. The event turned
out to be a good thing for North Wollongong (H.
Peterson, F. Thomas, L. Hallenan and W. Lance).
They travelled 50 miles and defeated the city lads
bv scoring 87.16 points. Burwood Boy Scouts were
next on the list with 79.82. North Wollongong won
the championship last year. The event, which was
for lads under 16, deserves more attention from
the enthusiasts whose job it is to see that Young
Australia understands the practical side of paddling.
* * * *
“Raiigan”: During Christmas I was at.
Brisbane’s popular seaside resort, Redcliffe, when the
cry for help went up and hundreds of fingers pointed
to" two heads moving steadily out to sea. Fortunately
the two in difficulties managed to just reach a reef
of rocks, from which they were rescued. “Pretty
close shave,” murmured the crowd, and went on
splashing itself. I thoughtfully got dressed and made
inquiries. “No,” said one of the inhabitants, we
don’t use life-saving reels here. This is the safest
beach in Australia.” Everybody was of the same
opinion. Later, I found that Queensland possessed
15 “safest beaches in Australia’’ and six reels. Up
till a year ago Yeppoon had “the safest beach in
Australia” ; then, during one week, a man was taken
by a shark and three people were drowned. Yep-
poon began to think, and while it was thinking
another woman was drowned. So it bought reel.
Southport and Tweed Heaijs had an average of two
deaths a year, before they were induced to instal
life-saving appliances. They now have five or six
rescues a year. The Royal Life Saving Society asked
the Home Sec. to make local authorities protect
bathers by supplying reels, etc. “Well,” said Hux-
ham, “you write and ask them about it, and if they
do nothing, I might think about it” ; and there you
have the spirit of Huxham’s administration.
* * * *
Jimmy H. Ashton, a tall, smiling son of
a N. S. Wales Leg. Councillor, is a promising High
School athlete. He is a' front-ranker at cricket,
football and swimming’, and with the mits won the
middle- and heavy-weight championships of C. of E.
Grammar last year. In the “heavies” final he
defeated G. S. Holcombe, captain of the School, and
the fine distance ,ped. Ashton is a two-handed
boxer, who springs on his opponents a wealth of
tricks put into his youthful head during a holiday
jaunt through America last year.
* * * *
There’s reason for the joy in Sydney High
Schools over the dash of L. A. Bannister, a 15-year-
old lad who recently gave other sprinters a fine view
of his flying legs in the 100yds. all-schools
colleges championship, which he won in 10 4 ; ssec.
The burst equals the time of C. Farquhar and A.
Carroll in High School efforts ; but J. B. Sandilands
N.S.W. 100yds. schools record (10 3-10 sec. j, put up
in 1912, is still on its feet. Bannister won the
junior 100yds. (11 l-ssec.) and 220yds. (24 4-ssec.)
at this year’s High Schools championships; and his
turn of speed has caused hard heads to ponder how
long it is since they saw his equal.
* # * *
Dick Coombes, of Sydney, the best-known
and certainly the best-informed writer on athletics
in Australia, reached the 60th pole the other day.
Richard, among other things, is vice-president of
the Great Public Schools A.A.A. and of the Uni-
versity Athletic Club —the only man not connected
with the schools or the ’Varsity who has been elected
an office-bearer on either.
* * * *
“Leg-bail”: John Madden was a button
that if pressed usually delivered the goods. Three or
four vears ago I was compiling a history of St. Kilda,
and wanting information about the first meet of the
Melbourne Hunt Club at St. Hilda Junction I wrote
to Chief Justice John, whose father was a hunting
man of 1857. He did not know me from a crow, but
that did not matter. He replied that he was brooding
over some Full Court judgments, but later he d have
ago at Early St. Kilda-—and hang the meet, ot
which he knew little! About three months after-
wards 20 sheets of foolscap came staggering along
under a penny stamp and a fine of eightpence. They
were full of good stories of Dicky Ireland, Butler
Cole Aspinall and other bucks of the ’fifties, who
stripped in the early morning in Captain Kenny s
bathing ship, seeking to cool their hot coppers
begotten of the night before at the Prince of Wales
or the George. The stories, with the history, he in
the St. Kilda Council’s strongroom.
* * * *
By way of a change, this year’s N.S.W.
sinatfe bowls championship was not won by a player
who decked a dilapidated old hat. In bowls, moie
than any other nastime, players turn out in the
oldest and quaintest headgear; and when their
skill gathers a title, the credit therefor is piled on
the troubles of the old hat. The men in the final were
J Morrison (Petersham) and J. J. Deane (Ryde),
and the Petersham player, in a clean bright hat (and
a necktie, even!), made every end a winner, finish-'
ir.g up 31-21. Deane's old hat looked like making
up the leeway at half-way (15-10) ; but “nerves”
finally wrecked the village blacksmith. Both were
new aspirants for the title. In the teams match
City and Ivuring-gai are to fight it out, Mosman
having gone under.
“45/90”: In spite of Bob Rogers (B.
14/2/ ; 18), 1 again assert that Livingston has killed
more ’roos than any three men in the game to-day.
As Bob apparently misconstrued my few remarks
into a reflection on him, let me say I know him to
be the best shot and hardest battler (with the excep-
tion, perhaps, of Jack Freich) in the Aramae district,
even though he sidesteps the Downs, which has
always been Livingston’s stamping ground. As to
Livingston’s “methods being slow and out of date"
—well, he is ' now about 60, and I have a notion
that if Bob puts in another 30 years at the game
and leads the life “Liver” has, Iris methods may be
a bit slow. In regard to “many of us getting four
to his one,” that may be true in 1918 —but, listen.
Away back in the early ’eighties “Liver” was pump-
ing a .44 on Northampton, Lome, Terrick and Home
Creek. That was before the railway reached the
West and the abos. were holding corroborees on the
sites of Barcaldine and Longreach. Somewhere about
’B5, on Ravensbourn, the book-keeper kept tag of
“Liver’s” ' earnings for six months, and found
he was making more than the manager, overseer and
book-keeper combined. Scalps at that time wfere 3s.
and tallies of 50 or 60 a day ths usual thing. In
the roaring days of the 21 mile in ’9O and "91
Livingston was reckoned to throw the fastest and
straightest gun in the West, and this at a time when
every third man packed a .44 and lived on the game.
And his name was known from the Barcoo to the
Flinders what time Bob Rogers was puzzling out the
mechanism of his first popgun.

Dear Bulletin,—This, my final argument,
I am prepared to back with hard cash. “Ranger”
(B. 21/2/’18) does not mention my 2 to 1 offer tlrart
a rifleman cannot hit the bull’s-eye without being
tied down in a sling in a position impossible, in
nine cases out of 10, on active service. Now, to
further prove what “a farce” sighting shots are and
marking each shot, I offer 4 to 1 for any number of
shots against the rifleman hitting the bull from
300yds. upwards without sling, si,"liters or marking,
none of which you can use on active service. Rifle-
shooting as now carried on teaches a man to load
and shoot —-but only to shoot accurately with artifi-
cial aid impossible when put to the tfist in the field.
—Yours, Geo. L. Hobson.
* * * •- *
“H.P.W.” : Captain McKeaehie’s rifle, re-
ferred to by “Pick” (B. 14/2/’18), was a Sharp’s old
model carbine, first used by the U.S. army in
Mexico, and, later, in the Civil War. An improved
model had a cap magazine, worked by the hammer
as it cocked, which enabled the weapon to be fired
10 times per minute. Some English cavalry regi-
ments were supplied with Sharp’s carbine in 1857,
but it was abandoned on account of the escape of gas
at the breech, which tended to clog the mechanism
after a few shots had been fired. In the ’sixties
Sharp’s co. got over this trouble by adopting a
metallic central-fire cartridge, and in this form the
rifle became the favorite of the buffalo-lnmter, and
had a large share in exterminating the American
bison.

“Adelaide”: Killed my first dinner with the
car one moonlit evening lately in the hills —a fine
hare, which foolishly bolted across the road and was
dead meat the next moment. I have run down
kangaroos with the car, but it didn’t kill them.
Dogs don't count, and. the fowls that have committed
suicide per wheel were hardly worth gathering up
after it had done with them. This is the first prac-
tical game in the bag.

Can You Defend Yourself?
These are important questions to YOU. Unless you confidently feel

superiority. You will feel that confidence in YOUR power so NECESSARY
to ACHIEVE victory.
Why put off learning to defend yourself any longer?
I Teach Boxing by Post
There are 32 lessons in my course. Each tells in
clear simple words how to deliver a serviceable hit,
and the best way to block it. V -
Photographs and diagrams show exactly how the
hands, arms, head, shoulders, body and feet are used
in attack and defence. Well-satisfied pupils in parts
of Australia are constantly praising my methods.
This is an example of the letters I’m constantly
receiving:—

and I am HIGHLY SATISFIED with it, and pro-
gressing favorably.”
I am anxious to send you full particulars of my
methods, terms and also an interesting booklet, “Scien-

Hadn’t you better write for it now, while you
think of it, and send 3d. to cover the postage?

such service deter many makers from a similar enterprise, yet
the matter is one of vital importance to the prospective motor-
cycle purchaser.

The Ipswich Gas and Coke Co. (Queens-

Under the Queensland Gas Act accounts]
are to be made up to December 31 in each
year; so those now submitted are for 10
months only. Avoiding an error of the
N.S.W. Act, the Queensland Parliament
didn’t fix a standard price. Instead it pro-
vided for a referee, who was to ascertain
(a) the cost of manufacture and (b) the
capital in the undertaking, add 8£ p.c. on
the capital to the total cost, and, after
making certain fixed allowances for cash-
discount and gas-leakage, arrive thus at the
price to be charged per thousand to the
public. It is a fairer scheme than that
contained in either the old or the amended
N.*S. Wales measure, though 84 p.c. is too
much interest to allow. If a co. raises a
lot of money on cheap debentures or by an
issue of pref. shares, it may easily pay 12
p.c. on its ordinary capital; and as the
referee in fixing the cost (and therefore the
selling) price had to allow for depreciation,
there is no visible reason why a Queensland
gas co. should ever pay less than 81 p.c. so
long as it has a monopoly. And by getting
the use of money for less than 81 p.c. it
ought generally to pay more. But it is not
compelled to charge the high rate which the
referee necessarily had to fix by following
the law: it may conclude that it will pay
better to encourage consumption by smaller
charges. The Ipswich Co., with a share
capital of £36,000, is financially very snug;
for though plant, etc., has been nearly
doubled during the past five years, the
directors have got in the. cash to do it with,
increasing the share capital from £20,000 to
£36,000.

vestment Co. (Wellington, M.L.)

That is a slump of about £3OOO below recent
profits; but nothing else could be ex-
pected. First, there is the increased taxa-
tion. Land and income taxes have moved
along in this brisk way: 1914, £866; 1915,
£1956; 1916, £2535; 1917, £5510. Some
concerns can, of course, very well afford to
carry all the extra war imposts that are
being put upon them, as the war is booming
their business. But cos. like this are not in the
boom. This concern has over half a million
of mortgages and other securities, and as
the capital value falls while the demand
for money grows, foreclosures become more
frequent—except, of course, where a mora-
torium intervenes. So a new item drops in
among the outgoings: “Depreciation busi-
ness premises and other property”—not
much yet; only £507; but unpleasantly sig-
nificant.
• > •
However, the directors are apparently not
concerned about the future, and they con-
tinue to lend money, themselves borrowing
to do it. This next comparison shows the
movements: —•
1905. 1915 1916. 1917.
Capital & reserves 139,604 200,348 201,511 199,636
Liabilities.. .. 308,5.79 366,880 383,833 392,131
Mortgages, etc. .. 429,106 528,871 545,659 554,901
Freeholds .. .. 3,500 14,586 14,286 13,7*9
Interest owing .. 5,566 7.324 7,803 8,120
Liquid assets .. 10,011 16,447 17,596 14,967
Of the liabilities £386,535 is* debited to
“Deposits and interest and investors”; and
a lot depends on how ,that money is held.
Property values in Australia and Maoriland
are governed largely by the abundance or
otherwise of loan money; and as we go on
borrowing jags, followed by periods of whip-
ping the cat, property values see lots of
violent ups and downs. Finance cos.’ losses
are nearly always due to the fact that they
are not able to carry their bundle from peak
to peak, but have to unload when they are
down in the valley; and in nine cases out
of ten they have to do that because their
own creditors want their money. Hence
until one knows more about and
interest and investors” than the published
accounts tell him, one knows very little
about this co. That the depositors and in-
vestors are safe enough seems quite certain,
though. The co. owes £392,000. Deduct
£23.000. liquid assets in hand; arid you get
£369,000 of debt represented by £554,901 of
mortgages and £13,779 of business premises
and other property. There is a wide enough
buffer there to carry the creditors over
almost any conceivable bump.
* * * *
A man really has to be careful when he
is writing official reports about wayback
shows. Twelve months ago Oliver, Director
of Mines, Northern Territory, visited
Hatch’s Creek wolfram field, the most re-
mote working claims in Australia —over 300
miles from anywhere. Oliver’s report, which
lately arrived on the field, says the favorite
method of attack where the slope of the hill
is favorable is by open cut, the mullock
being disposed of by means of wheelbarrows.
Since then all Hatch’s Creek has been look-
ing for the wheelbarrows; and, curiously, one
can’t be discovered on the field. As a mat-
ter of fact the shifting of the spoil is ail
either shovel or windlass work.

Randolph Bedford writes :
(Re Pearce and his Department of Expense and
“G.J.H.’s” complaint of tremendous deliberation in
the muddler’s correspondence. My son left Australia
early in November after a final leave to see me in
Queensland. After he had been four weeks on the
water, the alert Department wrote me saying it had
given him leave to proceed to Queensland. That’s
the way to win wars.

IMPORTANT
Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Hough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
ire too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is-scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free. 1/3. Free sample posted.

“Gallery G.”: Most amusing chaps, these
producers. One in Sydney has a cure of his
own for fainting. The other night, during a
particularly exhausting bit of stage “busi-
ness,” one of the. chorus girls flopped. At
the first opportunity Mr. Drivem made a
little speech to the co. and announced that
the next girl who fainted would be put off
for a week. Go on the stage, young woman
—go on the stage!
“Stargazer”: With regard to reflections
upon the utility of the surprise drama {vide
“Ibsenshaw” and some
others), can any line
be laid down and spiked
to hard-and-fast sleepers
with regard to this par-
ticular form of play?
You cannot say em-
phatically and forever
that it is right or
wrong, bad or good.
A play so fabricated
may be intolerable or
exceedingly pleasing. It
is all in the making.
If a drama gives you
good character and
strong or interesting
situations, the fact that
it is a surprise packet
is not going to destroy
your delight in it, any
more than finding a
sovereign in your box
of lollies prejudices you
against the sweets.
The surprise in the
play, as in the O. Henry
•story, should be an
added inducement.
TAYLOR
DARBYSHIRE,
J.C.W. Ltd.'s New
Associate Director.
Sol Bloom, who some years ago was well
known hereabouts as a Shakspearean
reader and (in his spare time) as a lawyer,
has been heard from with a Chicago address
to his notepaper. Sol has apparently got rid
of his Parliamentary ambitions (he once
gave ex-Labor leader Prendergast a good run
in North Melbourne), but is still keen on the
stage and takes a leading part in the “Little
Theatre” movement. Incidentally, Solomon
is full of admiration for Uncle Sam’s Navy
Department, but declares that so far as his
other war organisation is concerned he
hasn’t much to be proud of, and the cry of
“Graft!” is loud in the land.
“S.S.”: It was at a concert in Melbourne
a few nights ago. The portly soprano was
singing “The End of a Perfect Day,” and
was getting well down to it, with more
energy than art, and more vivacity than
voice, when a baby interpolated a protest.
The infant continued its cries, and presently
the singer stopped dead. Then came the
voice: “Ah, thanks! Now we can hear the
baby.”
A pretty youngster with large eyes named
Avery is understudy to Maggie Dickenson in
the Whittington panto., just gone on tour.
Understudy was given a solo dance in the
last hilarious week of the Melbourne run.
“T. 0.1.”: The first effort to establish sub-
scription opera in Australia was made by
George Coppin in Melbourne in September,
1856. When the public was appealed to no
less than £2OOO was promptly subscribed.
However, the promoter, to the disappoint-
ment of the subscribers, intimated that the
money would be returned, Anna Bishop and
Coppin having disagreed as to -terms. The
Theatre Royal, built by John Black, who
made a pile carrying tucker to the diggings,
was to have housed the co.

“8.T.0.”: It’s a real get-ripe-quick vari-
ety of stage peach that makes everyone’s
fortune in “Turn to the Right.” The crop
is dead-ripe and threatening to rot when
the ex-crooks get going on it in the third
act; the fourth act comes 10 months later,
and then the alleged peach-trees are in full
blossom. So there are only a couple of
months from flowering to picking time. The
common or garden peach takes more than
twice as long.
t “Barbavano”: When did Stannis Leslie
sing for us in grand opera (B. 24/1/T8) ?
He sang in the chorus of “Faust,” when
Nellie Stewart was Marguerite at the Prin-
cess’s Theatre. He also carolled the Sen-
try’s Song in “lolanthe”; but you can
hardly call that grand opera. Stannis, 30
years ago, could sing “Drinking” and “I Fear
No Foe” with any baritone or basso in Aus-
tralia ; but his nervous system prevented his
advancement, and he has never sung a solo or
taken a part in g.o. in all his 35 years’ con-
nection with the Australian stage.
“Eddyson’s” tale of the track: —
Tom Bonnet at the sleeper-cutters’ camp at Jim-
erow saw him coming down the line, “walking the
timber,” a tall, bony figure shouldering a small
bundle on the end of a stick. He was a precise
replica of the broken tragedian of the comic press,
and obviously he was tramping back, a remnant of
the dramatic co. that had run on the rocks and gone
to pieces at Boolbool a week before. But his feet
were his most noticeable feature. They were done up
in linen bundles, reminding Bonnet of the roly-
poly puddings his mother made when he was a kid,
and their owner balanced on them as if the world
were red hot. The sleeper-cutter had gone “hot foot”
himself in his day, and this poor blighter was 100
miles from Poverty Point and 15 from anywhere.
Bonnet called him and he came hobbling down with
painful alacrity. Arrived in front of the sleeper-cutter,
he raised his battered belltopper and said: “You called
sir? Excuse me if I sit.” He sat straight down in
the dirt, took up his larger foot very fondly, sighed
deeply and said: “Ah, that is better!” “Come far?”
asked Tom. “Far?” said the actor. “Far? From
the uttermost ends of the earth, sir. I had boots
when I started. Observe I have now no feet.”
“What,” said Bonnet casually, “of a drink and a
bit of dinner?” The old actor thought it over. “A
drink ?” he said. “Are there still drinks ? And
dinner i I have forgotten dinner, sir. And did you
say boots, sir—boots ?” “I daresay that can be
managed.” “Boots —aha-a!” He smacked his lips.
“Boots! Misery, sir, is an empty stomach, two bare
feet and a long road. Boots —aha-a 1” Bonnet took
Harold Branston into the camp. Five other hard,
timber-stained bushwhackers welcomed him. They
gave him treatment good for sore feet. Don Stoney
fitted him out with clean, soft socks and a pair of
pliable and roomy boots. He had whisky and a
regular meal. He paid them with recitations from
Henley, and with “Denver’s Dream” and Shakspearean
fragments, and was put to bed in Rowe’s tent, Rowe
being away at Wellend on his semi-annual jag. Only
Stoney saw the show right through. He awoke
unaccountably at about two in the morning, and
the fierce glare in his tent brought him up and out.
Tom Bonnet’s tent was one great flame, Harold
Branston was darting from Rowe’s ten-by-eight. He
did the thing in the approved dramatic manner.
He stood a moment before the blazing tent in the
attitude the stage hero invariably assumed when
about to do or die, he threw two appealing hands to
Heaven. “Me Gardl” he cried, “he is within.” Then
he dived into the fire, and the next moment
reappeared carrying Bonnet as the splendid hero of
the play always carries whatever he has rescued from a
hideous death. Bonnet was smoking hot, so was
Branston. Both called for a great deal of attention
and both appeared next morning elaborately done up
in the fragments of four white shirts. Branston had
his arm in a sling, and his eyebrows were singed
off, a roast ear was done up in a neat bundle, and
one leg was raw. Bonnet expressed his honest
gratitude. “You saved my life,” he said; “well,
any damn thing I can do for you, now or ever, I’ll
do. You can have all I’ve got. There’s my camp—
it’s yours as long as you like.” Harold warded
off all this gratitude as the hero always does. “Not
so, my dear fellow,” he said. “No talk of reward.
I could not dream of it.” Harold swung across R.,
bowed elaborately R.C., and made his exit R.U.E.
That evening a trap was provided to take him on
to Wellend, but before leaving the eminent tragedian
plucked Bonnet’s elbow and drew him tinder cover.
“Eh, eh, private,” he said, “most important. You
eh-expressed some gratitude and eh-eh-appreciation
of my ah-little effort. You were serious—quite
serious? “I meant it,” insisted Bonnet, “every
woyd, of it.” “Thank you, sir—thank you. Then,
eh, privately, you know, and strictly between our-
selves, would you-eh— would you loan me a shilling?”
“11. J.”: “Snowy” Baker makes a strenu-
ous screen dgbut in “The Enemy Within.”
Many of the wonderful feats done by actors
in the American nfcovie dramas are faked;
but Snowy scorned anything but the dinkum
article. When the hero at Los
Angela wishes to climb up the face of a
building, the producer orders a replica of
the front of a building from the property
department. The director lays this flat on
the ground, and orders the hero to wriggle
across it, while the camera man, looking
down from a platform close by, photographs
it until the hero reaches the “top” of the
edifice. Snowy with honesty of purpose
climbed over the face of a cliff 300 ft. high,
dived 80ft into the sea, and rescued the
heroine from a watery grave. The result on
the screen does not justify the risk. Two
hundred yards or more from the spectator
a white speck is seen moving slowly down
the face of the rock. After he had made the
big dive, and got to the rock to rescue the
heroine, he found that she was scared stiff,
and that he was in for something strenuous.
Baker says he was never before so near
drowning. He is doing another picture, but
this time there will be a little judicious
faking, as, Mrs. Baker has tacked up the
motto: “Safety First.”
From London:—
Plays for children have become as much a feature
of Christmas as pantomimes. “Bluebell in Fairy-
land is one, and Ellaline Terriss, though now she has
a big daughter of her own, still plays the name-part.
On the other hand, this year’s “Alice in Wonder-
land” has a 10-year-old Alice; Hayden Coffin brings
his fine voice into the part of the Mad Hatter.' This
play has been running more or less for 30 years.
“Peter Pan” is now aged 13. Martin Lewis (of
Milestones and “Bella Donna” in Australia) is
this year’s Mr. Darling. A fresh piece, in
its second winter, is “The Happy Family,” de-
signed by the animal-painter Cecil Aldin; most of
the players are children, but William Cromwell (in
Australia long ago in “Miss Hook of Holland”) has
a big part as Peter the Pig. “Charley’s Aunt” and
“The Private Secretary” are regular and safe
Christmas pieces; the latest Secretary is Charles
Walenn, who played Gilbert and Sullivan comedy-
leads in Australia.
Peter Dawson is one of the fortunate
artists without any stories to tell of early
struggle. His family owns a prosperous
metal-working business in Adelaide, where
he was born in 1882. C. J. Stevens w T as his
chief teacher there, and the pupil sang in both
Orpheus and Choral Society, as well as in the
choir of St. Andrew’s (Presbyterian). At
19 he was sent by dad to London to study
under Santley; and when he came back to
Australia in 1908 his reputation was alreadv
made in grand opera at Covent Garden.

. “Mat”: The pictures look like wrecking
an old-time custom in India. The upper
classes hide their faces behind a heavy veil ;
but the film has got hold of higli caste its
well as low. To see the show the obstruc-
tion has to be got rid of somehow. Re-
cently a lady of high degree vaulted the
obstacle by hanging a mosquito-net from the
top of the box, through which she viewed
the show —and uncovered her face!
THE LAST LUNCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne publicans have definitely abolished the
free lunch.
His face was grave. He looked at me,
And in his eye there shone
A melancholy sore to see.
“The world is passing on,”
In low, sepulchral tone said he;
“My last free lunch is gone.
“The counter-lunch has had its day.
Its finish is a crime,
For ’twas the actor’s prop and stay.
Full often in my time,
When he was elsewhere turned away,
It fed the resting mime.
“The darkest hours of art it lit.
The actor gaunt and slim
Had but to bag a thrip’ny-bit,
When finding fortune grim—•
A mere threepenny-piece, and it
Was meat and drink to him.
“I’m growing old. With Punch and down
I’ll maunder in the calms
Of senile years, and speak renown,
And clap my withered palms
For days when any pub in town
Was still The Actor’s Alms.
“On that last lunch I worked my will,
Then spoke a grace: ‘O Thou
Who feed’st the little sparrow still,
As Scripture doth avow,
Is there on earth one left who’ll fill
The actor’s swallow now ?’ ”
VICTORIA.
STARGAZER.
FILM FRENZY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A telephone, a double-bed, a vampire and a crook,
A millionaire, a tape-machine, a flapper with a book.
A mining-camp, a faro-bank, a pile that’s lost and
won,
A frame-up and a double-cross, a Greaser with a gam.
A bad man and a tenderfoot, a rough-house homi-
cide,
A lost soul of the underworld, a sheriff’s lonely ride.
A coyote and a cowboy, in chaparajos and slouch,
A train or two, a faithful nig., a half-breed with a
grouch.
A burro and a broncho and a million dollars cash,
A long-lost child, a precipice, a dreadful auto smash.
A calumet, a cocktail, a catastrophe, and then
A honeymoon, a happy end, and—mix ’em up again!

ESTABLISHED 1852


London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olcja A msdi’ll, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, hut how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For this
purpose pure powdered pheminol may he
applied directly to the objectionable hair-
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair, but to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol. obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
. Removing Her Skin.
The quickest and surest way "to make a
good complexion out of a bad one is to
actually remove the thin veil of faded or dis-
colored outer skin from the face. This can
be done easily, safely and privately by any
woman. The process is one of gentle absorp-
tion.
Get some pure mercolised wax from your
chemist and apply it at night like cold cream,
washing it off in the morning. In a few
days the mercolide in the wax will have
absorbed the disfiguring cuticle, revealing
the fresh young skin underneath; you will
have a beautiful, clear, natural complexion.
The process is pleasant and harmless, making
the face look brilliantly attractive and youth-
ful. It also effectually removes such blem-
ishes as freckles, tan, moth patches, liver
spots, pimples, etc. Every woman should
keep a little mercolised wax on hand, as this
simple, old-fashioned remedy is the best com-
plexioned beautifier and preserver known.
Blackheads Instantly Go.
A very simple, harmless and pleasant pro-
cess is now used to remove blackheads and
correct greasiness and large pores in the
skin. You have only to drop a tablet of
stymol, obtained from the chemist, into a
glass of hot water and bathe the face with
the liquid after the .effervescence has sub-
sided. The blackheads will then come right
off on the towel. The enlarged pores immedi-
ately Contract to normal and the greasiness
disappears, leaving the skin smooth, soft and
cool and free from blemish. But to make sure
that this desirable result is permanent it is
advisable to repeat the treatment several
times at intervals of say about four or five
days.
Grey Hair Unnecessary.
One need not resort to the very question-
able expedient of hair dye in order not to
have grey hair. The grey hair can easily be
changed back to a natural color in a few
days time merely by the application of a
simple, old-fashioned and perfectly harmless
home-made lotion. Procure from your chem-
ist two ounces of tammalite concentrate and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this to the hair a few times with a small
sponge, and you will soon have the pleasure
ol: seeing your grey hair gradually darkening
to the desired shade. The lotion is pleasant,
not sticky or greasy, and does not injure the
hair in any way.
Naturally Wavy Hair.
Good stallax not only makes the best possi-
ble shampoo, but has the peculiar property of
leaving the hair with a pronounced natural
wave, an effect presumably desired by many
ladies. A teaspoonful of the stallax granules,
dissolved in a cup of hot water, is ample for
a luxurious head wash, and leaves the hair
with a peculiar lustre and fluffiness obtainable
from nothing else as far as known. It is per-
fectly harmless, and can be obtained from
most chemists, although since it has been but
little used for this purpose it comes only in
Jib. sealed original packages, enough to make
up 25 or 30 individual shampoos. [2
