Remarks of Sydney business-man T. W.
Heath at the annual meeting of the Aus-
tralian Association of British Manufacturers
(Melb.) :
Many business men in Australia were sending
registered designs of British goods to Japan to be
copied and put on the Australian market.
Shocking, of course; but so far as the Aus-
tralian workman is concerned, the fact to be
glued inside his hat is that he loses a job
equally whether the imported goods are made
in Birmingham or Kobe.
“Block 10”: N.S.W. Education Depart-
ment must have money to throw away. Two
years ago the teacher of a small 1 country
school applied for a supply of supplementary
readers. Six months later the school was
closed. La&t week, 18 months after kids and
teacher had said good-bye, the books arrived
at the nearest post-office. The shelves of
the p.o. are burdened with supplies of
blank forms, etc., which have been arriving
intermittently for the school ever since it
was shut.
The more uncommon the name the
greater the trouble when it happens to be
duplicated—as Pinero found out long ago
when he invented a Mrs. Ebbsmith as
“notorious,” and found a little after that
there was a real lady of that name who did
not like the ad. A respectable Adelaide
citizen with the quite unusual mime of
Summergreen lately appeared in the police
court just to mention that he was not iden-
tical with a man of that name found in S.
Aus. and badly wanted in Victoria. He
got nothing from the Bench except an obvi-
ous pun on his name, and left to advertise
his innocence and his misfortunes, in the
papers.
London, which has become a very apprecia-
tive customer, can’t get enough Australian
wines these arid days. To lessen the drought
and at the same time help along a useful
Commonwealth industry, High Commissioner
Fisher lately asked Colonial Secretary Long
to provide shipping space for 500 tons of
Australia’s liquefied sunlight. The proposal
gave the Australian Alliance Prohibition
Council a chance to advertise its patriotism
by cabling to Long a protest against the
suggested “waste of freight space,” which
should be filled with wheat. It’s a queer
sort of patriotism, though, which works
overtime to assist Germany’s post-war trade.
“Jimmie Pannikin” : Some of them are still
at. it. Recently the N. S. Wales State Child-
ren s Relief Department prosecuted a cow
cocky for merely kicking the base of an un-
fortunate State boy’s spine and for beating
him with a plough rein. (Somehow cocky
overlooked the ploughshare and the swingle-
tree.) The fine was 10 shillings. Evidently
State children are of little account in the
eyes of the law.. Had the brute been caught
slygrog-selling instead of putting the boot
into a small bit of hired-aut humanity, he
would have been fined anything from £3O to
£oo John Ruskin said: “Life is the only
wealth.” But he had never had the benefit
of a talk with the magistrate in this case.
This month the State Savings Bank of
South Australia will be three score and ten.
It started in a little office in Gawler-place
in 1848, spent most of its time in a King
William-street bank, and now has a fine
place of its own in Currie-street. Actually
two out of every three of the population of
the State are depositors. Its funds are close
on 11 millions. It has not paid as much as
5 per cent, for the past 30 years, but it is
again earning 4 p.c. for its depositors. S.
Aus. has long held the lead among the States
for the thrift that runs to a pass-book. In-
cluding the Commonwealth Savings Bank,
there are desposits of over £26 for
every man, woman and child in the
State. The figures for Victoria, N. S.
Wales and Queensland are all round about
the £22 mark. So is the average for the
whole of Australia. When Federation began
it was only £B, and the comparison would
look magnificent if it wasn’t for the fact that
then the public debt was only £53 and to-day
it is somewhere about £lO2.
“Hamer”: Here in England the lunch that
gets stale in its package before the time for
eating it arrives has practically disappeared,
like many of the 44d. eating dens, and the
workers crowd into canteens. The works,
public and private, which have not already
established canteens are rushing the system,
tor the very good reason that it’s a success
and attracts workpeople—or. anyway, the
absence of a canteen repels them. It’s only
the keeper of the surviving untidy 44d. joint
who is likely to talk about the arrangement
as an interference with the liberty of work-
people to eat where they like.
Castor-oil, having been requisitioned by
the Italian and British Governments, is now
at famine prices in Australia. In six months’
time nurses will be under the necessity of
bringing up babies without this detestable
but necessary cathartic, and the result will
not be advantageous to the Australian baby,
ihe oil plant, if oermitted, will grow here
with the profusion of Brer Bunnv ; but the
other oil plant is not available—in the
absence of Protection nobody has ventured
to put in the necessary machinery. And so
Australia has to run half-way round the
earth for a dose of oil every time babv has
a pang in his little bingie.
“Y.Z.’ : My wife brought home two hanks
• J' l * in S'Wool, for which she had just
given 2s. sd. They weighed precisely 20z.. and
thus cost 19s. 4d. per lb. No doubt it was
Australian wool that had been to Yorkshire
and back, aiid for the growing of which Aus-
tralia may have been paid lid. or so. Good
old primary industry run on these lines
means providing the importing thug with the
material to make a rope to choke you.
There are of course no pro-Germans in
the Defence Department, but even the most
bigoted enemy alien couldn’t do more to
make Australia’s compulsory training system
unpopular than some of the people who run
kfc °ther day an area-officer presented
to the Malvern (Vic.) Court a lad who had
missed some drills, and said: “His father
was killed at the war, his mother died from
the shock and he has a delicate youn-'
brother to support. I have tried my best
to have him exempted from drill, but the
Department will not do so.” And the Court
being allowed no discretion, had to send the
lad to Queenscliff for seven days. d tbe

MANCHESTER.GLASGOW.
“Cyez”: During a hot spell in the “new
Mallee” a citizen came into Ouyen (Vic.)
and got badly soused and very obstreperous.
The lock-up wasn’t finished, but John Hop-
kins didn’t worry. A big empty boiler was
lying handy, and the drunk was quite ready
to be good after he had roasted in there for
a few hours.
A son of Professor Butler, of Sydney
Uni., was officially reported killed on Gal-
lipoli. The report was greatly exaggerated,
and as a matter of fact Butler kept going
until a few weeks ago, when he was returned
to Australia. He had to ask for something
or other at Victoria Barracks, and then his
record was turned up. “Yes,” they said,
“but Butler’s dead.” lie assured them he
wasn’t, but they said he’d have to prove it;
and lie was op that job when The Bulletin
last heard about him.
“O. Blia”: Russian generals are agreed
that vodka played the leading part in the
weakening of Ivan’s punch. . In one retreat
Korniloff could only raise 800 out of a
Cossack division capable of intelligent
action. The rest had drowned their bellige-
rency in Russian beer and were massacring
one another with great enthusiasm. Alto-
gether, Korniloft’s report is a hair-raising
indictment of the Russian peasant when he
is satisfying a thirst that has been held long
in abeyance. Among other things, he men-
tions that his soldiers commonly knocked out
the teeth of dead officers for the sake of the
gold-fillings!
“F.C.”: “E.S.S.” (B. 14/2/’18) doesn’t
expose all the infamy of that recent importa-
tion of 54,000 cases of American apples.
Roughly, it means 1000 tons of shipping
space, at a time when shipping space is
about the scarcest and most valuable thing
on earth. And there was no justification for
Australia importing one solitary apple. Last
season’s apple crops in Vic. and Tas. were
good; pears and peaches were abundant;
and the Vic. cool stores only emptied out the
last of the crop just before Christmas,
after the new fruit was in. If the Federal
Government would pay as much attention
to organising fruit distribution and stimu-
lating consumption as it does to importers
it would be better for the man on the land.
But one can hardly expect that much wisdom
from a Government which is now trying to
exploit the foreign markets —especially
China and thereabouts —while it can’t cap-
ture its own.
“Plumber” : The heads of Vic. Education
Department are wasting themselves where
they are; they should be assisting Hughes
with his famous shipbuilding scheme. Five
months ago I told about the yards of red
tape with which the Department festooned
me in -my effort to collect a few shillings due
for work done to a State school building.
As I happily collected the cash, and gave
duplicate receipts—four months after the
work was done—l regarded the episode as
finished. But it wasn’t —I didn’t know the
Department. The other day I received a
note enclosing another official receipt for me
to. sign; this one was for the school com-
mittee to show that it hadn’t embezzled the
blessed 17s. 6d. As this was on a foolscap
sheet of better paper than I can afford to
buy and was'enclosed in an official envelope,
with an embossed stamp, and as another
equally chic envelope was enclosed for reply,
I signed the thing and sent it back. But I
want the Minister of Education to under-
stand that I’m tired of the foolishness. Three
receipts for 17s. 6d. is over the odds. If the
State is going to demand three receipts for
every note it pays there’ll be no ink left to
sign the lOU’s.
“Talaponsi”: Bill Massey’s Wellington
speech concerning the occupation of Samoa,
and his reminder that the Maorilanders car-
ried back in 1914 the British flag that was
taken from Samoa. in 1899, recalls an inci-
dent on the occasion of the hoisting of the
German flag at that time. The German
occupation certainly was not agreeable to
the whole of the native population; although
they enjoyed _ the gala day and helped to
make the rejoicings picturesque with dancing
and song. The flag was hoisted by the
same Dr. Solf ■ who is making such a row
about the Pacific possessions being restored
to the Hun after war. It was left
proudly floating over the green sward about
a quarter of a mile from Apia. Late in the
evening the reigning belle of Samoa, and
incidentally sister of the young native king,
cut the halliards and dragged the double
eagle along the dusty roadway to the
court-house in the main street of Apia. Here
she did the sword-dance on the bedraggled
ensign and left it in a mud-puddle, to be dis-
covered by its infuriated adorers. The
princess suffered exile for the act, and many
of her father’s tribe along with her. But
the Germans were diplomatic enough to make
little fuss over the matter.
Died recently, at an advanced age, the
widow of Nicholas Chevalier, an artist well
known in Victoria in the ’fifties and ’sixties.
In the middle of the ’fifties he came to Mel-
bourne on a mission for his father, and was
about to return to his studies at Rome when
the projectors of Melbourne Punch in-
duced him to remain. That settled him in
Melbourne for several years; but in 1866
his art took him to Maoriland, which he ex-
plored, always travelling with packhorses
and camping out. The result was several
hundred water-color sketches, which, after
being shown in Australia, were sent to Paris
Exhibition and then to London. Chevalier
was one of the promoters of the first National
picture galleries in Australia. Re gained the
prize (£200) given by the Victorian Gov-
ernment for a picture which formed the
nucleus of the Melbourne Gallery. From
the commencement of the Art Gallery in
Sydney he acted as honorary purchaser. In
1569, while preparing to return to England,
he accepted an invitation from the Duke of
Edinburgh at Melbourne to accompany him
in the Galatea ; he left the vessel at Colombo
and went to London, reaching there in 1870,
and settled at Porchester Terrace, where he
executed many commissions for the Duke of
Edinburgh. For the Queen he painted
“Thanksgiving Day at St. Paul’s.” The
wife was remarkable in her own way; she
accompanied her husband in many of his ex-
peditions, and to the end had a" warm love
for Australia.

I Think Your Remedy a Fine One,
writes MISS MURIEL STARR.
This Brilliant Actress says she will never again travel
without a bottle of Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery.
Miss Muriel Starr, who has had a
wonderful career of star roles in America,
won a place for herself in the hearts of
all Australians during her previous visit
here. This talented and charming lady
is again delighting Australian audiences
by her powerful acting.
Miss Starr writes to the Sheldon Drug
Company, Ltd., as follows —
Dear Sirs, —I have been using; your
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery for the
past week, and although I have never
given a testimonial for anything
before in all my life, I feel that it
is my duty now to write and tell you
what a splendid remedy I think Dr.
Sheldon’s New Discovery is.
I had such a nasty little cold, and
it bothered me so much and affected
my voice when speaking. I never
was so annoyed before, and I could
not seem to get rid of it—sometimes
it was a little better and then much
worse. At last I was told to try Dr.
Sheldon’s New Discovery, and 1 did,
and I think it fine. The very first
dose eased my throat and helped my
voice, and now every trace of the
cold is gone. There was no getting
better, and then worse again, with
that cold after starting with New
Discovery—l simply improved right
along, and I want to tell you that. I
think your remedy a fine one, and I
intend never to travel again without
a bottle in my grip.
Wishing New Discovery every suc-
cess,
I am, sincerely yours,
(Sgd.) MURIEL STARR.

The Ruby in the Well.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
wrinkled carline sat and plied
Her distaff by the water’s brink ;
The king's son lighted at her side
And led his weary horse to drink.
But the tall roan did sweat and start
And twitch the bridle in his hand:
“Now, art thou faint or stout of heart,
Thou rider from a far-off land?
“Thy steed will venture not the well
Which runneth ghastly deep and cold;
But thou, and this is sooth I tell,
Mayst win therein more wealth than gold
“Lo, I have dropped my magic ring”—
ller knotted fingers clawed his arm
And bent him to that silent spring;
A red star drew him as a charm.
“Look down and see the ruby shine!
If thou canst catch it where it gleams
Straightwav this guerdon shall be thine,
The fairest damsel of thy dreams!”
His suit of mail be laid aside
By lance and helm and wrought surcoat:
llis goodly limbs with gentle pride
The aspen-checkered sunbeams smote.
He plunged, all hot for his reward;
The icy waters burned like flame,
Strange anguish pierced him like a sword,
And never near the ring he came.
Among the efts and slimy stones
No warm red jewel met his ken,
Only the horrid white of bones
And eyeless skulls of drowned men.
Undaunted rose he to the air
And rested on the daisied shore:
“Old mother, prithee point me where
The magic ruby lies, once more.”
“There, fool!” her laugh a raven’s scream.
“ ’Tis well within thy reach, I wis!”
From the still deeps the rosy gleam
Looked like a mouth held up to kiss.
He knew not if he waked or slept,
Certes, the water showed a maid
Most beautiful, who nearer crept
With weeping eyes and lifted blade:
But litliely swinging on his heel
He seized the hag in act to strike.
And laughing at the murderous steel
He kissed her lips full lover-like.
“Wise lord, the spell is broke at last!”
And in his arms, right sweet to see,
A dream-fair damsel he held fast,
As white and smooth as ivory.
He kissed her that she might not stand
Without his hold, or might not speak.
But sighed, and raised a snowdrop hand
And softly stroked his brow and cheek.
He kissed the tears from her bright eyes
As one who drinks for heavenly drouth.
“Now have I won a magic prize,
The ruby of thy lovesome mouth !”
The good roan bore them both away.
Whitethorn made bridal all the glen ;
The silver water shimmered gay
Over the bones of witless men.
VICTORIA,
ELSIE COLE.
Nemesis.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f HE fierce heat beat down on the white
roofs of the scattered houses and
on the huts of the natives on the
island. In the ti-trees near the beach a
few birds twittered now and again, then
sank to rest until the cool of the evening.
Like great crimson stars, the hibiscus
flowers alone seemed unaffected by the. in-
tense heat. Even the flaring poinciana
drooped in protest against a too-ardent sun.
In the distance the sea gleamed and sparkled
like a million gems; the white fishing skiffs
lying at anchor appeared like huge white
birds.
In the trader’s house upon the hill Jim
Carter turned uneasily in his afternoon
siesta. Like a great brown young god he
lay upon his sofa, one arm thrown back
supporting his head; his shirt open at the
throat; the whole body suggesting youth
and strength.
The door of the room gently opened, and
a face peeped in. Seeing the man asleep
a virl glided over to the sofa with a move-
ment almost snake-like in its grace and
silence. A half-caste islander, the daughter
of Carter’s housekeeper, she possessed that
indefinable grace and beauty seen only in
such wild creatures. Her hair hung down
her back in two long plaits, and pinned over
each ear was a frangipani bloom, no more
beautiful than her own flower-like face. Her
native costume of many colors lent charm to
her.
She sat down on the edge < f the sofa
and intently watched the man till, uncon-
sciously. she began to chant a native melody.
Line after line she crooned, her body sway-
ing backwards and forwards, each time bend-
ing lower until at last her face was close to
the sleeping man’s.
Half sobbing, half laughing, she bent still
closer, and, light as the touch of a butterfly,
touched his lips with her own warm red
ones.
Roused by her boldness she started back
with a little gasp as the man’s eyes flew
open.
“Tamerini!” he exclaimed. “What are you
doing here?”
“Ah, Meester Cartier,” she cried plain-
tively, “you seem so hot, I come and fan
you, see?”
With the cunning of her race, she had the
lie ready, and, picking up a fan, she sat and
commenced to wave it to and fro.
Somewhat mystified the man accepted the
explanation.
Tamerini, sitting on th floor and idly
fluttering her fan, gazed up into his face with
the artlessness of a child.
“Do you know, Tamerini,” ho said, bend-
ing down towards her, “this is the first time
I have ever had a proper look at you—
where does your mother keep you locked
up?”
The girl showed her white teeth in a
smile, but made no answer.
“Do you know you are very pretty?”
Carter asked, as he bent towards her.
The big brown eyes below gazed up into
his with the look of a faithful dog, and again
the ripe lips parted in a smile.
“Ah !” she breathed softly.
“And do you know, Tamerini,” he con-
tinued, taking one of her brown hands, “just
now I dreamt you kissed me. Perhaps it
was true!”
“Ah!” she breathed again, sharply.
“Anyhow, you’re such a bewitching little
beggar that I wouldn’t mind if you did it
again now—eh, Tamerini?”
“Ah, Meester Cartier,” she whispered as
he placed his arms around her shoulders,
and drew the head back on the sofa. The
warm red lips were raised to his, her arms
flew up and clasped his neck.
* * * *
That evening, at dusk, Tamerini lay
crouched among the shadows of the big poin-
ciana trees in the garden. At the foot of
the hill the sea muttered and growled
angrily, and on some of the white-winged
skiffs lights were burning.
Tamerini bit savagely at her little hands
and rocked herself from side to side. What
was this she had heard a while ago—heard
casually from some of the divers down on
the beach : that Carter was leaving in the
morning, going to Melbourne, for who knew
how long? Of course he would come back —
but how? Supposing—the girl shuddered in
the darkness —he should bring a white, clear-
skinned woman!
The girl sprang to her feet.
“No! no!” she whispered, passionately.
“He is mine—Jimmee!”
And Tamerini crept from the shade of the
trees.
Trembling with fear she stole into the
house and paused at the door of Carter’s
room. Her feet and hands were icy cold,
her heart thumping as she noiselessly opened
the door. His breathing came slowly and
evenly—ho was fast asleep. With a little
sob she kicked off her sandals.
“Jimmee!” she whispered breathlessly, as
h.er arms stole round his neck. “Oh,
Jimmee.”
“Great God!” The man sprang up
startled out of all sleep by the girl’s pre-
sence.
“Tamerini!” he cried in amazement. But
her arms were again around his neck, her
fluttering lips pressed to his.
“Oh, Jimmee, Jimmee!” she whispered 1
passionately. “You go away so soon. Tel!
me you come back soon —come back to
Tamerini.”
The dawn had spread across the sky and
in the east the first pink rays of the sir
appeared when Tamerini crept away. H<
heart was beating its pean of gladnes
Carter had promised, madly, to return —
her!
James Harrington Carter was in a fi
In fact, .Tames Harrington Carter wasfighi
ing the fight of his life, for the mpst unex- 1
pected of all tilings had happened—he was
in love.
They had both boarded the boat at Mel-
bourne, and for the first day or two Carter
had hardly noticed her. A plain, intelligent
face, hair brushed back, a pair of clear,
steady grey eyes and a winning smile. After
making her acquaintance it did not take
Carter long to learn that she was one of
those rarely-found women who measure nig
in all things. Essentially good in the truest
sense of the word, yet large in her views,
charitable towards all, she made a striking
contrast to everything which had hitherto
taken part in the man’s life, and it was
this which captured him. And he wanted
her. wanted her more than he had ever
wanted anything in his life.
The boat was nearing Cairns, where she
was to disembark ; but Carter’s idea was to
marry her and take her on to the Island
with him. And always, just when the words
were on his lips, just as he was about to
ask the vital question, Tamerini’s face ap-
peared before him, glowing, ardent, bewitch-
ing—and he had promised to return ! Some
finer instinct in him bade him keep true to
the girl who had so passionately declared
her love for him, even if it were only for a
little while, until he could explain every-
thing to her. But then there was Laura !
He wanted to take her on with him—now.
Either he must tell Laura and risk losing
her, or—he cursed angrily and threw away
the butt of his cigar.
“Damn it all!” he muttered fiercely, “why
should I bother about her like that —she’s
only a native girl, and surely to God noth-
ing could have happened ! A year is a long
time —perhaps she has forgotten.
But, asrain, suppose something had hap-
pened. Suppose he took Laura and Tame-
rini made a scene on arrival, and he had to
tell the whole story to his wife. Why had
he made that mad promise!
By the time the boat reached Cairns
Carter had come to a conclusion. He told
Laura Frazer everything, and then asked her
if she would marry him. The girl’s eyes
held a hurt, frightened look as she sent him
back to his Island love. Tamerini, she
declared, had a prior claim and he must
abide by his promise. Thus they parted.
The man was glad when the boat moved
again on its way. For him the door of hap-
piness was closed.
* * # *
The sea glistened and sparkled in the sun-
shine. The crowd of whites and natives
talked and jostled on the wharf. Away up
on the hill the trader’s house stood like a
sentinel.
Carter was in a black mood. Since leav-
ing Cairns he had cursed his luck, cursed
the hour he ever set eyes pn Tamerini, cursed
his own folly.
Slowly the boat churned and churned
away until the wharf was quite distinct-
till. one by one, he made out the forms mov-
ing about on the wharf. He saw the natives,
the few white men on the Island—among
them, Martin, his partner—and standing out
clear from them all. her long b’ack plaits
hanging, a flaring red hibiscus flower over

Tamerini was not alone. In her arms she

R.I.G.
The Son of Belial.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I AM one of the most careful navigators
that ever left one port to try to find
another. I seldom receive the sack for
merely getting wrecked. It is usually tor
something else as well. I am remarkably
unfortunate—*-more unfortunate, one would
imagine, than a man so intelligent deserves
to be. I think that I must have been born
at the wrong moment. It is extremely irri-
tating.
My worst misfortunes have resulted from
following the advice of people who had a
convincing way of not telling the truth.
This was the case, for instance, when the
steamer Kount of -Katoomba foundered. We
struck a rock that was too near the surface
of the water. I had gone out of my usual
course to oblige a passenger, who explained
that he grew walking-sticks for a living on
an immense island with a private wharf
from which fishing had been constantly pro-
hibited. Before we said good-night to each
other he showed me some post-cards of the
principal wharves and volcanoes, and gave
me a very nice cigar. I bought the island
from him. One afternoon when we hap-
pened to be passing near it he said he
wished to land there. As I needed several
pieces of pumice-stone for toilet purposes,
and some fish. I did not make any objection,
and we headed for Cockroach Island. But
just as I was getting ready to lasso a pile
on the private wharf from which fishing had
been constantly prohibited, nearly all the
bottom of the Kount of Katoomba suddenly
scraped off on the rock that was too near the
surface of the water. I knew that the ship
was doomed, and soon afterwards she sank
beneath my feet. The water, however, was
quite warm, and I was only bitten slightly
b.v one fish.
As we were loaded with Christmas pre-
sents for the heathen this wreck caused an
uproar of disappointment in irreligious
circles. I was blamed, and the owners (the
Methylated Wetliodist Mission) urged me to
find another vacancy at my earliest conveni-
ence. It was not at all convenient, but mv
plausibility is such that ultimately I suc-
ceeded. My next command was a vessel
called the Maiden’s Prayer, and my com-
mand with her would in all likelihood have
terminated similarly only for the fact that
she blew up.
No one was more relieved to be rid of her
than I was. The masts rattled when one
brushed against them, and her cockroach-
excluders were a signal failure.
She blew up because one of the engineers
turned the wrong tap on. But it did not in
the least surprise me, nevertheless, to hear
that I was considered chieflv to blame in the
matter. The Court of Inquiry found me
guilty of wilful neglect in allowing an un-
skilled mechanic to tamper witli the ship's
machinery. Somehow or other they managed
to find out that the engineer who turned the
wrong tap on was employed previously in
an umbrella hospital, where lie had studied
his profession insufficiently in the dinner-
hour. How was I to know that? It seems
to me that every second person one meets
nowadays is a liar.
Sometimes I feel that I would be much
happier if I wasn’t a ship’s captain at all.
Lots of people have advised me to give it
up altogether, and mind fowls instead, or
lead a life somewhat similar.
It annoyed me very much indeed when,
one morning about a quarter to nine, the
Son of Belial also sank. For one thing I
had the most comfortable bunk on board, and
for another I forgot my pet tarantula and
cigarette-card album in the turmoil of an
unpremeditated departure. My album con-
tained many interesting and curious cigar-
ette cards, some of which were issued with
brands of cigarettes not now procurable, and
my pet tarantula and I had many things in
common. Neither of us, for instance, could
swim, and I have always been noted for my
cunning wheil dealing with flies. There are
also times wlien I seem to be walking across
the ceiling. But I am chiefly grieved at the
loss of my cigarette-card album. I had in
it most of the English criminals who up to
iliat time had failed to elude capture, the
• hief waterfalls of Assyria, and a collection
uf unknown birds and animals which for
magnitude was said to be unrivalled. There
were also some foreign cigarette-cards for
the issue of which the distributors have since
been prosecuted.
The Son of Belial was an experiment.
She was built on the eight-day principle. It
was quite a magnificent idea. Instead of the
ordinary kind of machinery she had
works exactly like the apparatus of an
eight-day tliree-and-sixpenny clock. The
experiment, however, was a failure. In con-
nection with it many people spoke of me
with disrespect. This was unjust.
Lot us consider, slowly and in an unbiased
manner the facts of the case.
The Son of Belial was a tin-screw alumin-
ium steamer of 909 tons, lined throughout
with reinvigorated cardboard. She had
accommodation for a great deal of cargo ana
seven passengers. Very little wood was used
in her construction, because it was found
that reinvigorated cardboard was cheaper
and could be painted to resemble imitation
oak. Exteriorly she was painted hippopota-
mus color and patterned to resemble the skin
of an alligator. Her works, as the wholly
intelligent will already have divined, were
not the usual clink-clank-clangety-clunk
kind. They were on the tick-tock-tack prin-
ciple. The owners decided that as no fuel
would be needed neither would a funnel.
They thought that it would be nice to save
the expense, but I made a fuss. 1 detest a
steamer that hasn't got a funnel, so I had
one built out of my own banking account.
The mention of my banking account reminds
me, by the way, to comment on the neces-
sity for having a sinking fund. I never
travel without one.
Toy steamboats that you wind up for
voyages in the washhouse are much the very
same in appearance as the Son of Belial
In fact, I have often been struck with the
resemblance. The only real difference, so
far as I can see, is that toy steamboats are
much smaller, sink oftener and for no
reason at all when you put them in the
bath, and are seldom painted hippopotamus
color or patterned to resemble the skin of
an alligator. The Son of Belial only sank
once, and that was through an oversight.
Her clockwork speed was fourteen acres per
pound.
We left the Gulf of Carpentaria for Ant-
arctica with seventy-seven passengers and a
cargo of Polar bears, which it was thought
would very likely thrive there. We left on
Grandfather’s Day of that year. I forget
what year it was, but I remember very well
that most Gulf of Carpentarians were wear-
ing in their buttonholes the prawn-colored
marguerite which symbolised in that locality
a carefully-veiled disinterest in grandfath-
ers. I have a distinct recollection that on
the afternoon of our departure the tempera-
ture was 188.7 in bed.
At first, I must confess, I was charmed
with the innumerable constructional novel-
ties possessed by the Son of Belial, and won-
dered why they had never been thought of
before. I soon discovered, however, that
clockwork has several disadvantages. For
one thing, the tick-toclc-tacking of it is irri-
tating, especially when one wishes to sleep
On a moderately calm day the inhabitants of
any port we happened to be trying tiO find
were always aware of our approach the
afternoon before we were expected to arrive.
In the remoter and more savage islands this
was an inconvenience. We were regarded as
a nuisance and treated with contumely.
Winding up the clockwork every eighth day
at twenty-two minutes to three by means of
windmills was seldom simple, especially when
there wasn’t any wind and the winding had
to be done by hand. There were also times
when the supply of wind was found to greatly
exceed the demand, and a most sanguineous
likelihood that the Son of Belial’s mainspring
might overwind would then ensue.
Yet the failure of this experiment was not
due to the constructional differences which
distinguished the Son of Belial from other
vessels that previously had been or subse-
quently have been built, nor was it due to
any lack of observation on my part. The ex-
periment was a failure merely because it did
not. have time to succeed properly.
One morning before breakfast the last per-
son to have a bath in the Son of Belial’s
basement left the ship’s plug out. She slowly
filled with salt water and kept on sinking
until'she reached the bottom of the ocean. I
then got out of bed, and after entering the
occurrence in the ship’s log I put on a mac-
intosh and floated slowly to the surface. I
felt that what I needed most was a large
gulp of dry air.
People were a little annoyed, I think, be-
cause there were so few survivors and I hap-
pened to be among them. Those who were
rescued said that I was always the last per-
son to utilise the ship’s bath, and that there-
fore the ship’s plug must have been left out by
me. But I deny it. I remember nuite distinctly
that on the morning when the disaster occur-
red I did not have a bath. I was busy gum-
ming some rare cigarette-cards into my
album, and had issued instructions that I
was not on any account to be disturbed. I
hate being disturbed.
In connection with the loss of the Son of
Belial I was treated with a much greater
amount of disrespect and suspicion than ever.
My certificate was marked “Issued by mis-
take.” and the street collection society of
which, for years, I had been an honorary
receiver, wrote me an insulting letter inti-
mating that owing to circumstances over
which the shareholders did not exercise con-
trol, I was not invited to the next two
annual picnics. I think that this was unjust.
LES ROBINSON.
Gipsy-joy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LIGHTS are flashing through the wood-
j land trees.
■ Spangles glance
To a brown foot's dance.
Zithers breaking on the lilting breeze
Banish sorrow with their melodies.
Who’ll come roving to a Romany rhyme
Hand in hand
With a gipsy band,
Clicking fingers to the chestnuts’ chime.
Finding Fortune in the front of Time?
Trinkets tinkle to a restless tune.
File the load
For the open road !
Careless kettles ’neath a vagrant moon
Sing of horses to be jogging soon.
Who’ll leave fetters for the wild, free dir,
Strolling far
By a vagabond star?
Outlaw Laughter is the only care
Gipsies carry with the gold they share.
Join in, traveller, with the jingling dance.
Fairy Fate
Is a merry mate.
Luck is lurking in the cards’ romance.
Come and shuffle them and take your chance.
N.S.W.

If Engineers and Steam-plant Owners would only
realise the importance of EFFICIENTLY insulating
their Boilers, Steam Pipes, etc., more attention might
be paid to this particular matter. Great economies
can he effected in this direction, in some cases the coal
consumption being reduced as much as 50%. But the
insulating material must be of the very best.
Carbonate of Magnesia is to-day recognised as the best
non-conductor of heat. Newali s Coverings are com-
posed of 85% of this material, and 15% Asbestos—the
Asbestos being used for binding purposes only—and in
addition possess innumerable advantages over all other
forms of lagging. Newall’s Coverings are used, practi-
cally exclusively, by the British Admiralty. N.R.W.
and Victorian .Governments, and have been used
throughout the UPimo. White Bay, Melbourne, Perth
and Canberra Power Stations.

3?. D. MoM ASTER, Esq., Dalkeith, Cassilis, writes, under date 24th January:
“I have pleasure in stating: that I have given both of the 2 Stand Portable
Crutching Plants I purchased from you an exhaustive trial, and with the
utmost satisfaction. They are handy to move about, economical to run, and

considerable.”
